The Third Doubt: Divine Knowledge of Particulars
Third doubt on divine knowledge of particulars with a dilemma argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt against divine knowledge of particulars and why it poses a challenge to the doctrine of God's immutability.

Core Idea
If God knows changeable things, doesn't His knowledge have to change too?

The third doubt presents a powerful dilemma: either God knows particulars or He does not. If He does, His knowledge would change with the changing objects, implying change in the divine essence — which contradicts His necessity. If He does not, then He is ignorant. The passage presents the first horn and begins the second, but the resolution is cut off. Here we explore the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the dilemma structure: two alternatives, with the first argued in detail.
The passage is incomplete; the second alternative is introduced but not fully argued.
2
Identify the first horn's reasoning
Trace the logic: if God knows particulars, His knowledge must conform to them. Since particulars change, His knowledge would change, implying change in His essence. A changing essence cannot be necessary; it would be originated.
Key phrase: 'لأن محل الحوادث حادث' — the locus of originated events is originated.
3
Recognize the incompleteness
The passage states 'The second is false, for we know that...' and cuts off. Reflect on what the expected answer might be: that God knows particulars without change, perhaps because His knowledge is eternal and encompasses all temporal states without alteration.
The full argument requires continuation; this lesson focuses on the doubt itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة ( منها ( لو كان ( الواجب ( موجوداً لكان إما عالماً بالجزئيات أولا والأول باطل وإلا لزم التغير فيه ( أي في ذات الواجب تعالى ( لتغير المعلوم ) الجزئي من حال إلى حال. فإن زيدا مثلا يتصف تارة بالقيام وأخرى بعدمه، والعلم لا بد فيه من أن يطابق معلومه فيتغير أيضاً بحسبه ( فلا يكون ) الواجب على هذا التقدير ( واجبا ) بل حادثا لأن محل الحوادث حادث ) والثاني باطل لأنا نعلم )
The third of them: If the Necessary were existent, He would be either knowing of particulars or not. The first is false, for otherwise change would be necessary in Him – i.e., in the essence of the Most High – due to the change of the particular object of knowledge from one state to another. For Zayd, for example, is sometimes described as standing and other times as not. Knowledge must conform to its object, so it would also change accordingly. So the Necessary, on this assumption, would not be necessary but rather originated, because the locus of originated events is originated. The second is false, for we know that...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is the third doubt concerning God's knowledge of particulars. The dilemma: either God knows particulars or He does not. If He does, then because particulars change, His knowledge must change, implying change (ḥudūth) in His essence – making Him originated. If He does not, then He is ignorant. The text cuts off during the proof of the second alternative, but the expected answer would likely affirm that God knows particulars without change, perhaps by arguing that His knowledge is eternal and does not change with the object's changes (a standard Ashʿarī position). The passage as provided is incomplete, but it presents the doubt clearly.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised in the third doubt regarding God's knowledge of particulars?
A) That God cannot know particulars because they are infinite.
B) That knowledge of changeable particulars would require change in God.
C) That God's knowledge is not conformable to objects.
D) That particulars are not known by God.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt argues that if God knows particulars, His knowledge would change, implying change in God — a serious challenge to divine immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every action is eternal and unchanging, does that make your choices seem predetermined? How does your understanding of God's timeless knowledge affect your sense of personal responsibility? Commit to living each moment as if it is eternally known — with full accountability.
?
When you experience moments of spiritual decline, do you believe that God's view of you changes? How does the truth that God's knowledge does not change challenge your concept of repentance? Pause to consider: are you relying on God's unchanging mercy or on your own changing deeds?
?
The doubt raised here suggests that if God knows changing things, He must change. But you know your own thoughts shift constantly — how does it feel to realize that your Creator knows every fleeting thought without any change in Himself? Let this awe motivate you to watch your thoughts more carefully today.

Defending Divine Knowledge of Particulars
Al-Jurjani addresses the objection that knowledge of particulars entails change in the divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians defend divine knowledge of particulars without attributing change to God's essence, by distinguishing between real attributes and relational aspects.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not change; only its relation to changing objects changes.

The objection that knowledge of particulars would require God to change seems powerful, but it rests on a misunderstanding. In this passage, al-Jurjani explains a crucial distinction between the real attribute of knowledge and its relational ties to changing objects. Understanding this distinction is key to preserving divine transcendence while affirming God's complete knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage and identify the two horns of the dilemma: either God knows particulars (which seems to require change) or He does not (which contradicts observed order).
Pay attention to the example of Zayd standing.
2
Examine the refutation of both horns
Notice why the first horn is false (change in God) and the second horn is false (intuition of design).
3
Grasp the answer: change in relations only
Understand the key distinction: the real attribute of knowledge is one and unchanging; what changes is the relation (idāfah) to the particular object. This preserves God's transcendence.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the author's advice
Al-Jurjani advises to master this principle and accumulate proofs. Consider how this distinction resolves similar objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة ( منها ( لو كان ( الواجب ( موجوداً لكان إما عالماً بالجزئيات أولا والأول باطل وإلا لزم التغير فيه ( أي في ذات الواجب تعالى ( لتغير المعلوم ) الجزئي من حال إلى حال. فإن زيدا مثلا يتصف تارة بالقيام وأخرى بعدمه، والعلم لا بد فيه من أن يطابق معلومه فيتغير أيضاً بحسبه ( فلا يكون ) الواجب على هذا التقدير ( واجبا ) بل حادثا لأن محل الحوادث حادث ) والثاني باطل لأنا نعلم بالبديهة (أن هذه الأفعال المتقنة المشاهدة في الجزئيات ( لا تستند إلى عديم العلم، والجواب نختار أنه عالم بالجزئيات والتغير اللازم في العلم إنما هو ( في الإضافات لا في الذات ) أي لا في صفاته الحقيقة فإن علمه تعالى صفة واحدة حقيقية قائمة بذاته ومتعلقة بالمعلومات كلها فإذا تغيرت لم تتغير تلك الصفة بل تغيرت تعلقاتها به وإضافاتها إليه، فيكون تغيرا في أمور اعتبارية لا في صفات حقيقية، ( وإنه جائز) في الواجب ( كما سيأتي ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن هذا منشأ للشبهات التي طول بها الكتب وعد ذلك التطويل ) تبحرا في العلوم وتوسعاً في التحقيق والتدقيق ) وعليك بعد الإهتداء إليه ) بما نبهناك به من الضابطة والأمثلة ( أن توفر من الأباعر ) جمع بعير
The third of them: If the Necessary existed, it would be either knowing of particulars or not. The first is false because otherwise change would occur in it (i.e., in the essence of the Necessary, may He be exalted) due to the change of the known particular from state to state. For example, Zayd is sometimes described as standing and sometimes as not, and knowledge must correspond to its known object, so it would also change accordingly. Thus the Necessary on this assumption would not be Necessary but contingent, because the locus of contingencies is contingent. The second is false because we know by intuition that these precise observed acts in particulars do not originate from one lacking knowledge. The answer: we choose that He is knowing of particulars, and the necessary change in knowledge is only in relations, not in the essence—i.e., not in His real attributes. For His knowledge, exalted is He, is one real attribute subsisting in His essence and related to all known things. When they change, that attribute does not change; rather, its relations and additions to them change. So it is a change in mental considerations, not in real attributes. And it is permissible in the Necessary, as will come. And we will restrict ourselves to this extent, for this is the source of the sophisms with which books have been lengthened, and that lengthening is considered a deep investigation in the sciences and an expansion in verification and scrutiny. And after being guided to it—by what we have alerted you to of the principle and examples—you should accumulate from the 'abā'ir (plural of ba'īr).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objection runs: if the Necessary Being knows particulars, then since particulars change, His knowledge would have to change to correspond, implying change in the divine essence, which is impossible for a Necessary Being. If He does not know particulars, then the precise acts observed in the world cannot come from an ignorant agent—so that too is impossible. The answer is that the Necessary Being does know particulars, but the change is only in the relations (idāfāt) of His knowledge to the changing objects, not in the real attribute of knowledge itself. His knowledge is one eternal attribute that relates to all known things; when the known changes, the relation changes, but the attribute remains unchanged. This change in relations is permissible for the Necessary Being. The author then advises the reader to focus on this principle and accumulate proofs (metaphorically 'stack camels'? or 'provide proofs from the 'abā'ir' meaning other sources). The key nuance is distinguishing between the real attribute and its relational aspects.

Key Terms
علم
جزئيات
إضافة
ذات
صفة حقيقية
تعلق
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the answer admits change in God's essence or real attributes.
Correction: The answer distinguishes between change in real attributes (impossible) and change in relational aspects (permissible). Only the relations of the one knowledge to changing objects change, not the knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what changes when a particular object changes?
A) The eternal attribute of knowledge itself
B) The relation of the knowledge to the object
C) The essence of the Knower
D) The act of knowing

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of particulars does not imply change in His essence because the change is only in the relations of the one eternal knowledge to changing objects, not in the knowledge attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a past sin you committed, do you truly believe that God's knowledge of you at that moment was the same as His knowledge of you now—unchanged? How does that unchanging view of you shape your desire to turn back to Him today?
?
The objection assumes that if God's knowledge changes with our changing states, then He would be imperfect. Now think about your own knowledge of God: does it change with your moods? When you feel distant, do you act as if God's attributes have changed? What step will you take today to anchor your perception of God in His unchangeable reality?
?
If God's eternal knowledge of your future failures and successes is equally certain and unchanging, does that frighten you or free you? How can you actively rely on His unchangeable knowledge to make one specific decision today with confidence?

God's Knowledge of Changing Particulars
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that God lacks knowledge of changing particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's unchanging knowledge can encompass changing particulars without implying change in God.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one unchanging attribute; change is in relations, not essence.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a key objection to divine omniscience: if God knows particulars that change, His knowledge would seem to change. He resolves this by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its relations. This distinction is crucial for preserving God's immutability while affirming His comprehensive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Locate the objection (that precise actions cannot come from one lacking knowledge) and the answer (God knows particulars, change is in relations).
3
Understand the distinction
Focus on the key distinction between the essence of knowledge (an unchanging real attribute) and its relations (iḍāfāt) to objects.
The Arabic terms إضافات and أمور اعتبارية are central.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this answer preserves God's immutability: change is only in conceptual matters, not in real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول : المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع . أمثاله بالبديهة أن هذه الأفعال المتقنة المشاهدة في الجزئيات لا تستند إلى عديم العلم، والجواب نختار أنه عالم بالجزئيات والتغير اللازم في العلم إنما هو في الإضافات لا في الذات، أي لا في صفاته الحقيقة فإن علمه تعالى صفة واحدة حقيقية قائمة بذاته ومتعلقة بالمعلومات كلها فإذا تغيرت لم تتغير تلك الصفة بل تغيرت تعلقاته بها وإضافاته إليها، فيكون تغيرا في أمور اعتبارية لا في صفات حقيقية.
The First Observatory: The First Objective: Concerning Establishing the Maker. Its examples by intuition: that these precise actions observed in particulars are not attributed to one lacking knowledge. The answer: we choose that He is knower of particulars, and the change necessitated in knowledge is only in the relations, not in the essence—i.e., not in His real attributes. For His knowledge, exalted is He, is one real attribute subsisting in His essence and related to all known things. So when they change, that attribute does not change; rather, its relations and additions to them change. Thus it is a change in conceptual matters, not in real attributes.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is: if God knows changing particulars, His knowledge would change, which is impossible for an immutable being. The answer uses the distinction between the essence of knowledge (which is a single unchanging attribute) and its relations to objects. The attribute itself does not change; only the relations (iḍāfāt) change when the objects change. Thus, there is no change in God's real attributes, only in conceptual matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya). The commentator (al-Jurjani) is explaining how God can know changing things without change in His essence.

Key Terms
عديم العلم
علم حقيقي
إضافات
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge changes when its objects change.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the knowledge itself is unchanging; only the relational aspects (iḍāfāt) change, which are conceptual matters (umūr iʿtibāriyya), not real attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God's knowledge not change when particulars change?
A) Because God does not know particulars.
B) Because knowledge is not an attribute of God.
C) Because the change is in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the essence of knowledge.
D) Because God only knows universals.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is an unchanging attribute; change occurs only in its relations to changing objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God’s knowledge of every detail of your life is unchanging—your past, present, and future are all equally known to Him. Does this truth make you feel exposed or secure? Will you live today with the awareness that you cannot hide any part of yourself from Him?
?
If change in God’s knowledge is only conceptual, not real, then His attribute of knowledge never adapts to you—you adapt to His eternal knowledge. Are you trying to fit God into your changing plans, or are you aligning your changing self with His unchanging will?
?
The objection assumed that knowledge of particulars would force God to change. But the answer shows that His relationship to you changes as you change, yet He remains perfect. Will you stop projecting human limitations onto Him and start trusting that His unchanging knowledge is exactly what you need for your changing life?

Essence Similar in Essentiality, Distinguished by Four States
Presents the claim of conceptual similarity and four distinguishing states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that early theologians held God's essence to be conceptually similar to other essences but uniquely distinguished by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power.

Core Idea
God's essence is like others in concept, but unique in four states.

Before examining the divine attributes in detail, the early theologians first addressed how God's essence relates to other essences. Their position was that there is a conceptual commonality, but God is distinguished by four specific states. This lesson presents that foundational view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the main claim.
Notice the four states are listed in parentheses.
2
Identify the four states
Find the four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, perfect power. Understand that these are the distinguishing attributes of God.
The states are also described as complete wajibiyyah, hayiyyah, alimiyyah, qadiriyyah.
3
Understand similarity in essentiality
The passage states that God's essence is similar to other essences in essentiality and reality. Note that this similarity is only conceptual, not actual.
The commentary clarifies that 'essence' is a common concept applied to all beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قدماء المتكلمين ذاته تعالى مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة ( وإنما نمتاز عن سائر الذوات بأحوال أربعة الوجوب والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة التامة ) أي الواجبية والحيية والعالمية والقادرية التمامتين . قوله : ( مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة ومبنى كلامهم على أن مفهوم الذات تمام حقيقة ما تحته كما سبق وإلا فمشاركة ذاته تعالى لسائر الذوات بمعنى أن مفهوم الذات أعني ما يقوم بنفسه أو ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه صادق على الكل صدق العارض على المعروض مما لا نزاع فيه . قوله : ( والحياة والعلم التام إلخ ) إنما لم يقيد الحياة بشيء بمتاز به عن حياتنا كما قيد العلم والقدرة، لأن حياته تعالى خالفة بالنوع لحياتنا على ما سيجيء في رابع مقاصد المرصد الرابع فلا حاجة إلى التقييد بخلاف العلم والقدرة هكذا قيل وفيه أنهم صرحوا بأن علمه تعالى وكذا قدرته ليسا بعرض بخلاف علمنا وقدرتنا فكيف المماثلة ويمكن أن يقال إنما لم يقيد الحياة، لأن حياته تعالى عند غير الفلاسفة وأبي الحسين صفة توجب صحة العلم والقدرة كما سيجيء، والظاهر أن مراد مثبتي الأحوال بهما العالمية والقادرية اللتان جعلوهما مميزتين له تعالى فتقييدهما بالتامة تقييد للحياة في المآل والله أعلم .
And the early theologians said: His essence, exalted, is similar to all other essences in essentiality and reality, and it is distinguished from other essences only by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power, meaning the complete wajibiyyah, hayiyyah, alimiyyah, and qadiriyyah. His saying: 'similar to all other essences in essentiality and reality' — the basis of their statement is that the concept of essence (dhat) is the complete reality of what is under it, as previously. Otherwise, the participation of His essence, exalted, with other essences in the sense that the concept of essence — meaning that which subsists by itself or that which is correct to be known and informed about — is true of all, as an accident is true of a subject, is a matter of no dispute. His saying: 'life, perfect knowledge, etc.' — life was not restricted with something distinguishing it from our life as knowledge and power were restricted, because His life, exalted, is different in kind from our life, as will come in the fourth objectives of the fourth observation, so there is no need for restriction, unlike knowledge and power. Thus it is said, and there is in it that they explicitly stated that His knowledge, and similarly His power, are not an accident, unlike our knowledge and power, so how is the similarity? And it can be said that life was not restricted only because His life, exalted, according to others than the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn, is an attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and power, as will come. And it appears that the affirmers of states intend by them the alimiyyah and qadiriyyah which they made distinguishing for Him, exalted, so restricting them with 'perfect' is a restriction of life in the final analysis, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the early theologians (qudama' al-mutakallimin) that God's essence is similar (mumathilah) to other essences in essentiality (dhatiyyah) and reality (haqiqah) but is distinguished by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power. These states are explained as the complete wajibiyyah, hayiyyah, alimiyyah, and qadiriyyah. The commentary then clarifies the meaning of similarity in essentiality: the concept of 'essence' (ma yaghumu bi nafsihi) applies to God and other beings in the same way that an accident applies to a subject, but this is only a conceptual commonality, not a real participation. Then it explains why life was not qualified with a distinguishing term like 'perfect' was added to knowledge and power. The reason is that God's life is different in kind (mukhalifah bi al-naw') from our life, as will be discussed later, so it needs no qualification. In contrast, knowledge and power might be thought to be similar to ours, so 'perfect' is added to emphasize their distinction. The commentary also notes that some scholars argued that God's knowledge and power are not accidents (as ours are), raising a question about similarity. Finally, it suggests that by 'knowledge and power' the affirmers of states mean 'alimiyyah' and 'qadiriyyah' which are distinguishing attributes, and adding 'perfect' is ultimately a restriction on life itself.

Key Terms

أحوال أربعة
الوجوب
الحياة
العلم التام
القدرة التامة
الذاتية
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that similarity in essentiality implies that God's essence is like others in all respects or shares their contingent nature.
Correction: The similarity is only in the concept of 'essence' itself. God's essence is not like others in reality; it is distinct through the four states.

Checkpoint
According to the early theologians, what distinguishes God's essence from other essences?
A) Its essentiality and reality
B) Four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, perfect power
C) Its similarity to human essence
D) Its lack of attributes

Key Takeaway
The early theologians argued that God's essence is conceptually similar to others but uniquely distinguished by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that God's essence is like yours in concept but distinguished by wajub — absolute necessity. Does your daily life reflect a deep-seated reliance on the One who is necessary, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What is one decision today that you will consciously make while acknowledging your contingency and His necessity?
?
God's essence is uniquely characterized by perfect knowledge and power. When you sin, do you truly believe that He sees everything and is fully able to hold you accountable? What area of your life have you been hiding from His knowledge, and how will you expose it to His light today?
?
If God's essence is similar in concept but distinguished by these four states, then He is not a distant abstract concept but a Knowing, Living, Powerful, and Necessary reality. How does this truth challenge your tendency to reduce God to a mere idea or to treat Him as less than fully present? What will you do this week to actively remember His life, knowledge, and power over you?

Why Life is Not Qualified: Distinguishing Divine Attributes
Explains why life is not qualified and the reasoning behind qualifying knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why early theologians added 'perfect' to knowledge and power but not to life, and the philosophical reasoning behind this distinction.

Core Idea
God's life is different in kind from ours; knowledge and power need qualification.

In the previous lesson, we saw the four states. Now we explore a detailed commentary on why only two of them are qualified with 'perfect'. The early theologians argued that divine life is inherently different from human life, while knowledge and power could be ambiguously similar. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the part starting from 'قوله : ( والحياة والعلم التام إلخ )'. Understand the discussion about qualification.
This passage contains an objection and a possible answer.
2
Identify the reasoning for life
The passage explains that life was not qualified because God's life is different in kind (mukhalifah bi al-naw') from our life, so no need for a distinguishing qualifier.
Later in the text, it is said that life will be discussed in more detail.
3
Understand the qualification of knowledge and power
Knowledge and power are qualified as 'perfect' because they might be thought similar to human attributes, but they are not accidents like ours.
An objection is raised: if they are not accidents, how can they be similar? The answer is that the similarity is only conceptual.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The passage ends with a possible interpretation: the 'alimiyyah' and 'qadiriyyah' are what distinguish God, and adding 'perfect' ultimately qualifies life itself.
This shows the depth of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قدماء المتكلمين ذاته تعالى مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة ( وإنما نمتاز عن سائر الذوات بأحوال أربعة الوجوب والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة التامة ) أي الواجبية والحيية والعالمية والقادرية التمامتين . قوله : ( مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة ومبنى كلامهم على أن مفهوم الذات تمام حقيقة ما تحته كما سبق وإلا فمشاركة ذاته تعالى لسائر الذوات بمعنى أن مفهوم الذات أعني ما يقوم بنفسه أو ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه صادق على الكل صدق العارض على المعروض مما لا نزاع فيه . قوله : ( والحياة والعلم التام إلخ ) إنما لم يقيد الحياة بشيء بمتاز به عن حياتنا كما قيد العلم والقدرة، لأن حياته تعالى خالفة بالنوع لحياتنا على ما سيجيء في رابع مقاصد المرصد الرابع فلا حاجة إلى التقييد بخلاف العلم والقدرة هكذا قيل وفيه أنهم صرحوا بأن علمه تعالى وكذا قدرته ليسا بعرض بخلاف علمنا وقدرتنا فكيف المماثلة ويمكن أن يقال إنما لم يقيد الحياة، لأن حياته تعالى عند غير الفلاسفة وأبي الحسين صفة توجب صحة العلم والقدرة كما سيجيء، والظاهر أن مراد مثبتي الأحوال بهما العالمية والقادرية اللتان جعلوهما مميزتين له تعالى فتقييدهما بالتامة تقييد للحياة في المآل والله أعلم .
And the early theologians said: His essence, exalted, is similar to all other essences in essentiality and reality, and it is distinguished from other essences only by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power, meaning the complete wajibiyyah, hayiyyah, alimiyyah, and qadiriyyah. His saying: 'similar to all other essences in essentiality and reality' — the basis of their statement is that the concept of essence (dhat) is the complete reality of what is under it, as previously. Otherwise, the participation of His essence, exalted, with other essences in the sense that the concept of essence — meaning that which subsists by itself or that which is correct to be known and informed about — is true of all, as an accident is true of a subject, is a matter of no dispute. His saying: 'life, perfect knowledge, etc.' — life was not restricted with something distinguishing it from our life as knowledge and power were restricted, because His life, exalted, is different in kind from our life, as will come in the fourth objectives of the fourth observation, so there is no need for restriction, unlike knowledge and power. Thus it is said, and there is in it that they explicitly stated that His knowledge, and similarly His power, are not an accident, unlike our knowledge and power, so how is the similarity? And it can be said that life was not restricted only because His life, exalted, according to others than the philosophers and Abu al-Husayn, is an attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and power, as will come. And it appears that the affirmers of states intend by them the alimiyyah and qadiriyyah which they made distinguishing for Him, exalted, so restricting them with 'perfect' is a restriction of life in the final analysis, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the early theologians (qudama' al-mutakallimin) that God's essence is similar (mumathilah) to other essences in essentiality (dhatiyyah) and reality (haqiqah) but is distinguished by four states: wajub, life, perfect knowledge, and perfect power. These states are explained as the complete wajibiyyah, hayiyyah, alimiyyah, and qadiriyyah. The commentary then clarifies the meaning of similarity in essentiality: the concept of 'essence' (ma yaghumu bi nafsihi) applies to God and other beings in the same way that an accident applies to a subject, but this is only a conceptual commonality, not a real participation. Then it explains why life was not qualified with a distinguishing term like 'perfect' was added to knowledge and power. The reason is that God's life is different in kind (mukhalifah bi al-naw') from our life, as will be discussed later, so it needs no qualification. In contrast, knowledge and power might be thought to be similar to ours, so 'perfect' is added to emphasize their distinction. The commentary also notes that some scholars argued that God's knowledge and power are not accidents (as ours are), raising a question about similarity. Finally, it suggests that by 'knowledge and power' the affirmers of states mean 'alimiyyah' and 'qadiriyyah' which are distinguishing attributes, and adding 'perfect' is ultimately a restriction on life itself.

Key Terms
الحياة
العلم التام
القدرة التامة
قدماء المتكلمين
أحوال أربعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God's attributes share names with human attributes, they are similar in all respects.
Correction: The attributes differ in kind; for example, divine life is different in kind from human life, and divine knowledge and power are not accidents.

Checkpoint
Why did the early theologians not qualify life with a distinguishing term like they did for knowledge and power?
A) Because life is not an attribute
B) Because God's life is different in kind from human life
C) Because life is not one of the four states
D) Because they forgot

Key Takeaway
The early theologians qualified knowledge and power with 'perfect' to distinguish them from human accidents, but left life unqualified because it is inherently different in kind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your knowledge and power are fundamentally different from God's? In what area of your life do you most need to acknowledge that His knowledge is perfect and yours is not? What practical step can you take today to submit to His perfect knowledge over your own limited understanding?
?
The early theologians left life unqualified because it is different in kind. Are you living as if God's life is just like yours—limited by time, change, and need? How would your prayer change if you truly meditated on the fact that God's life is absolutely other, and that He is the Living (al-Hayy) who never dies?
?
When you read the phrase 'perfect knowledge' and 'perfect power' in this lesson, do you recognize that your own knowledge is not just imperfect but of a completely different order? In which area of life do you most struggle to trust that God's knowledge is perfect and yours is not? Will you commit to asking Him for guidance in that area right now?

Abu Hashim's Fifth State: Ilahiyyah
Presents Abu Hashim's fifth state and the response to the verse objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's addition of a fifth state called ilahiyyah that distinguishes God from other essences, and how the verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay' is interpreted to deny similarity only in the most specific attributes.

Core Idea
God's uniqueness is preserved by denying similarity in His most specific attributes, not in His essence.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i proposed that God is distinguished from all other essences by a fifth state (halah khamisah) called ilahiyyah, which causes the four states of necessity, life, knowledge, and power. This view appears to conflict with the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him.' However, Abu Hashim's followers argue that the verse denies similarity only in the most specific attributes, not in essence and reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: حالة خامسة, الإلهية, المماثلة المنفية.
The passage is concise.
2
Identify the fifth state
Find what Abu Hashim calls the fifth state and what it necessitates.
It is called ilahiyyah and it necessitates the other four states.
3
Understand the objection
The verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay' is presented as a potential objection. How do they respond?
They say the negation is limited to the most specific attributes.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why they distinguish between similarity in attributes vs. essence.
This preserves God's transcendence while allowing for distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا عند أبي علي الجبائي، (و) أما ( عند أبي هاشم ) فإنه ( يمتاز ) عما عداه من الذوات ) بحالة خامسة هي موجبة لهذه الأربعة نسميها بالإلهية ) قالوا : ولا يرد علينا قوله تعالى ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى : ۱۱ ] ، لأن المماثلة المنفية هاهنا هي المشاركة في أخص صفات النفس دون المشاركة في الذات والحقيقة فإن قيل: المذكور في الموقف الثاني الموجودية بدل الوجوب وهو الموافق لما في المحصل ،والأربعين أجيب بأن الوجود عند مثبتي الأحوال مشترك بين الموجودات كلها، فلا يتصور كونه مميزاً فالمراد بالموجودية المميزة هو الموجودية المقيدة بالواجبة. قوله : (ليس كمثله شيء) قيل العرب إذا أرادت المبالغة في نفي المشابهة جمعت بين حرفي التشبيه فيقول : ليس كمثله فلان أحد، وقيل الكاف صلة زيدت في الكلام للمبالغة. وقيل : المثل صلة كما قال الله تعالى : فإن آمنوا بمثل ما آمنتم به ﴾ [البقرة : ١٧ ] ، وقالوا يقال : ليس هذا كلام مثلك أي كلامك والكلام في هذه الآية مبسوط في المطلول وحواشينا عليه فلينظر فيهما . قوله: (هي المشاركة في أخص صفات النفس قد سبق في أواخر بحث الوجود أن المشاركة في الحقيقة تستلزم المشاركة في اللوازم ويشير إليه الآن أيضاً فنفي المشاركة في أخص الصفات يستلزم نفي المشاركة في الحقيقة فلا يصح قوله دون المشاركة في الذات، والحقيقة اللهم إلا أن يكون مرادهم مجرد استدلال الأصحاب بهذه الآية على نفي المشاركة في الحقيقة ويحمل كلامهم على المنع والسند فتأمل.
This is the view of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. As for Abu Hashim, he distinguishes Him from other essences by a fifth state that necessitates these four, which we call ilahiyyah. They said: The verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Quran 42:11) does not refute us, because the similarity denied here is participation in the most specific attributes of the self, not participation in essence and reality. If it is said: In the second station, existence (al-mawjudiyyah) is mentioned instead of wajub, which agrees with what is in al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in, the answer is that existence according to the affirmers of states is common among all existents, so it cannot be conceived as a distinguisher. What is meant by the distinctive existence is existence restricted by wajub. His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' — it is said: the Arabs, when they intend to exaggerate in negating similarity, combine two particles of comparison and say: 'There is no one like him.' And it is said: The kaf is an extra particle added for exaggeration. And it is said: 'Mithl' is an extra as God said: 'Then if they believe in the like of what you believe in' (Quran 2:137), and they said: It is said: 'This is not the speech of someone like you' meaning your speech. The discussion of this verse is detailed in the matlub and our glosses upon it, so let them be consulted. His saying: 'It is participation in the most specific attributes of the self' — it has preceded in the end of the discussion of existence that participation in reality necessitates participation in necessary concomitants, and he is also alluding to it now. So negating participation in the most specific attributes necessitates negating participation in reality. Thus his saying 'without participation in essence and reality' is not correct, unless their intent is merely the companions' use of this verse to negate participation in reality, and their statement is interpreted as prevention and support. So reflect.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage contrasts the views of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim. Abu Hashim distinguishes God from other essences by a fifth state (halah khamisah) called ilahiyyah, which causes the other four states (wajub, life, knowledge, power). It then addresses an objection based on the verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay'' (Quran 42:11). The response is that the similarity (mumathalah) denied in the verse is only participation in the most specific attributes (akhas sifat al-nafs), not participation in essence and reality. Then a further objection is considered: in some works (al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in) 'existence' (al-mawjudiyyah) is mentioned instead of 'wajub' as a distinguishing attribute. The answer is that existence is common among all existents, so it cannot distinguish unless it is restricted by wajub (al-mawjudiyyah al-muqayyadah bi al-wajibah). The commentary then discusses linguistic interpretations of the word 'ka' in the verse, explaining that the Arabs sometimes use it for exaggeration, and that 'mithl' can be extra. Finally, it critiques the earlier statement that the negation applies only to most specific attributes, arguing that negating participation in the most specific attributes implies negating participation in reality, so the claim that the verse does not deny participation in essence and reality is problematic. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms
الإلهية
الحالة الخامسة
المماثلة المنفية
أخص صفات النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse denies any similarity between God and creation whatsoever.
Correction: According to Abu Hashim, it only denies similarity in the most specific attributes, allowing for similarity in essence and reality (which is still transcendent).

Checkpoint
What is the fifth state according to Abu Hashim?
A) Wujub
B) Ilahiyyah
C) Qudrah
D) Hayah

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's fifth state ilahiyyah distinguishes God, and the Quranic verse only negates similarity in the most specific attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'laysa kamithlihi shay', do you ever feel a tension between God's transcendence and your desire to know Him? How does Abu Hashim's distinction between similarity in essence and similarity in attributes affect your confidence that you can know God through His attributes? Which one of God's attributes do you need to rely on more today?
?
The verse only negates similarity in the most specific attributes. Think of an attribute you consider uniquely yours—like your sense of humor or your patience. Now reflect: God's attributes are not like yours in their specific reality. Does that distance make you feel disconnected from Him, or does it protect you from forming a limited image of Him? What step will you take today to relate to God through His actual attributes as revealed, not through your own projections?
?
Abu Hashim argues that God's essence is distinguished by a 'fifth state' that causes other attributes. Examine your own knowledge of God: do you tend to focus on His essence (what He is in Himself) or His attributes (what He does and how He relates to creation)? Which focus brings you more comfort? Which challenges your faith more? How can you intentionally balance both in your worship this week?

Resolving the Objection: Existence vs. Wajub
Addresses an objection about the use of 'existence' instead of 'wajub' and provides the solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some works use 'existence' instead of 'wajub' as the distinguishing attribute, and how the response clarifies that it is existence restricted by necessity.

Core Idea
Mere existence cannot distinguish God; the distinguishing attribute is existence qualified by necessity.

In the discussion of divine attributes, some theological works like al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in use the term 'existence' (al-mawjudiyyah) instead of 'wajub' as a distinguishing state. This raises an objection: how can existence be a distinguisher when it is common to all existents? The response refines the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Note the claim that 'existence' is mentioned instead of 'wajub' in some texts.
This comes from a questioner.
2
Understand the answer
The answer explains that existence as such is common, but existence restricted by necessity (al-mawjudiyyah al-muqayyadah bi al-wajibah) is distinctive.
This clarifies the terminology.
3
Connect to the broader theory
Reflect on how this refinement preserves the uniqueness of God's existence.

Linguistic Interpretation and Critique of the Verse
Provides linguistic analysis of the verse and critiques the limited interpretation of similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic nuances of 'laysa kamithlihi shay' and the critique that negating similarity in the most specific attributes implies negating similarity in essence.

Core Idea
The linguistic devices of the verse support an all-encompassing negation, undermining the limited interpretation.

The commentary explores three linguistic explanations for the double comparative in the verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay', arguing that it reinforces the negation. It then critiques the earlier claim that the verse only denies similarity in the most specific attributes, showing that such a negation necessarily extends to essence.

Guided Path
1
Study the linguistic options
Three explanations are given for the construction 'kamithlihi' – as combination of two comparatives, as an extra kaf, or as an extra mithl.
All indicate exaggeration.
2
Understand the critique
The passage challenges the earlier response by arguing that denying similarity in specific attributes entails denying similarity in essence.
This is due to the necessary concomitance.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider whether the companions' intent was merely to claim prevention, not that the verse is limited.
The author ends with 'reflect'.

Source Text

هذا عند أبي علي الجبائي، (و) أما ( عند أبي هاشم ) فإنه ( يمتاز ) عما عداه من الذوات ) بحالة خامسة هي موجبة لهذه الأربعة نسميها بالإلهية ) قالوا : ولا يرد علينا قوله تعالى ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى : ۱۱ ] ، لأن المماثلة المنفية هاهنا هي المشاركة في أخص صفات النفس دون المشاركة في الذات والحقيقة فإن قيل: المذكور في الموقف الثاني الموجودية بدل الوجوب وهو الموافق لما في المحصل ،والأربعين أجيب بأن الوجود عند مثبتي الأحوال مشترك بين الموجودات كلها، فلا يتصور كونه مميزاً فالمراد بالموجودية المميزة هو الموجودية المقيدة بالواجبة. قوله : (ليس كمثله شيء) قيل العرب إذا أرادت المبالغة في نفي المشابهة جمعت بين حرفي التشبيه فيقول : ليس كمثله فلان أحد، وقيل الكاف صلة زيدت في الكلام للمبالغة. وقيل : المثل صلة كما قال الله تعالى : فإن آمنوا بمثل ما آمنتم به ﴾ [البقرة : ١٧ ] ، وقالوا يقال : ليس هذا كلام مثلك أي كلامك والكلام في هذه الآية مبسوط في المطلول وحواشينا عليه فلينظر فيهما . قوله: (هي المشاركة في أخص صفات النفس قد سبق في أواخر بحث الوجود أن المشاركة في الحقيقة تستلزم المشاركة في اللوازم ويشير إليه الآن أيضاً فنفي المشاركة في أخص الصفات يستلزم نفي المشاركة في الحقيقة فلا يصح قوله دون المشاركة في الذات، والحقيقة اللهم إلا أن يكون مرادهم مجرد استدلال الأصحاب بهذه الآية على نفي المشاركة في الحقيقة ويحمل كلامهم على المنع والسند فتأمل.
This is the view of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. As for Abu Hashim, he distinguishes Him from other essences by a fifth state that necessitates these four, which we call ilahiyyah. They said: The verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Quran 42:11) does not refute us, because the similarity denied here is participation in the most specific attributes of the self, not participation in essence and reality. If it is said: In the second station, existence (al-mawjudiyyah) is mentioned instead of wajub, which agrees with what is in al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in, the answer is that existence according to the affirmers of states is common among all existents, so it cannot be conceived as a distinguisher. What is meant by the distinctive existence is existence restricted by wajub. His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' — it is said: the Arabs, when they intend to exaggerate in negating similarity, combine two particles of comparison and say: 'There is no one like him.' And it is said: The kaf is an extra particle added for exaggeration. And it is said: 'Mithl' is an extra as God said: 'Then if they believe in the like of what you believe in' (Quran 2:137), and they said: It is said: 'This is not the speech of someone like you' meaning your speech. The discussion of this verse is detailed in the matlub and our glosses upon it, so let them be consulted. His saying: 'It is participation in the most specific attributes of the self' — it has preceded in the end of the discussion of existence that participation in reality necessitates participation in necessary concomitants, and he is also alluding to it now. So negating participation in the most specific attributes necessitates negating participation in reality. Thus his saying 'without participation in essence and reality' is not correct, unless their intent is merely the companions' use of this verse to negate participation in reality, and their statement is interpreted as prevention and support. So reflect.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage contrasts the views of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim. Abu Hashim distinguishes God from other essences by a fifth state (halah khamisah) called ilahiyyah, which causes the other four states (wajub, life, knowledge, power). It then addresses an objection based on the verse 'laysa kamithlihi shay'' (Quran 42:11). The response is that the similarity (mumathalah) denied in the verse is only participation in the most specific attributes (akhas sifat al-nafs), not participation in essence and reality. Then a further objection is considered: in some works (al-Muhassal and al-Arba'in) 'existence' (al-mawjudiyyah) is mentioned instead of 'wajub' as a distinguishing attribute. The answer is that existence is common among all existents, so it cannot distinguish unless it is restricted by wajub (al-mawjudiyyah al-muqayyadah bi al-wajibah). The commentary then discusses linguistic interpretations of the word 'ka' in the verse, explaining that the Arabs sometimes use it for exaggeration, and that 'mithl' can be extra. Finally, it critiques the earlier statement that the negation applies only to most specific attributes, arguing that negating participation in the most specific attributes implies negating participation in reality, so the claim that the verse does not deny participation in essence and reality is problematic. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms
المبالغة
الكاف الصلة
المثل الصلة
اللوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only denies similarity in some attributes, not in essence.
Correction: The critique shows that denying similarity in the most specific attributes logically entails denying similarity in essence.

Checkpoint
What does the critique conclude about the claim that the verse denies similarity only in specific attributes?
A) It is correct
B) It is incorrect because participation in attributes implies participation in essence
C) It is a valid interpretation
D) It is not discussed

Key Takeaway
The linguistic structure of the verse emphasizes negation, and the limited interpretation is logically untenable unless seen as a rhetorical objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'there is nothing like Allah,' are you truly negating every resemblance, or do you unconsciously reserve some aspects of similarity (e.g., attributes you can relate to)? Let this lesson push you to purify your tawhid from hidden analogies.
?
You just learned that limiting God's transcendence to specific attributes is logically untenable. Which areas of your life do you subtly measure Allah by human standards—such as forgiveness, patience, or justice—and how will you now uproot that limiting mindset?
?
The verse uses exaggerated negation to leave no room for comparison. How does it change your relationship with Allah to realize that nothing in your experience—no love, no power, no wisdom—can truly be likened to His? What step will you take today to act on that awe?

The Core Proof: Sharing Essence Leads to Composition
Jurjani presents the core argument against shared essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main argument that if God's essence were shared with another, composition would be necessary, which contradicts His necessary existence.

Core Idea
Two beings sharing the same essence would force composition—impossible for God.

Jurjani begins by establishing the central proof for God's unique essence. The argument is simple but profound: if two beings shared the exact same ultimate reality (quiddity), they would still need to be distinguished by something else, introducing composition into each being's identity. This composition conflicts with the necessary existence of God, as previously shown. Let's examine this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Begin by reading the opening lines of the source passage in Arabic. Notice the logical structure starting with 'لو شاركه غيره في الذات والحقيقة'.
Focus on understanding the conditional statement and its consequence.
2
Identify the three key elements
Look for three elements: the shared essence, the distinguishing individuation (تعين), and the necessary composition (تركيب). See how Jurjani connects them.
3
Understand why composition is impossible for God
Recall that earlier in the text, it was established that the Necessary Being cannot have composition. This is the foundation that makes the proof effective.
The proof relies on a previously proven premise.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Ask yourself: why is this argument considered definitive? Because it shows that any sharing of essence inevitably leads to an internal structure incompatible with absolute necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرجع التمييز بالحقيقة إلى القيد وتندفع المنافاة بين الكلامين (لنا) في إثبات المذهب الحق أنه تعالى (لو) شاركه غيره في الذات والحقيقة لخالفه بالتعين ضرورة الإثنينية فإن المتشاركين في تمام الماهية لا بد أن يتخالفا بتعين وتشخص حتى تمتاز به هويتهما ويتعددا (و) لا شك أن (ما به الاشراك غير ما به الامتياز فيلزم التركيب في هوية كل منهما وهو ينافي الوجوب الذاتي كما تقدم. قوله: فيلزم التركيب في هوية كل منهما فإن قلت الشخص عين الماهية في الخارج كما سبق تحقيقه فلا يلزم التركيب فيه. قلت: لو سلم فالتركيب في العقل أيضاً ممتنع في الواجب تعالى كما سلف. بقي أن يقال تعين الواجب تعالى زائد على حقيقته عند المتكلمين لكنه أمر عدمي ليس بداخل في هويته تعالى والمتشخص معروضه فلا يلزم التركيب فالأولى في الاستدلال أن يقال ذلك التعين ليس مقتضى ذاته ولا لازم ذاته تعالى وإلا لم يوجد شخص آخر من تلك الحقيقة مع أن المفروض اشتراك الذوات في تلك الحقيقة فتعين استناده إلى أمر منفصل فيلزم الاحتياج ويمكن أن يقال في بيان لزوم التركيب ذلك لما لم يجز أن يكون ذاته تعالى ولا لازم ذاته لما ذكرنا ولا عارضاً لأمر منفصل للزوم الاحتياج تعين عروضه لأمر مختص، وذلك الأمر إما ذاتي أو مستند إليه لئلا يلزم التسلسل وعلى التقديرين يلزم التركيب فتدبر.
So the distinction in reality goes back to the restriction, and the contradiction between the two statements is removed. (We say) in proving the true doctrine that He, exalted, (if) another shared with Him in essence and reality, He would differ from it by individuation (taʿayyun) necessarily due to duality. For two participants in the complete quiddity (māhiyya) must differ by individuation and particularization so that their identities are distinguished and they become two. And there is no doubt that that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so composition (tarkīb) would be necessary in the identity of each of them, and this contradicts necessary existence (al-wujūb al-dhātī) as previously mentioned. His statement: 'so composition would be necessary in the identity of each of them' — if you say: the individual is identical to the quiddity in the external world as previously verified, so no composition would be necessary in it. I say: even if granted, composition in the mind is also impossible for the Exalted Necessary Being as previously established. It remains to say: the individuation of the Exalted Necessary Being is additional to His reality according to the theologians, but it is a non-existent (ʿadamī) matter, not entering into His identity, and the individual itself is its subject, so composition is not necessary. So it is better in the proof to say: that individuation is neither a requirement of His essence nor a necessary concomitant of His essence, exalted; otherwise no other individual of that reality would exist, given the assumption that essences share in that reality. So it must be attributed to something separate, necessitating need (iḥtiyāj). And it can be said in explaining the necessity of composition: since it is not permissible for it to be His essence, exalted, nor a necessary concomitant of His essence due to what we mentioned, nor an accident from something separate due to the necessity of need, then its occurrence is determined by a specific thing. That thing is either essential (dhātī) or dependent on it to avoid regress (tasalsul), and in both cases composition is necessary. So ponder.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the initial proof that God's essence cannot be shared with any other being. The proof proceeds from the premise that if two entities share the same complete quiddity (māhiyya), they must still be distinguished by individuation (taʿayyun). The factor that makes them share is not the same as the factor that distinguishes them, and therefore each entity would be composed of a common element and a distinguishing element, i.e., composition (tarkīb). But composition is incompatible with necessary existence (al-wujūb al-dhātī), as previously established in the work. The commentator then addresses an objection: the individual in external reality is identical to the quiddity, so composition does not arise. The reply is that even if that is granted, composition in the mind is also impossible for God. Then a further point: the individuation of God is considered by some theologians to be additional but non-existent; still, such a distinguishing factor cannot be from His essence or a necessary concomitant, otherwise there would be no other individuals of that quiddity. Therefore it must come from an external source, which implies need (iḥtiyāj). Thus composition follows either way, and the argument is strengthened.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
تعين (taʿayyun)
تركيب (tarkīb)
وجوب ذاتي (wujūb dhātī)
مشاركة (mushāraka)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the immediate consequence of two beings sharing the same complete quiddity?
A) They become identical in every way
B) They must differ by individuation and particularization
C) They need to have different essences
D) Their identities become simple

Key Takeaway
Sharing the same essence forces composition, which is impossible for the Necessary Being, proving God's essence must be unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every created thing is composed of a shared quiddity and an individuating factor, how does that highlight your utter dependence on the uniquely simple Creator? What changes in how you view yourself and your possessions?
?
The proof shows that no being can share in God's essence. Are there any 'gods'—people, ambitions, or comforts—that you have allowed to occupy a place in your heart that belongs only to the One whose essence is incomparable? What concrete step will you take to remove them?
?
This lesson teaches that composition contradicts necessary existence. Reflect on your own life: where have you tried to add something to God's exclusive domain—like attributing power or control to other than Him? How can you actively repent from this subtle shirk?

Objections and Replies: Composition in Mind and External Reality
Jurjani addresses an objection and reinforces the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the individual is identical to the quiddity externally, and the reply that even mental composition is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Even if composition is denied in external reality, mental composition is equally impossible for God.

After presenting the main proof, Jurjani anticipates an objection: perhaps the individual (shakhṣ) is exactly the same as the quiddity (māhiyya) in the external world, so there is no real composition. He then gives a reply that even if that were true, composition in the mind is also impossible for God. Let's unpack this exchange and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection in Arabic
Find the phrase 'فإن قلت الشخص عين الماهية في الخارج' and understand the objection: the individual is identical to the quiddity externally, so composition does not arise.
The objection relies on a previous discussion where the identity of individual and quiddity in externals was established.
2
Read Jurjani's reply
Notice his reply: 'قلت: لو سلم فالتركيب في العقل أيضاً ممتنع في الواجب تعالى'. He concedes for argument but introduces a new point: mental composition is also impossible.
3
Appreciate the logical move
Jurjani is not conceding the objection; he is showing that even if it is granted, the argument still holds because any kind of composition—even mental—is impossible for God.
4
Connect to the core proof
Recall that the core proof required composition. Now we see that the impossibility of composition applies universally, not just in external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refining the Proof: Individuation as Non-Existent and the Better Argument
Jurjani provides a refined proof against shared essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined argument that individuation cannot be a part of God's essence or a necessary concomitant, nor can it come from without, thus composition is inevitable.

Core Idea
Individuation of God must be either essential, concomitant, or external—all impossible, forcing composition.

Some theologians argued that individuation (taʿayyun) is a non-existent (ʿadamī) addition, not part of the essence. Jurjani addresses this and presents a better argument: the distinguishing factor cannot be His essence itself nor a necessary concomitant (otherwise no other individual of that quiddity could exist), nor can it come from an external source (that would imply need). Therefore, it must inhere in a specific attribute, leading to composition. Let's follow this intricate reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the refined argument in Arabic
Start from 'بقي أن يقال تعين الواجب تعالى زائد على حقيقته عند المتكلمين' and follow the reasoning about the nature of individuation.
Notice the phrase 'الأولى في الاستدلال' indicating a preferred way of proving.
2
Understand the three possibilities
Jurjani lists three possibilities for the source of individuation: essence itself, necessary concomitant, or external cause. He rules out each.
3
See how composition follows
Once external cause is ruled out, individuation must come from something specific within the being, which introduces composition.
The final line: 'وعلى التقديرين يلزم التركيب'.
4
Reflect on the depth of the argument
This refined proof accounts for the view that individuation is non-existent and still shows composition is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرجع التمييز بالحقيقة إلى القيد وتندفع المنافاة بين الكلامين (لنا) في إثبات المذهب الحق أنه تعالى (لو) شاركه غيره في الذات والحقيقة لخالفه بالتعين ضرورة الإثنينية فإن المتشاركين في تمام الماهية لا بد أن يتخالفا بتعين وتشخص حتى تمتاز به هويتهما ويتعددا (و) لا شك أن (ما به الاشراك غير ما به الامتياز فيلزم التركيب في هوية كل منهما وهو ينافي الوجوب الذاتي كما تقدم. قوله: فيلزم التركيب في هوية كل منهما فإن قلت الشخص عين الماهية في الخارج كما سبق تحقيقه فلا يلزم التركيب فيه. قلت: لو سلم فالتركيب في العقل أيضاً ممتنع في الواجب تعالى كما سلف. بقي أن يقال تعين الواجب تعالى زائد على حقيقته عند المتكلمين لكنه أمر عدمي ليس بداخل في هويته تعالى والمتشخص معروضه فلا يلزم التركيب فالأولى في الاستدلال أن يقال ذلك التعين ليس مقتضى ذاته ولا لازم ذاته تعالى وإلا لم يوجد شخص آخر من تلك الحقيقة مع أن المفروض اشتراك الذوات في تلك الحقيقة فتعين استناده إلى أمر منفصل فيلزم الاحتياج ويمكن أن يقال في بيان لزوم التركيب ذلك لما لم يجز أن يكون ذاته تعالى ولا لازم ذاته لما ذكرنا ولا عارضاً لأمر منفصل للزوم الاحتياج تعين عروضه لأمر مختص، وذلك الأمر إما ذاتي أو مستند إليه لئلا يلزم التسلسل وعلى التقديرين يلزم التركيب فتدبر.
So the distinction in reality goes back to the restriction, and the contradiction between the two statements is removed. (We say) in proving the true doctrine that He, exalted, (if) another shared with Him in essence and reality, He would differ from it by individuation (taʿayyun) necessarily due to duality. For two participants in the complete quiddity (māhiyya) must differ by individuation and particularization so that their identities are distinguished and they become two. And there is no doubt that that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished, so composition (tarkīb) would be necessary in the identity of each of them, and this contradicts necessary existence (al-wujūb al-dhātī) as previously mentioned. His statement: 'so composition would be necessary in the identity of each of them' — if you say: the individual is identical to the quiddity in the external world as previously verified, so no composition would be necessary in it. I say: even if granted, composition in the mind is also impossible for the Exalted Necessary Being as previously established. It remains to say: the individuation of the Exalted Necessary Being is additional to His reality according to the theologians, but it is a non-existent (ʿadamī) matter, not entering into His identity, and the individual itself is its subject, so composition is not necessary. So it is better in the proof to say: that individuation is neither a requirement of His essence nor a necessary concomitant of His essence, exalted; otherwise no other individual of that reality would exist, given the assumption that essences share in that reality. So it must be attributed to something separate, necessitating need (iḥtiyāj). And it can be said in explaining the necessity of composition: since it is not permissible for it to be His essence, exalted, nor a necessary concomitant of His essence due to what we mentioned, nor an accident from something separate due to the necessity of need, then its occurrence is determined by a specific thing. That thing is either essential (dhātī) or dependent on it to avoid regress (tasalsul), and in both cases composition is necessary. So ponder.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the initial proof that God's essence cannot be shared with any other being. The proof proceeds from the premise that if two entities share the same complete quiddity (māhiyya), they must still be distinguished by individuation (taʿayyun). The factor that makes them share is not the same as the factor that distinguishes them, and therefore each entity would be composed of a common element and a distinguishing element, i.e., composition (tarkīb). But composition is incompatible with necessary existence (al-wujūb al-dhātī), as previously established in the work. The commentator then addresses an objection: the individual in external reality is identical to the quiddity, so composition does not arise. The reply is that even if that is granted, composition in the mind is also impossible for God. Then a further point: the individuation of God is considered by some theologians to be additional but non-existent; still, such a distinguishing factor cannot be from His essence or a necessary concomitant, otherwise there would be no other individuals of that quiddity. Therefore it must come from an external source, which implies need (iḥtiyāj). Thus composition follows either way, and the argument is strengthened.

Key Terms
تعين (taʿayyun)
عدم (ʿadam)
لازم ذات (lāzim dhāt)
احتياج (iḥtiyāj)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refined proof, why can't individuation be a necessary concomitant of God's essence?
A) It would make God dependent on creation
B) No other individual of that reality could then exist
C) It would imply multiple necessary beings
D) It would be external to the essence

Key Takeaway
Even when individuation is considered non-existent, the proof that composition follows holds by eliminating all possible sources of individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that any attempt to avoid composition in God's attributes leads to need or dependence. How does your own understanding of God's oneness sometimes rely on comfortable assumptions that this argument exposes as insufficient? What specific assumption about God's simplicity are you tempted to hold without scrutiny?
?
Consider the phrase 'So ponder' (fataʿammal) at the end. What does it reveal about the seriousness of this intellectual pursuit? Are you truly willing to engage in this rigorous logical examination of your beliefs, or do you prefer to avoid it out of fear of complexity? What would it cost you to take this 'ponder' seriously?
?
The argument forces a choice: either individuation leads to composition or to need. Both are impossible for God. How does this challenge your conception of God's unique being? Are you comfortable with a God who is beyond all categories and logical distinctions, or do you subconsciously try to limit Him to fit human logic? What would it mean to truly accept that God's oneness is absolutely simple and beyond our comprehension?

Refutation of the Argument that Essence is Shared
Jurjani responds to three objections claiming essence is shared.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while the concept of essence is shared between God and creatures, the real essence is not, and the distinction between concept and reality is crucial.

Core Idea
Shared concept, not shared reality.

The opponents argue that because the word 'essence' applies to both God and creation, they must share the same reality. In this passage, Jurjani refutes this by distinguishing between the concept of essence and the real particular essences. He shows that the shared concept is merely an accidental description, not the true quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of objection and answer.
Pay attention to the three objections and the structure of the response.
2
Identify the three objections
Notice how the opponents present three distinct reasons for claiming that essence is shared: (1) division into necessary and possible, (2) certainty about essence despite uncertainty about particulars, (3) rational division of sciences.
Each objection attempts to show a unitary concept of essence.
3
Understand the answer
Jurjani's response distinguishes between the concept (mafhūm) of essence and the real essence. The shared concept is an accident (ʿāriḍ) of the particular essences, not the essence itself.
The concept is what can be known or spoken about, or what subsists by itself.
4
Reflect on the consequence
The opponent's view leads to the absurdity that all things share the same quiddity and differ only in accidents. Our view avoids this and is coherent.
This shows the importance of distinguishing concept from reality in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على كون الذات مشتركة بين الواجب وغيره بما مر في اشتراك الوجود من الوجوه وتقريرها هنا أن الذات تنقسم إلى الواجب والممكن ومورد القسمة مشترك أقسامه وأيضاً فنحن نجزم به أي بالذات مع التردد في الخصوصيات من الواجب والجواهر والأعراض على قياس ما مر في الوجود وأيضا فقولنا العلوم إما ذات وإما صفة حصر عقلي، فلولا أن المفهوم من الذات شيء واحد لم يكن كذلك، والجواب أن المشترك مفهوم الذات أعني ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه أو ما يقوم بنفسه وأنه أي مفهوم الذات على الوجهين أمر عارض للذوات المخصوصة المتخالفة الحقائق على مآل قولهم إلى أن الأشياء متساوية في تمام الماهية اختلافها في اللوازم وهو غير معقول، ومآل قولنا إلى عكس ذلك وهو ممكن، وهذا بين مع ما ذكرنا
They argued that essence (al-dhāt) is shared between the Necessary and other than Him, based on what has been mentioned regarding the sharing of existence (al-wujūd) in some respects. Its presentation here is that essence is divided into the necessary and the possible, and the subject of division is common to its divisions. Also, we are certain of it (i.e., essence) while being uncertain about the particularities (al-khuṣūṣiyyāt) of the Necessary, substances, and accidents, analogous to what was mentioned regarding existence. Also, our statement 'the sciences are either essence or attribute' is a rational division, so unless the concept (al-mafhūm) of essence were one thing, it would not be so. The answer: what is shared is the concept of essence, i.e., what can be known and spoken about, or what subsists by itself. And that (i.e., the concept of essence in both senses) is an accident (ʿāriḍ) of particular essences with differing realities. This leads to the consequence that things are equal in the complete quiddity and differ only in concomitants (lawāzim), which is unintelligible. Our view leads to the opposite, which is possible. This is clear with what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents and the author's answer. The opponents argue that the term 'essence' (dhāt) is shared between God and creatures, using three arguments: (1) essence divides into necessary and possible, so the subject of division is common; (2) we are certain of essence while doubting its particularizations; (3) the rational division of sciences into essence and attribute presupposes a unitary concept of essence. The answer is that what is shared is only the concept (mafhūm) of essence, not the reality itself. This concept is an accident (ʿāriḍ) that applies to different particular essences. The opponents' view leads to the absurdity that all things share the same quiddity and differ only in accidental concomitants, while the author's view avoids that absurdity. Thus the alleged sharing is only in the concept, not in the real nature of things.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
مفهوم (mafhūm)
عارض (ʿāriḍ)
ماهية (māhiyya)
متشارك (mushtarak)
خصوصيات (khuṣūṣiyyāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the word 'essence' applies to God and creatures, they must share the same essence.
Correction: The shared concept is an accident; real essences are fundamentally different.

Checkpoint

A) The reality of essence
B) The concept of essence
C) Neither
D) Both

Key Takeaway
The concept of essence is shared, but the real essence of God is unique and distinct from all contingent essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists' and 'I exist,' do you unconsciously imagine God's existence as a greater version of your own existence? How does the lesson's distinction between shared concept and shared reality force you to rethink that assumption?
?
The opponents argued that shared language implies shared reality. In your prayer or contemplation, where might you be guilty of assuming that the same word (like 'mercy' or 'love') means exactly the same thing when applied to God and humans? Will you still use those words, and how will you remind yourself that they are merely conceptual accidents?
?
The author warns that treating essence as a shared reality leads to the absurdity that all things are equal in their quiddity. Have you ever, even subtly, imagined that God and creation are 'the same kind of thing' only differing in degree? What practical difference would it make in your worship and awe of God if you truly internalize that His essence is utterly unique?
