Refutation of the Philosophers' View on Divine Essence
Refutation of the philosophers' statement on God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' claim that God's essence is common existence is false and leads to impossible consequences.

Core Idea
God's essence cannot be mixed with any contingent, even filth.

This lesson examines a transmitted statement attributed to the philosophers, who claimed that God's essence is the same as His existence common to all existents. The passage presents two clearer formulations of this view, then refutes them by showing that the first entails God being mixed with all things, which is impossible, and the second leads to equality in necessary attributes. The author also expresses doubt about the accuracy of this transmission, citing al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā as holding the opposite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmitted statement and its formulations
Read the Arabic passage describing the philosophers' view and the two improved formulations. Notice how each formulation tries to clarify the distinction.
The first formulation says God's existence is not additional; the second says it is distinguished by not being accidental.
2
Understand the two refutations
Identify the two consequences that prove the view false: mixing with filth and equality in necessary attributes. Reflect on why these are impossible for God.
3
Consider the author's doubt and citation
Read where the author casts doubt on the transmission and quotes al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā as saying the opposite. This shows the view may not represent all philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تنبيه نقل عن الحكماء أنهم قالوا ذاته تعالى ( وجوده المشترك بين جميع الموجودات ويمتاز عن غيره بقيد سلبي وهو عدم عروضه للغير، فإن وجود الممكنات مقارن لماهية مغايرة له ووجوده ليس كذلك، وفي هذه العبارة نوع قصور والأظهر أن يقال ذاته الوجود المشترك بين الجميع ويمتاز عن غيره بقيد سلبي وجوده ليس زائداً عليه بل هو عنيه بخلاف سائر الموجودات، فإن وجودها زائد على ماهياتها أو يقال ذاته وجوده المساوي لسائر الموجودات بناء على اشتراك الموجود ويمتاز عنها بعدم عروضه لماهيته بخلاف وجودات الممكنات فإنها عارضة لماهياتها ( وهذا باطل ) بطلانه ظاهر، أما على المعنى الأول فلأنه يلزم منه أن تكون حقيقة الواجب أمراً مخالطاً لجميع الممكنات حتى القاذورات ولا يخفى استحالته، وأما على المعنى الثاني فلأنه يلزم منه التساوي في الصفات اللازمة قال المصنف ( ولم يتحقق عندي هذا النقل عنهم بل قد صرح الفارابي وابن سينا بخلافه فإنهما قالا الوجود المشترك الذي هو الكون في الأعيان زائد على ماهيته تعالى بالضرورة وإنما هو مقارن لوجود خاص هو المبحث ) هل هو زائد عارض لماهيته أو ليس بزائد .

[ المقصد الثالث : في أن وجوده نفس ماهيته ]
) في أن وجوده نفس (ماهيته كما هو مذهب الشيخ وأبي الحسين والحكماء ) ) أم زائد ) عليها كما هو مذهب جمهور المتكلمين، ( وإنه مساو لوجود الممكنات أم مخالف وقد تقدم في الأمور العامة ما فيه كفاية ) فلا معنى للإعادة .

ثم قال الشارح:
قوله : ( تنبيه نقل من الحكماء الأظهر أن يذكر هذا التنبيه في المقصد الثالث كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ( وفي هذه العبارة نوع (قصور) لأن الضمير في قوله : ويمتاز عن غيره إن كان راجعا إلى ذاته تعالى لم يصح أن يجعل عدم العروض للغير سبباً للامتياز لأن كثيراً من الجواهر، كذلك اللهم إلا أن يجعل ضمير عروضه لوجوده أعني الوجود المضاف من حيث هو مضاف ليصح كونه سبب امتياز الذات، وهو على تقدير صحته خلاف الظاهر وإن كان راجعاً إلى الوجود المشترك فهم . منه على تقدير على صحته أن المقصود بيان امتياز الوجود املطلق عن الوجودات الخاصة وذلك الامتياز ليس بمقصود بالبيان هاهنا بل المقصود إما بيان امتیاز ذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات أو بيان امتيازه عن سائر الوجودات الخاصة .
قوله : (مخالطاً لجميع الممكنات قالوا لا يخفى أن نور الشمس إذا وقع على الأشياء لم يعرض له بسببه نقصان أصلا وإذا كان في المحسوسات شيء كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ما هو عال عن ادراك العقول كذلك والحق أنه عذر بارد .
(A note: It is transmitted from the philosophers that they said: His Essence is His existence common among all existents, and it is distinguished from others by a negative condition, namely the non-occurrence of accidents to it. For the existence of contingent things is concomitant with an essence other than it, while His existence is not like that. In this expression there is a kind of deficiency. The more apparent is to say: His Essence is the existence common among all, and it is distinguished from others by a negative condition: His existence is not additional to Him, rather it is identical to Him, unlike the rest of existents, for their existence is additional to their essences. Or to say: His Essence is His existence equal to the rest of existents, based on the commonality of existence, and it is distinguished from them by the non-occurrence of accidents to His essence, unlike the existences of contingents, for they are accidental to their essences. And this is false; its falsehood is clear. As for the first meaning, it entails that the reality of the Necessary is something mixed with all contingent things, even filth, and its impossibility is not hidden. As for the second meaning, it entails equality in necessary attributes. The author said: I have not verified this transmission from them; indeed, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā explicitly stated the opposite, for they said: The common existence which is being in external entities is additional to His essence necessarily, and it is only concomitant with a specific existence, which is the subject of investigation: whether it is additional and accidental to His essence or not.

[The Third Aim: On that His existence is identical to His essence]
On that His existence is identical to His essence, as is the doctrine of al-Shaykh, Abū al-Ḥusayn, and the philosophers, or additional to it, as is the doctrine of the majority of theologians, and whether it is equal to the existence of contingent things or different. And what suffices has already been presented in the general matters, so there is no point in repetition.

Then the commentator said:
His statement: (A note: transmitted from the philosophers) – It is more apparent that this note should be mentioned in the third aim, as is not hidden.
His statement: (In this expression there is a kind of deficiency) – Because the pronoun in his statement “and it is distinguished from others” if it refers to His Essence, then making non-occurrence of accidents to it a cause of distinction is not valid, because many substances are like that. Unless the pronoun of “its occurrence” refers to His existence, i.e., the existence added insofar as it is added, so that it is valid as a cause of distinction of the essence. But on the assumption of its validity, it is contrary to the apparent. And if it refers to common existence, then one understands from it on the assumption of its validity that the purpose is to show the distinction of absolute existence from particular existences, but that distinction is not intended for clarification here; rather, the purpose is either to show the distinction of His Essence from other essences, or to show its distinction from other particular existences.
His statement: (mixed with all contingent things) – They said: It is not hidden that when sunlight falls on things, it does not suffer any deficiency because of it. And if something like that exists in sensible things, then why is it not permissible that what is above the perception of intellects be like that? The truth is that it is a weak excuse.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a transmitted statement attributed to the philosophers regarding God's essence, followed by the introduction of the third maqṣad on whether God's existence is identical to His essence or additional. The passage begins with a note (tanbīh) that the philosophers are reported to have said that God's essence is His existence common among all existents, distinguished by a negative condition: it does not have accidents. The commentator points out a deficiency in this expression and offers two clearer formulations: either that God's essence is the existence common to all and His existence is identical to Him (not additional), or that His essence is His existence equal to all existents based on common existence, distinguished by not being accidental to His essence. Both formulations are declared false. The first entails God's essence being mixed with all contingent things, including filth, which is impossible. The second entails equality in necessary attributes. The author (the original writer of al-Mawaqif) then says he has not verified this transmission, and he cites al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā as explicitly saying that common existence (al-wujūd al-mushtarak) is additional to God's essence necessarily. Then the third maqṣad is presented, stating the two positions: existence identical to essence (the view of al-Shaykh, Abū al-Ḥusayn, and the philosophers) versus additional (the view of most theologians), and whether it is equal or different. The commentator notes that the note should have been placed in this third maqṣad. He then explains the deficiency in the transmitted expression: the pronoun reference causes issues, and the intended distinction is not clearly about absolute versus particular existence but about God's essence versus others. Finally, he refutes the possible objection that if God's essence were mixed with all things, it would be like sunlight that does not suffer deficiency, calling it a weak excuse.

Key Terms
قيد سلبي
وجود مشترك
مخالط
بطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers' view that God's essence is common existence is acceptable and even mystical.
Correction: The passage shows it is false because it would make God's essence mixed with all things, including impure, and would imply equality in attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence of the philosophers' statement that God's essence is common existence?
A) It implies that God's existence is additional to His essence
B) It implies that God's essence is mixed with all contingent things, even filth
C) It implies that God's essence is equal to all other essences
D) It implies that God's existence is non-existential

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that God's essence is common existence is false because it leads to impossible consequences like mixing with filth and equality in attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's essence could be mixed with something as lowly as filth, what does that say about the reverence you truly hold for Him in your daily thoughts and actions? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of His absolute transcendence?
?
You just read that a false belief about God leads to 'equality in necessary attributes' between Him and creation. In your own life, where have you unconsciously treated God as if He shares limitations with His creation—perhaps in how you expect Him to act or how you measure His mercy?
?
The author doubts the transmission of this view from the philosophers, reminding us to verify what we attribute to scholars or schools of thought. Think of one theological claim you have accepted without scrutiny. Are you willing to re-examine it today, even if it means changing your understanding?

The Third Maqṣad: Existence Identical to Essence or Additional
Introduction of the third maqṣad and commentator's analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on whether God's existence is identical to His essence or additional, and the commentator's critique of the phrasing of the philosophers' view.

Core Idea
Whether God's existence is identical to His essence or additional is a core debate in kalam.

After refuting the specific philosophical claim about common existence, the text introduces the third maqṣad which formally poses the question: Is God's existence identical to His essence or additional? The commentator then explains that the earlier note should have been placed here, and analyzes the deficiency in the expression of the transmitted statement. He also refutes a weak analogy comparing God's essence mixing with things to sunlight affecting objects without being corrupted.

Guided Path
1
Read the third maqṣad text
Read the short passage that defines the aim: whether existence is identical to essence or additional, and whether it is equal to contingent existence or different.
The author says this has been sufficiently discussed in the general matters, so no repetition.
2
Understand the commentator's placement remark
The commentator notes that the earlier note about the philosophers should have been placed here in the third maqṣad. Think about why this is more appropriate.
3
Analyze the deficiency in the expression
The commentator explains why the original expression has a deficiency: the pronoun reference is unclear and the intended distinction is ambiguous.
This shows the importance of precise language in theological statements.
4
Reflect on the sunlight analogy refutation
The passage ends with a refutation of an analogy that mixing is like sunlight not being corrupted. The commentator calls it a weak excuse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تنبيه نقل عن الحكماء أنهم قالوا ذاته تعالى ( وجوده المشترك بين جميع الموجودات ويمتاز عن غيره بقيد سلبي وهو عدم عروضه للغير، فإن وجود الممكنات مقارن لماهية مغايرة له ووجوده ليس كذلك، وفي هذه العبارة نوع قصور والأظهر أن يقال ذاته الوجود المشترك بين الجميع ويمتاز عن غيره بقيد سلبي وجوده ليس زائداً عليه بل هو عنيه بخلاف سائر الموجودات، فإن وجودها زائد على ماهياتها أو يقال ذاته وجوده المساوي لسائر الموجودات بناء على اشتراك الموجود ويمتاز عنها بعدم عروضه لماهيته بخلاف وجودات الممكنات فإنها عارضة لماهياتها ( وهذا باطل ) بطلانه ظاهر، أما على المعنى الأول فلأنه يلزم منه أن تكون حقيقة الواجب أمراً مخالطاً لجميع الممكنات حتى القاذورات ولا يخفى استحالته، وأما على المعنى الثاني فلأنه يلزم منه التساوي في الصفات اللازمة قال المصنف ( ولم يتحقق عندي هذا النقل عنهم بل قد صرح الفارابي وابن سينا بخلافه فإنهما قالا الوجود المشترك الذي هو الكون في الأعيان زائد على ماهيته تعالى بالضرورة وإنما هو مقارن لوجود خاص هو المبحث ) هل هو زائد عارض لماهيته أو ليس بزائد .

[ المقصد الثالث : في أن وجوده نفس ماهيته ]
) في أن وجوده نفس (ماهيته كما هو مذهب الشيخ وأبي الحسين والحكماء ) ) أم زائد ) عليها كما هو مذهب جمهور المتكلمين، ( وإنه مساو لوجود الممكنات أم مخالف وقد تقدم في الأمور العامة ما فيه كفاية ) فلا معنى للإعادة .

ثم قال الشارح:
قوله : ( تنبيه نقل من الحكماء الأظهر أن يذكر هذا التنبيه في المقصد الثالث كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ( وفي هذه العبارة نوع (قصور) لأن الضمير في قوله : ويمتاز عن غيره إن كان راجعا إلى ذاته تعالى لم يصح أن يجعل عدم العروض للغير سبباً للامتياز لأن كثيراً من الجواهر، كذلك اللهم إلا أن يجعل ضمير عروضه لوجوده أعني الوجود المضاف من حيث هو مضاف ليصح كونه سبب امتياز الذات، وهو على تقدير صحته خلاف الظاهر وإن كان راجعاً إلى الوجود المشترك فهم . منه على تقدير على صحته أن المقصود بيان امتياز الوجود املطلق عن الوجودات الخاصة وذلك الامتياز ليس بمقصود بالبيان هاهنا بل المقصود إما بيان امتیاز ذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات أو بيان امتيازه عن سائر الوجودات الخاصة .
قوله : (مخالطاً لجميع الممكنات قالوا لا يخفى أن نور الشمس إذا وقع على الأشياء لم يعرض له بسببه نقصان أصلا وإذا كان في المحسوسات شيء كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ما هو عال عن ادراك العقول كذلك والحق أنه عذر بارد .
(A note: It is transmitted from the philosophers that they said: His Essence is His existence common among all existents, and it is distinguished from others by a negative condition, namely the non-occurrence of accidents to it. For the existence of contingent things is concomitant with an essence other than it, while His existence is not like that. In this expression there is a kind of deficiency. The more apparent is to say: His Essence is the existence common among all, and it is distinguished from others by a negative condition: His existence is not additional to Him, rather it is identical to Him, unlike the rest of existents, for their existence is additional to their essences. Or to say: His Essence is His existence equal to the rest of existents, based on the commonality of existence, and it is distinguished from them by the non-occurrence of accidents to His essence, unlike the existences of contingents, for they are accidental to their essences. And this is false; its falsehood is clear. As for the first meaning, it entails that the reality of the Necessary is something mixed with all contingent things, even filth, and its impossibility is not hidden. As for the second meaning, it entails equality in necessary attributes. The author said: I have not verified this transmission from them; indeed, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā explicitly stated the opposite, for they said: The common existence which is being in external entities is additional to His essence necessarily, and it is only concomitant with a specific existence, which is the subject of investigation: whether it is additional and accidental to His essence or not.

[The Third Aim: On that His existence is identical to His essence]
On that His existence is identical to His essence, as is the doctrine of al-Shaykh, Abū al-Ḥusayn, and the philosophers, or additional to it, as is the doctrine of the majority of theologians, and whether it is equal to the existence of contingent things or different. And what suffices has already been presented in the general matters, so there is no point in repetition.

Then the commentator said:
His statement: (A note: transmitted from the philosophers) – It is more apparent that this note should be mentioned in the third aim, as is not hidden.
His statement: (In this expression there is a kind of deficiency) – Because the pronoun in his statement “and it is distinguished from others” if it refers to His Essence, then making non-occurrence of accidents to it a cause of distinction is not valid, because many substances are like that. Unless the pronoun of “its occurrence” refers to His existence, i.e., the existence added insofar as it is added, so that it is valid as a cause of distinction of the essence. But on the assumption of its validity, it is contrary to the apparent. And if it refers to common existence, then one understands from it on the assumption of its validity that the purpose is to show the distinction of absolute existence from particular existences, but that distinction is not intended for clarification here; rather, the purpose is either to show the distinction of His Essence from other essences, or to show its distinction from other particular existences.
His statement: (mixed with all contingent things) – They said: It is not hidden that when sunlight falls on things, it does not suffer any deficiency because of it. And if something like that exists in sensible things, then why is it not permissible that what is above the perception of intellects be like that? The truth is that it is a weak excuse.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a transmitted statement attributed to the philosophers regarding God's essence, followed by the introduction of the third maqṣad on whether God's existence is identical to His essence or additional. The passage begins with a note (tanbīh) that the philosophers are reported to have said that God's essence is His existence common among all existents, distinguished by a negative condition: it does not have accidents. The commentator points out a deficiency in this expression and offers two clearer formulations: either that God's essence is the existence common to all and His existence is identical to Him (not additional), or that His essence is His existence equal to all existents based on common existence, distinguished by not being accidental to His essence. Both formulations are declared false. The first entails God's essence being mixed with all contingent things, including filth, which is impossible. The second entails equality in necessary attributes. The author (the original writer of al-Mawaqif) then says he has not verified this transmission, and he cites al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā as explicitly saying that common existence (al-wujūd al-mushtarak) is additional to God's essence necessarily. Then the third maqṣad is presented, stating the two positions: existence identical to essence (the view of al-Shaykh, Abū al-Ḥusayn, and the philosophers) versus additional (the view of most theologians), and whether it is equal or different. The commentator notes that the note should have been placed in this third maqṣad. He then explains the deficiency in the transmitted expression: the pronoun reference causes issues, and the intended distinction is not clearly about absolute versus particular existence but about God's essence versus others. Finally, he refutes the possible objection that if God's essence were mixed with all things, it would be like sunlight that does not suffer deficiency, calling it a weak excuse.

Key Terms
وجود نفس الماهية
مذهب الشيخ
أبي الحسين
الحكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that God's essence being mixed with creation is analogous to sunlight falling on objects without being affected.
Correction: The commentator calls this a weak excuse; the analogy fails because God's transcendence is beyond such comparisons.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, where should the note about the philosophers' statement have been placed?
A) In the first maqṣad
B) In the second maqṣad
C) In the third maqṣad
D) In a separate appendix

Key Takeaway
The third maqṣad frames the debate on whether existence is identical to essence, and the commentator clarifies that precise language is crucial.

Have you ever spoken about Allah's attributes with ambiguity, assuming your listener understands exactly what you mean? How does the commentator's critique of the philosophers' ambiguous pronoun convict you to be more precise in your own speech about God? Commit to examining one statement you make about Allah this week and ensuring it is clear and accurate.

The commentator dismisses the sunlight analogy as a 'weak excuse'. Where in your own faith have you used a weak analogy or rationalization to avoid a deeper truth about Allah? Will you ruthlessly examine and discard such excuses today?

The Verbal Nature of the Dispute Over Direction
Al-Jurjani analyzes a verbal dispute over divine direction and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani classifies certain theological disputes as merely verbal, and why affirming a direction unlike bodies is not a meaningful position.

Core Idea
Not every dispute is real; some are just about wording.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a specific anthropomorphic view that says Allah is in a direction but not like bodies. He argues that this claim is only verbal, not a real difference in meaning. He then defends this classification against an objection that compares it to the case of affirming vision for Allah. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being assessed
Read the opening line where al-Jurjani presents the view of someone who says Allah is in a direction but not like bodies. Notice that he immediately calls it a verbal dispute.
The claim is from anthropomorphists who try to avoid crass anthropomorphism.
2
Understand the objection
The next part introduces an objection: affirming a direction unlike bodies is not the same as negating direction, just like affirming vision is not the same as negating vision. So why is the dispute verbal?
The objection compares direction to vision to argue that both can be affirmed in a unique way.
3
Follow the answer
Al-Jurjani responds by distinguishing the two cases. Vision has a clear meaning—complete visual disclosure without direction or facing—so it can be debated. Direction, however, only makes sense as pointing 'here' or 'there', so any other sense is meaningless and not even negated.
The key difference: vision's meaning is definable; direction's only definable sense is physical pointing.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the only meaningful sense of direction is physical pointing, and that is negated, affirming a direction unlike bodies adds nothing real. Thus the dispute is merely verbal.
This shows how careful definition resolves apparent disagreements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : ليس كونه في الجهة ككون الأجسام في الجهة والمنازعة مع هذا القائل راجعة إلى اللفظ دون المعنى والاطلاق اللفظي متوقف على ورود الشرع به . قوله : ( راجعة إلى اللفظ دون المعنى قيل فيه بحث لأن إثبات الجهة لا كجهات الأجسام ليس كنفيها، كما أن إثبات الرؤية لا كرؤية الأجسام ليس كنفيها، وأجيب بأن الرؤية المذكورة لها معنى يصلح محلا للنزاع، وهو الانكشاف التام البصري بلا جهة ومقابلة بخلاف الجهة فإن المنفي منها هو الجهة المشار إليها بهاهنا أو هناك وأما ما سواها فمسكوت عنه لا منفي .
And among them is one who said: His being in the direction is not like the being of bodies in the direction. And the dispute with this speaker reverts to wording without meaning, and the verbal application depends on the arrival of the Law. His statement: (reverts to wording without meaning) – it was said there is a discussion in it, because affirming a direction not like the directions of bodies is not like negating it, just as affirming vision not like the vision of bodies is not like negating it. And it was answered that the mentioned vision has a meaning that is suitable as a locus of dispute, which is complete visual disclosure without direction or confrontation, unlike the direction, for what is negated of it is the direction indicated by 'here' or 'there', and as for what is other than that, it is not mentioned, not negated.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage deals with a particular anthropomorphic view that asserts Allah is in a direction but not like bodies. The author claims that this dispute is only verbal, not real, and that any verbal affirmation of direction would require scriptural warrant. A potential objection is raised: affirming a direction that is unlike bodily directions is not the same as negating direction altogether, just as affirming vision for Allah (unlike bodily vision) is not the same as negating vision. The response clarifies that the case of vision is different because the meaning of vision—complete visual disclosure without direction or facing—provides a valid subject for dispute. However, for direction, what is negated is specifically the direction that can be pointed to as 'here' or 'there'. Any other sense of direction is left unmentioned and therefore not negated. Hence the dispute is not over a meaningful content but merely over wording.

Key Terms
اللفظ
المعنى
الجهة
الرؤية
الانكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming a direction unlike bodies is a meaningful middle ground.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that the only meaningful sense of direction involves physical pointing, which must be negated. Any other sense is undefined and not a real position.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani consider the dispute over direction to be verbal rather than substantive?
A) Because the anthropomorphists have no proof.
B) Because direction has no coherent meaning other than physical pointing, which is negated.
C) Because the Law has not spoken on direction.
D) Because the dispute is about wording only, and wording is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Theological disputes may be verbal when one side uses a term with no clear, shared meaning; definition resolves them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's 'hand' or 'face', do you slip into imagining a physical limb? How does this lesson's distinction between terms with defined meanings and undefined ones challenge you to guard your tongue and heart from such images?
?
The objection in this passage equates direction and vision as analogous cases. Are there other theological terms you have treated as analogous without checking whether they have a clear meaning? How can you know when a term is truly beyond human comprehension?
?
Al-Jurjani says the dispute is 'only about wording'. Think of a disagreement you have had about religion that might have been just about words. What would it cost you to admit that and seek the real meaning?

Refuting the Multiplicity of Gods Argument
The author presents and refutes the argument that if God were a body, parts would have attributes leading to multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that God as a body leads to multiple gods is refuted by the permissibility of a single attribute attaching to the whole.

Core Idea
One attribute can unify the parts, so no multiplicity of gods follows.

This passage presents a key objection against the corporeality of God and its refutation. The objection argues that a body with parts would necessitate multiple deities because each part would have distinct attributes. The refutation, however, shows that attributes can apply to the whole as a whole, preserving divine unity without implying multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection, its implications, and the refutation.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فيلزم' (necessitates) and 'وهذا الاستدلال ضعيف' (this reasoning is weak).
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objection argues: if God were a body, then each part would have distinct attributes (knowledge, power, life). Since one accident cannot be in two places, these attributes must be multiple, leading to multiple gods. Also, the objector must hold that a single human has multiple such attributes to avoid inconsistency.
The objector's commitment is highlighted: he must accept multiple attributes in one human to save his proof.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that the objection is very weak because it is permissible for a single attribute to attach to the whole as a whole. Therefore, the parts do not need separate attributes, and no multiplicity of gods follows.
This refutation undermines the assumption that attributes must be tied to individual parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الواجب جسماً لقام بكل جزء منه علم وقدرة وحياة مغايرة لما قام بالجزء الآخر ضرورة امتناع قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين، فيكون كل واحد من أجزائه مستقلاً بكل واحد من صفات الكمال فيلزم تعدد الآلهة، وهذا المستدل يلتزم أن الإنسان الواحد علماء قادرون أحياء كيلا ينقض دليله بالإنسان الواحد لجريانه فيه، وهذا الاستدلال ضعيف جداً لجواز قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمجموع من حيث هو مجموع فلا يلزم ما ذكر من المحذور.
If the Necessary Being were a body, then each part of it would have knowledge, power, and life distinct from what belongs to the other part, due to the impossibility of a single accident subsisting in two loci. Thus each of its parts would be independent with respect to each of the attributes of perfection, necessitating a multiplicity of gods. This proponent is committed to the view that a single human has many knowledges, powers, and lives, lest his proof be refuted by the case of a single human, because it would apply to it. This reasoning is very weak because it is permissible for one single attribute to attach to the whole insofar as it is a whole, so the aforementioned problem does not follow.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection. The objection argues: if the Necessary Being (God) were a body, then each part would have distinct attributes (knowledge, power, life), leading to multiple gods. To avoid this, the objector must also claim that a single human has multiple such attributes, which is absurd. The refutation states that the argument is weak because a single attribute can attach to the whole as a whole, so the multiplicity of gods does not follow. The commentator notes that the objection assumes attributes must be tied to parts individually, but this is not necessary.

Key Terms
واجب
جسم
عرض
محل
صفات الكمال
تعدد الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing multiple attributes to God necessarily implies multiple gods or multiplicity in essence.
Correction: Attributes can be unified in the whole, so they do not necessitate multiple deities.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in this passage, why is the first objection weak?
A) Because a single accident cannot subsist in two loci.
B) Because one attribute can attach to the whole as a whole.
C) Because humans do not have multiple attributes.
D) Because the Necessary Being is not a body.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because attributes can be unified in the whole, preserving divine unity without requiring multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that one single attribute can unify the whole, do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge, power, and life are not separate but one reality? How does that belief affect your trust in His complete awareness of your every action?
?
If you were to deny the unity of Allah's attributes as this fallacy proposes, what would that say about your understanding of His perfect simplicity? Are there areas in your life where you act as if Allah's attributes are divided, such as trusting His power but not His mercy?
?
The refutation shows that multiplicity does not follow from unity of attributes. Have you ever felt that your sins weigh so heavily that Allah's mercy cannot cover them all? How does this lesson's assurance of unified, all-encompassing divine attributes challenge that fear today?

The Fourth Proof: Refuting Spatiality from Self-Subsistence
Presents and refutes the proof that self-subsistence implies spatiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that attempts to derive God's spatiality from self-subsistence and the refutation that clarifies the correct definitions of self-subsistent and dependent.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence means independence, not spatiality.

This lesson presents the fourth proof that divides existents into self-subsistent and dependent, with the opponent claiming that self-subsistent is essentially spatial and dependent is spatial by following. Since God is self-subsistent, He would be spatial. The refutation corrects the definitions: self-subsistent merely means independent of a substrate, and dependent means needing a substrate, neither implying spatiality. An alternative formulation by al-Amidi is also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent divides existents and defines self-subsistent as essentially spatial, then concludes God is spatial.
Notice the claim: self-subsistent = essentially spatial.
2
Examine the refutation
The answer denies both definitions. Understand why self-subsistent does not imply spatiality and dependent does not imply spatiality by following.
3
Consider the alternative formulation
Al-Amidi's version argues that God's attributes subsist in Him, and subsistence means spatial by following, so God is spatial originally. The reply redefines subsistence as attributive appropriation (ikhtisas na'it).
This shows the debate about the meaning of qiyam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع الموجود ينقسم إلى قائم بنفسه وقائم بغيره والقائم بنفسه هو المتحيز بالذات والقائم بغيره هو المتحيز تبعا هو ( أي الواجب تعالى (قائم بنفسه فيكون متحيزا بذاته والجواب منع التفسيرين ( فإن القائم بنفسه هو المستغنى عن محل يقومه وليس يلزم من هذا كونه متحيز بذاته والقائم بغيره هو المحتاج إلى ذلك المحل ولا يلزم منه كونه متحيزاً تبعاً. وقد يقال في تقريره) أي تقرير الوجه الرابع ( أجمعنا على ( أن له تعالى صفات قائمة بذاته ومعنى القيام هو ( التحيز تبعا ) ، فيكون هو متحيزاً أصالة ويجاب بأن القيام هو الاختصاص الناعت كما مر ) .
The fourth: Existent is divided into self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) and dependent (qa'im bi-ghayrihi). Self-subsistent is essentially spatial; dependent is spatial by following. The Necessary, exalted, is self-subsistent, so He is essentially spatial. The answer: denial of both definitions. For self-subsistent is independent of a substrate that sustains it, and this does not necessitate it being essentially spatial. Dependent is in need of that substrate, and this does not necessitate it being spatial by following. It may be said in its formulation (i.e., the fourth face): We agree that He, exalted, has attributes subsisting in His essence, and the meaning of subsisting (qiyam) is spatial by following, so He is spatial originally. And it is answered: subsisting is attributive appropriation (ikhtisas na'it) as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This is the fourth proof. It divides existents into self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) and dependent (qa'im bi-ghayrihi). The opponent claims that self-subsistent is essentially spatial, and dependent is spatial by following. Since God is self-subsistent, He would be essentially spatial. The answer rejects both definitions: self-subsistent merely means independent of a substrate, not that it is spatial; dependent means need of a substrate, not that it becomes spatial by following. An alternative formulation by al-Amidi is mentioned: since God has attributes, their qiyam (subsistence) is interpretated as spatial by following, so He is spatial originally. The reply is that qiyam means attributive appropriation (ikhtisas na'it), not spatiality.

Key Terms
قائم بنفسه
قائم بغيره
متحيز بالذات
متحيز تبعاً
الاختصاص الناعت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term qa'im bi-nafsihi is often misunderstood as implying spatial extension or being a body.
Correction: The passage clarifies that qa'im bi-nafsihi merely means independent of a substrate that sustains it, not that it occupies space.

Checkpoint
What is the correct definition of 'qa'im bi-nafsihi' according to the answer?
A) That which is spatial by essence.
B) That which is independent of a substrate.
C) That which depends on a substrate.
D) That which is spatial by following.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof fails because self-subsistence does not entail spatiality; it only means independence from a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'independent' (qaim bi-nafsihi), do you unconsciously imagine Him as occupying a vast space? What created bias might you be projecting onto your conception of the Creator? Commit to mentally stripping away all spatial imagery from your understanding of God's self-subsistence starting today.
?
The opponent assumes that having attributes (like knowledge) requires spatial location. Do you ever treat God's attributes as if they were physical qualities? How can you train yourself to think of them as pure meanings without location? Write down one practical step to avoid this mental slip.
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat God as if He needs a place, like when you feel closer to Him in a mosque? Recognize that He is beyond all places. Will you correct yourself every time you catch yourself limiting Him by place?

Alternate Formulation of the Fourth Argument and Its Refutation
An alternate version of the fourth argument using the notion of attributes and subsisting by another, and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternate formulation of the fourth argument that derives spatial occupation from divine attributes, and its refutation using the concept of 'descriptive relation'.

Core Idea
Subsistence (qiyām) does not imply spatial occupation; it means a descriptive relation.

This passage presents a sharper version of the earlier fourth argument, this time focusing on the meaning of 'subsistence' (qiyām). A proponent might claim that if God has attributes subsisting in His essence, then that subsistence must mean derivative occupation of space, forcing God Himself to occupy space. The refutation clarifies the technical definition of qiyām, already established earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is very concise, containing both the alternate formulation and its refutation.
2
Identify the premise
The argument begins by stating that we are agreed that God has attributes subsisting in His essence. This is a shared premise.
3
Understand the argument's core
The proponent then defines 'subsistence' (qiyām) as 'derivative occupation of space'. If attributes subsist in God in that sense, then God Himself must originally occupy space.
4
Examine the refutation
The answer redefines 'subsistence' as 'descriptive relation' (ikhtiṣāṣ nā'it), not spatial occupation. This was already established in the refutation of the original fourth argument.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalām.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this refutation is effective: by clarifying the meaning of a key term, it blocks the conclusion. The argument relied on an equivocation of 'qiyām'.

وقد يقال في تقريره) أي تقرير الوجه الرابع (أجمعنا على ( أن له تعالى صفات قائمة بذاته ومعنى القيام هو ( التحيز تبعا ) ، فيكون هو متحيزاً أصالة ويجاب بأن القيام هو الاختصاص الناعت كما مر ).
And it might be said in its formulation (i.e., the formulation of the fourth aspect): We are agreed that the Exalted has attributes subsisting in His essence, and the meaning of 'subsistence' (al-qiyām) is 'derivative occupation of space' (al-taḥayyuz taba'an); therefore He would be originally occupying space. And it is answered that 'subsistence' (al-qiyām) is the descriptive relation (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nā'it), as has passed.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents an alternate formulation of the fourth argument, often attributed to al-Amidi. It argues that since God has attributes subsisting in His essence, and 'subsistence' (qiyām) is taken to mean 'derivatively occupying space' (taḥayyuz taba'an), then God Himself would be originally occupying space (mutahayyiz aṣāla). The answer is that 'subsistence' here means 'descriptive relation' (ikhtiṣāṣ nā'it), i.e., the relation by which the attribute describes the essence, not spatial occupation. This was the earlier refutation to the original fourth argument.

Misconception: The term 'subsistence' (qiyām) necessarily implies spatial subsistence.
Correction: In this context, 'subsistence' (qiyām) means a descriptive relation (ikhtiṣāṣ nā'it), not spatial occupation.

Key Takeaway
The term qiyām in the context of divine attributes refers to a descriptive relation, not spatial occupation, so the argument that God must occupy space fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you unconsciously imagine them as 'things' that exist in a place, like physical qualities? How does realizing that 'subsistence' means a descriptive relation—not a spatial one—change the way you relate to His attributes?

Essential Precedence and the Meaning of Qidam and Baqa'
Author explains God's precedence is essential and defines qidam and baqa' non-temporally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and temporal precedence and the correct meanings of qidam and baqa' as non-temporal attributes.

Core Idea
God's precedence is not temporal and His eternity is not time-bound.

In this passage, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding: God's precedence over the world is not like one event coming before another in time. He then defines two key attributes—qidam (pre-eternity) and baqa' (subsistence)—to show that they are not temporal concepts but are about the impossibility of non-existence and relationship with time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the glosses; they are secondary clarifications.
2
Identify the three main claims
The passage states three things: (1) God's precedence is essential, not temporal. (2) Baqa' is not existence in two times but impossibility of non-existence and concomitance with times. (3) Qidam is not being before every time.
Write these down in your own words.
3
Reflect on why these definitions matter
Ask yourself: why do these definitions protect God from being temporal? How would the wrong definitions lead to saying God is in time?
This connects to the later defense of the eternal Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه على ما يتضمنه هذا الأصل الذي مهدناه آنفاً ) يعلم مما ذكرنا أنا سواء قلنا العالم حادث بالحدوث الزماني ) كما هو رأينا، أو كما الذاتي) هو رأي الحكيم ( فتقدم الباري سبحانه عليه ) لكونه موجداً إياه (ليس تقدماً زمانياً وإلا لزم كونه تعالى واقعاً في الزمان، بل هو تقدم ذاتي عندهم وقسم
الموجود القائم بنفسه وبمعنى الذات والحقيقة إصطلاح شائع بين الحكماء وعليه قول محمد بن كرام أن الله تعالى إحدى الذوات إحدى الجوهر.
قوله : (كما يجر إلى الجهة (والمكان لفظ يجر يدل على أنهم لا يصرحون بالجهة والمكان، وقد سبق تصريحهم بهما وكأن مراده أن هذا الجر محقق ثابت باعتبار قولهم بالجسمية سواء صرحوا بهما أم لا ويجوز أن يكون إيراد الجر للمشاكلة .
قوله : ( أو الذاتي لفظة أو بمعنى الواو والأفسد المعنى إذ التسوية إما هي بين الأمرين لا
بين أحدهما وفي مثله توجيه آخر ذكرناه في بحث الحمد من حواشينا على المطول . قوله : ( وإلا لزم كونه تعالى واقعاً في الزمان ظاهره مناقض لما فهم من كلام الشارح في مباحث الزمان من أن الثابت إذا نسب إى المتغير بالتقدم الزماني لم يحتج المتقدم إلى الزمان والظاهر التقدم الزماني عند الفلاسفة تقدم لا يجامع فيه القبل البعد ويكفي فيه مقارنة المتقدم لزمان ما لا يقارنه المتأخر، اللهم إلا أن يقال ما ذكره الشارح هاهنا متابعة للمنصف وليس بمختاره كيف وتقدم الباري عز إسمه على الحوادث لا بد أن يكون زمانياً عند الفلاسفة تقدم كما أن تقدم الأب على الابن كذلك عندهم أيضاً، إذ قد صرحوا بوجود اجتماع المتقدم والمتأخر في غير التقدم الزماني، وبذلك استدلوا على سرمدية الزمان وبهذا يعلم أن قوله بل هو تقدم ذاتي عندهم إنما يستقيم بالنسبة إلى القديم من أجزاء العالم.
و يعلم أيضاً أن بقاءه ليس عبارة عن وجوده في زمانين ) وإلا كان تعالى زمانياً، بل هو عبارة امتناع عدمه ومقارنته مع الأزمنة ( ولا القدم عبارة عن أن يكون قبل كل زمان زمان)، وإلا لم يتصف به الباري تعالى ( وأنه ( أي ما ذكرناه من أنه تعالى ليس زمانياً يبسط العذر في ورود ما ورد من الكلام الأزلي بصيغة الماضي ولو في الأمور (المستقبلة) الواقعة فيما لا يزال كقوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحاً [نوح: ١]، وذلك لأنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لا بحسب ذاته ولا بحسب صفاته كان نسبة كلامه الأزلي إلى جميع الأزمنة على السوية إلا أن حكمته تعالى اقتضت التعبير عن بعض الأمور بصيغة الماضي وعن بعضها بصيغته المستقبل فسقط ما تمسك به المعتزلة في حدوث القرآن من أنه لو كان قديماً لزم الكذب في أمثال ما ذكر؛ فإن الإرسال لم يكن واقعاً قبل الأزل، ( وهاهنا أسرار أخر لا أبوح بها ثقة بفطنتك ) ، منها إذا قلنا كان الله موجوداً في الأزل وسيكون موجوداً في الأبد وهو موجود الآن لم نرد به أن وجوده واقع في تلك الأزمنة، بل أردنا أنه مقارن معها من غير أن يتعلق بها ( كتعلق ،الزمانيات ومنها أنه لو ثبت وجود مجردات عقلية لم تكن أيضاً زمانية، ومنها أنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لم يكن بالقياس إليه ماض وحال ومستقبل فلا يلزم من علمه بالتغيرات تغير في علمه إنما يلزم ذلك إذا دخل فيه الزمان .
قوله : ( عن وجوده في زمانين) أي عن وجوده الواقع فيهما بأن يكون أمراً تدريجياً منطبقاً
على الزمان، كما هو المتبادر من لفظة في بالنظر إلى الاستعمال الشائع بخلاف المقارنة . قوله : ( كان نسبة كلامه الأزلي ) سيجيء في بحث الكلام ما يفيد زيادة بصيرة. :قوله منها إذا قلنا قيل خلاصة هذا السر قد أبيح لها فيما سبق فالذي لم يبح به هو الثاني والثالث .
A note on what this principle that we laid down earlier includes. It is known from what we mentioned that whether we say the world is originated by temporal origination (as is our view) or by essential origination (which is the view of the philosopher), the precedence of the Creator, glorified, over it, because He is its originator, is not temporal precedence, for that would necessitate that He, Exalted, occurs in time, but rather it is essential precedence according to them. And the division of existence into self-subsistent and meaning of essence and reality is a common terminology among the philosophers, and upon it is the saying of Muhammad ibn Karram that God is one of the essences, one of the substances. His saying: (as it leads to direction) (and place) - the word 'leads' indicates that they do not explicitly state direction and place, and their explicit statement of them has preceded. It is as if his intent is that this leading is established and fixed by virtue of their saying of corporeality, whether they state them or not, and it is possible that the use of 'leading' is for rhetorical similarity. His saying: (or essential) - the word 'or' is in the meaning of 'and'; the more correct meaning is that comparison is between the two matters, not between one of them. And in its like there is another explanation we mentioned in the section on praise from our glosses on al-Mutawwal. His saying: (for then He would occur in time) - its apparent meaning contradicts what is understood from the commentator's words in the investigations of time, that if something fixed is related to something changing by temporal precedence, the prior does not need time. And the apparent meaning of temporal precedence according to the philosophers is a precedence in which the before and after do not exist together, and it suffices that the prior is concomitant with a time that the posterior is not concomitant with. However, it could be said that what the commentator mentions here is in following the author and not his own choice. How? The precedence of the Blessed God over events must be temporal according to the philosophers, just as the precedence of the father over the son is also temporal according to them. Indeed, they have stated the existence of combination of the prior and posterior in non-temporal precedence, and by that they argue for the eternality of time. And by this it is known that his saying 'rather it is essential precedence according to them' is only correct with respect to the eternal parts of the world. And it is also known that His subsistence (baqa') is not the expression of His existence in two times, for then He would be temporal, but rather it is the expression of the impossibility of His non-existence and His concomitance with times. And pre-eternity (qidam) is not the expression of being before every time, for then the Blessed God would not be described by it. And that (i.e., what we mentioned that He, Exalted, is not temporal) excuses what has come of eternal speech in the form of the past tense even in future matters that occur in what will not cease, like His saying: 'Indeed We sent Noah' [71:1]. And that is because if He is not temporal in His essence or His attributes, then the relation of His eternal speech to all times is equal; rather, His wisdom necessitated expressing some matters in the past tense and some in the future tense. So falls what the Mu'tazila clung to regarding the origination of the Quran, that if it were eternal, lying would be necessitated in such examples, for the sending was not real before pre-eternity. And there are other secrets here that I do not disclose, trusting in your intelligence. Among them: if we say 'God was existing in pre-eternity and will be existing in perpetuity and He exists now,' we do not mean that His existence occurs in those times, but rather we mean that He is concomitant with them without being related to them like the relation of temporal things. And among them: if there were established intellectual abstractions (mujarradat 'aqliyya), they would also not be temporal. And among them: if He is not temporal, then relative to Him there is no past, present, and future; so change in His knowledge does not follow from His knowledge of changes; that would only follow if time entered into it. His saying: (about His existence in two times) meaning about His existence actually occurring in both, so that it is a gradual thing corresponding to time, as is the immediate understanding of the word 'in' according to common usage, unlike concomitance. His saying: (the relation of His eternal speech) will come in the discussion of speech what gives increase of insight. His saying: among them if we say - it is said that the summary of this secret has been disclosed previously; what has not been disclosed are the second and third.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a 'tanbih' (note) expanding on God's non-temporality. It first explains that whether we consider the world's origination as temporal (our view) or essential (the philosophers' view), God's precedence over the world is not temporal but essential (taqaddum dhati). The author then includes glosses on the terminology, clarifying that 'essential' here relates to the essence, and that the corporealists' talk of direction is based on their corporealism. A longer gloss addresses an apparent contradiction: the commentator's statement that temporal precedence does not require the prior to be in time might conflict with the idea that God's precedence over events is temporal for philosophers. It reconciles by suggesting the commentator is following the author, not his own view, and that the philosophers' temporal precedence is a different kind that allows coexistence. Then the author states that God's 'baqa'' (subsistence) is not existence in two times but the impossibility of non-existence and concomitance with all times; similarly 'qidam' (eternity) is not being before all times. This leads to the explanation of why the Quran's eternal speech can refer to future events in past tense: God's relation to all times is uniform, and wisdom determines the tense. This refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the Quran must be created because future events cannot be referred to past tense in a pre-eternal speech. Finally, three secrets are mentioned: (1) our statements about God's existence in pre-eternity, eternity, and now denote concomitance, not location; (2) if there are intellectual abstractions, they are also non-temporal; (3) because God is not temporal, His knowledge of changes does not change. Glosses caution that the first secret has been disclosed before, but the second and third are not divulged.

Key Terms

القدم
البقاء
الحدوث الزماني
الحدوث الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that qidam means God existed before all times, as if He were in a temporal sequence.
Correction: The author explicitly denies this: qidam is not 'being before every time' because that would place God in time. Rather, qidam is an attribute that indicates God's existence is not preceded by non-existence, without temporal reference.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'baqa' (subsistence) of God mean?
A) His existence in two times.
B) His existence in all times.
C) The impossibility of His non-existence and His concomitance with times.
D) His being before all times.

Key Takeaway
God's qidam and baqa' are not temporal attributes but denote the impossibility of non-existence and concomitance with all times.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you still, somewhere in your mind, imagine God's 'eternity' as a long unending timeline stretching backwards and forwards? How does this lesson's definition—that qidam is not being before time but the impossibility of non-existence—challenge that mental picture? What must you let go of in your conception of God today?
?
If God's 'baqa'' is not existence in two times but the impossibility of non-existence and His being present with all times, then what does it mean that He is with you every moment? Does this truth make you aware of His closeness, or do you still act as if He is far, bound by time? Repent of any forgetfulness.
?
This passage says that even the philosophers' view of God's precedence over the world is not temporal—it is essential. Have you ever slipped into thinking of God as a 'first cause' in a temporal chain, like a beginner in a sequence? How does that diminish His absolute transcendence? Ask Allah to purify your tawheed from temporal associations.

The Eternal Speech and the Three Secrets
Author defends eternal Quran and reveals three secrets about God's non-temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternality of the Quran is defended against the Mu'tazila objection, and appreciate the three secrets about God's non-temporality.

Core Idea
Because God is not temporal, His eternal speech can use past tense for future events.

The author continues by addressing a key objection raised by the Mu'tazila against the doctrine of the eternal Quran. He then hints at three deep secrets that further illuminate God's transcendence beyond time. This passage shows how the Ash'ari understanding of God's relation to time solves a famous theological problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the defense of eternal speech
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on how the author responds to the Mu'tazila claim.
The verse 'Indeed We sent Noah' is given as an example.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument and the author's response
The Mu'tazila argue that if the Quran is eternal, then past tense statements about future events would be false. The author responds by saying God's eternal speech relates equally to all times; tense is a matter of divine wisdom.
Think about why this response requires God to be non-temporal.
3
Consider the three secrets
The author lists three secrets but says he will not disclose them fully. Try to infer their meaning from the clues given.
The first secret is about the phrase 'God was existing in pre-eternity...'—it means concomitance, not location.

و يعلم أيضاً أن بقاءه ليس عبارة عن وجوده في زمانين ) وإلا كان تعالى زمانياً، بل هو عبارة امتناع عدمه ومقارنته مع الأزمنة ( ولا القدم عبارة عن أن يكون قبل كل زمان زمان)، وإلا لم يتصف به الباري تعالى ( وأنه ( أي ما ذكرناه من أنه تعالى ليس زمانياً يبسط العذر في ورود ما ورد من الكلام الأزلي بصيغة الماضي ولو في الأمور (المستقبلة) الواقعة فيما لا يزال كقوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحاً [نوح: ١]، وذلك لأنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لا بحسب ذاته ولا بحسب صفاته كان نسبة كلامه الأزلي إلى جميع الأزمنة على السوية إلا أن حكمته تعالى اقتضت التعبير عن بعض الأمور بصيغة الماضي وعن بعضها بصيغته المستقبل فسقط ما تمسك به المعتزلة في حدوث القرآن من أنه لو كان قديماً لزم الكذب في أمثال ما ذكر؛ فإن الإرسال لم يكن واقعاً قبل الأزل، ( وهاهنا أسرار أخر لا أبوح بها ثقة بفطنتك ) ، منها إذا قلنا كان الله موجوداً في الأزل وسيكون موجوداً في الأبد وهو موجود الآن لم نرد به أن وجوده واقع في تلك الأزمنة، بل أردنا أنه مقارن معها من غير أن يتعلق بها ( كتعلق ،الزمانيات ومنها أنه لو ثبت وجود مجردات عقلية لم تكن أيضاً زمانية، ومنها أنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لم يكن بالقياس إليه ماض وحال ومستقبل فلا يلزم من علمه بالتغيرات تغير في علمه إنما يلزم ذلك إذا دخل فيه الزمان .
قوله : ( عن وجوده في زمانين) أي عن وجوده الواقع فيهما بأن يكون أمراً تدريجياً منطبقاً
على الزمان، كما هو المتبادر من لفظة في بالنظر إلى الاستعمال الشائع بخلاف المقارنة . قوله : ( كان نسبة كلامه الأزلي ) سيجيء في بحث الكلام ما يفيد زيادة بصيرة. :قوله منها إذا قلنا قيل خلاصة هذا السر قد أبيح لها فيما سبق فالذي لم يبح به هو الثاني والثالث .
A note on what this principle that we laid down earlier includes. It is known from what we mentioned that whether we say the world is originated by temporal origination (as is our view) or by essential origination (which is the view of the philosopher), the precedence of the Creator, glorified, over it, because He is its originator, is not temporal precedence, for that would necessitate that He, Exalted, occurs in time, but rather it is essential precedence according to them. And the division of existence into self-subsistent and meaning of essence and reality is a common terminology among the philosophers, and upon it is the saying of Muhammad ibn Karram that God is one of the essences, one of the substances. His saying: (as it leads to direction) (and place) - the word 'leads' indicates that they do not explicitly state direction and place, and their explicit statement of them has preceded. It is as if his intent is that this leading is established and fixed by virtue of their saying of corporeality, whether they state them or not, and it is possible that the use of 'leading' is for rhetorical similarity. His saying: (or essential) - the word 'or' is in the meaning of 'and'; the more correct meaning is that comparison is between the two matters, not between one of them. And in its like there is another explanation we mentioned in the section on praise from our glosses on al-Mutawwal. His saying: (for then He would occur in time) - its apparent meaning contradicts what is understood from the commentator's words in the investigations of time, that if something fixed is related to something changing by temporal precedence, the prior does not need time. And the apparent meaning of temporal precedence according to the philosophers is a precedence in which the before and after do not exist together, and it suffices that the prior is concomitant with a time that the posterior is not concomitant with. However, it could be said that what the commentator mentions here is in following the author and not his own choice. How? The precedence of the Blessed God over events must be temporal according to the philosophers, just as the precedence of the father over the son is also temporal according to them. Indeed, they have stated the existence of combination of the prior and posterior in non-temporal precedence, and by that they argue for the eternality of time. And by this it is known that his saying 'rather it is essential precedence according to them' is only correct with respect to the eternal parts of the world. And it is also known that His subsistence (baqa') is not the expression of His existence in two times, for then He would be temporal, but rather it is the expression of the impossibility of His non-existence and His concomitance with times. And pre-eternity (qidam) is not the expression of being before every time, for then the Blessed God would not be described by it. And that (i.e., what we mentioned that He, Exalted, is not temporal) excuses what has come of eternal speech in the form of the past tense even in future matters that occur in what will not cease, like His saying: 'Indeed We sent Noah' [71:1]. And that is because if He is not temporal in His essence or His attributes, then the relation of His eternal speech to all times is equal; rather, His wisdom necessitated expressing some matters in the past tense and some in the future tense. So falls what the Mu'tazila clung to regarding the origination of the Quran, that if it were eternal, lying would be necessitated in such examples, for the sending was not real before pre-eternity. And there are other secrets here that I do not disclose, trusting in your intelligence. Among them: if we say 'God was existing in pre-eternity and will be existing in perpetuity and He exists now,' we do not mean that His existence occurs in those times, but rather we mean that He is concomitant with them without being related to them like the relation of temporal things. And among them: if there were established intellectual abstractions (mujarradat 'aqliyya), they would also not be temporal. And among them: if He is not temporal, then relative to Him there is no past, present, and future; so change in His knowledge does not follow from His knowledge of changes; that would only follow if time entered into it. His saying: (about His existence in two times) meaning about His existence actually occurring in both, so that it is a gradual thing corresponding to time, as is the immediate understanding of the word 'in' according to common usage, unlike concomitance. His saying: (the relation of His eternal speech) will come in the discussion of speech what gives increase of insight. His saying: among them if we say - it is said that the summary of this secret has been disclosed previously; what has not been disclosed are the second and third.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a 'tanbih' (note) expanding on God's non-temporality. It first explains that whether we consider the world's origination as temporal (our view) or essential (the philosophers' view), God's precedence over the world is not temporal but essential (taqaddum dhati). The author then includes glosses on the terminology, clarifying that 'essential' here relates to the essence, and that the corporealists' talk of direction is based on their corporealism. A longer gloss addresses an apparent contradiction: the commentator's statement that temporal precedence does not require the prior to be in time might conflict with the idea that God's precedence over events is temporal for philosophers. It reconciles by suggesting the commentator is following the author, not his own view, and that the philosophers' temporal precedence is a different kind that allows coexistence. Then the author states that God's 'baqa'' (subsistence) is not existence in two times but the impossibility of non-existence and concomitance with all times; similarly 'qidam' (eternity) is not being before all times. This leads to the explanation of why the Quran's eternal speech can refer to future events in past tense: God's relation to all times is uniform, and wisdom determines the tense. This refutes the Mu'tazila argument that the Quran must be created because future events cannot be referred to past tense in a pre-eternal speech. Finally, three secrets are mentioned: (1) our statements about God's existence in pre-eternity, eternity, and now denote concomitance, not location; (2) if there are intellectual abstractions, they are also non-temporal; (3) because God is not temporal, His knowledge of changes does not change. Glosses caution that the first secret has been disclosed before, but the second and third are not divulged.

Key Terms
الكلام الأزلي
المعتزلة
الربط الزمني
المقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran's use of past tense for future events means it must be created (as the Mu'tazila claimed).
Correction: The author shows that because God is not temporal, His speech is not bound by temporal sequence, so the tense does not indicate temporal location.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Mu'tazila argument about the eternal Quran fails?
A) Because the Quran is not eternal but created.
B) Because God's eternal speech relates equally to all times, so tense is not a temporal marker.
C) Because the verse 'We sent Noah' is not actually in the past tense.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not understand Arabic grammar.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal speech is not bound by time, so its tense is not a temporal marker but a matter of divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read verses like 'We sent Noah' as past tense, do you truly believe they refer to events that were already decreed in God's eternal knowledge? How does this affect your trust in His promises about the future?
?
If God's speech is not bound by time, how should that change the way you listen to the Quran? Are you listening as if it is a message from an eternal present, not a bygone era?
?
The author suggests that God's knowledge of changes does not change because He is not temporal. Does your understanding of God's unchanging nature challenge you to stop worrying about future unknowns? What step can you take today to live in that trust?

Allah's Timelessness and the Pre-eternity of the Quran
Iji argues Allah is not temporal and refutes Mu'tazilite objection to Quran's pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's timelessness refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the Quran must be created because it uses past tense for future events.

Core Idea
Allah's timelessness makes His speech equally true for all times.

The Mu'tazila argued that if the Quran were pre-eternal, verses like 'Indeed We sent Noah' would be false because the sending hadn't happened yet. Al-Iji begins by clarifying that Allah is not temporal—He does not exist 'in' time. He then shows that this timelessness means His speech relates equally to all times, so using past tense for future events is not false but a matter of divine wisdom. This passage also offers deeper insights into Allah's relation to time and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms like زماني and بقاء.
2
Understand the definitions
Iji defines what divine subsistence (بقاء) and pre-eternity (قدم) are not. They do not imply existence in time or before every time. Understand what they actually mean.
This is key to avoiding anthropomorphism.
3
Apply to the Quranic problem
See how the principle that Allah is not temporal resolves the objection: His speech relates equally to all times, so past tense for future events is not false.
The verse 'We sent Noah' is given as an example.
4
Reflect on the three secrets
The passage ends with three additional points that further clarify Allah's timelessness: our language about Him, immaterial intellects, and divine knowledge.
These reinforce the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : أنه تعالى لا يتحد بغيره

سادس عندنا كتقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على بعضها، و يعلم أيضاً أن بقاءه ليس عبارة عن وجوده في زمانين وإلا كان تعالى زمانياً، بل هو عبارة امتناع عدمه ومقارنته مع الأزمنة ولا القدم عبارة عن أن يكون قبل كل زمان زمان، وإلا لم يتصف به الباري تعالى وأنه أي ما ذكرناه من أنه تعالى ليس زمانياً يبسط العذر في ورود ما ورد من الكلام الأزلي بصيغة الماضي ولو في الأمور المستقبلة الواقعة فيما لا يزال كقوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحاً [نوح: ١]، وذلك لأنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لا بحسب ذاته ولا بحسب صفاته كان نسبة كلامه الأزلي إلى جميع الأزمنة على السوية إلا أن حكمته تعالى اقتضت التعبير عن بعض الأمور بصيغة الماضي وعن بعضها بصيغته المستقبل فسقط ما تمسك به المعتزلة في حدوث القرآن من أنه لو كان قديماً لزم الكذب في أمثال ما ذكر؛ فإن الإرسال لم يكن واقعاً قبل الأزل، وهاهنا أسرار أخر لا أبوح بها ثقة بفطنتك، منها إذا قلنا كان الله موجوداً في الأزل وسيكون موجوداً في الأبد وهو موجود الآن لم نرد به أن وجوده واقع في تلك الأزمنة، بل أردنا أنه مقارن معها من غير أن يتعلق بها كتعلق الزمانيات ومنها أنه لو ثبت وجود مجردات عقلية لم تكن أيضاً زمانية، ومنها أنه إذا لم يكن زمانياً لم يكن بالقياس إليه ماض وحال ومستقبل فلا يلزم من علمه بالتغيرات تغير في علمه إنما يلزم ذلك إذا دخل فيه الزمان .
Observing post two - Fifth topic: That He, exalted, does not unite with another. Sixth with us: like the precedence of some parts of time over others. And it is also known that His subsistence is not an expression of His existence in two times, otherwise He would be temporal. Rather, it is an expression of the impossibility of His non-existence and His correlation with times. And pre-eternity is not an expression of there being before every time a time, otherwise the Creator would not be described by it. And that (i.e., what we mentioned of His not being temporal) provides an excuse regarding the occurrence of what has come of the pre-eternal speech in the past tense, even in future matters that will come to pass, like His statement: 'Indeed We sent Noah.' And that is because when He is not temporal, neither in His essence nor in His attributes, the relation of His pre-eternal speech to all times is equal, except that His wisdom necessitated expressing some matters in the past tense and some in the future tense. So falls what the Mu'tazila clung to concerning the temporal origination of the Quran, that if it were pre-eternal, lying would be necessary in such examples; for the sending was not actual before pre-eternity. And here are other secrets that I do not disclose, trusting in your intelligence: among them, when we say 'God existed in pre-eternity and will exist in eternity and exists now,' we do not intend thereby that His existence occurs in those times, but rather we intend that He is correlated with them without being attached to them like the attachment of temporal things. And among them, that if the existence of immaterial intellects were established, they also would not be temporal. And among them, that when He is not temporal, there is no past, present, and future relative to Him, so from His knowledge of changes, a change in His knowledge does not follow; that only follows if time enters into it.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument that Allah is not temporal (زماني) and that this fact resolves the Mu'tazilite objection against the pre-eternity of the Quran. The author begins by stating a sixth point (سادس) regarding the precedence of parts of time. He then explains that Allah's attribute of 'بقاء' (subsistence) does not mean He exists in two times, which would make Him temporal, but rather it means the impossibility of non-existence and coexistence with all times. Similarly, 'قدم' (pre-eternity) does not mean He existed before every time. The author then applies this to the problem of pre-eternal speech expressed in past tense for future events, such as the verse 'We sent Noah.' Since Allah is not temporal, His speech relates equally to all times; using past tense is a matter of wisdom, not falsity. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that if the Quran were pre-eternal, it would be false to say 'We sent Noah' before the event. The author then mentions three secrets: first, when we say 'God existed in pre-eternity and will exist in eternity and exists now,' we mean coexistence, not actual temporal location; second, if immaterial intellects exist, they are also non-temporal; third, since Allah is not temporal, there is no past, present, or future relative to Him, so His knowledge of changes does not change—only temporal beings experience changing knowledge.

Correction: Allah's timelessness means His speech is equally true for all times; past tense for future events is a matter of wisdom, not falsity, and His knowledge does not change.

Key Takeaway
Allah's timelessness ensures His speech is true for all times, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that the Quran must be created.

?

In what areas of your life do you measure Allah's decrees or actions by your limited understanding of time—perhaps complaining about delayed relief or not understanding why something happened 'in the past'? Are there moments when you fear that Allah's knowledge of your sins or failures is 'in the past' and that He no longer knows you as you are now? How does understanding His timelessness change the way you approach repentance and hope in His mercy? Will you start speaking to Allah as if He hears you now, without any delay?
