Defining Ḥādith and the First Division: Aḥwāl
Al-Jurjani defines ḥādith and begins its three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ḥādith, how it differs from al-mutajaddid, and the first division of temporal entities regarding God's essence.

Core Idea
Not every change in God is a temporal accident; only existing-after-nonexistence is ḥādith.

Al-Jurjani begins the sixth maqṣid by clarifying the key term 'al-ḥādith' (temporal originated thing) and distinguishing it from 'al-mutajaddid' (renewed). This distinction is crucial because the argument only concerns things that come into existence after non-existence, not mere changes in attributes. He then introduces the first of three divisions: the states (aḥwāl), noting the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri that such states can be renewed in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the definitions of ḥādith and mutajaddid, and note the mention of the first division.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the material given.
2
Distinguish between ḥādith and mutajaddid
Identify the defining characteristic of ḥādith: existence after non-existence. Contrast with mutajaddid, which does not involve existence from nothing but rather renewal of an attribute.
This distinction is central to the maqṣid's argument.
3
Understand the first division: aḥwāl
The first division is 'aḥwāl' (states). Note that only Abu al-Husayn al-Basri allowed renewal of states in God, e.g., the state of being all-knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) renewing with new objects of knowledge.
The passage cites al-Āmidī and al-Rāzī for differing Mu'tazila views.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The checkpoint question reinforces the definition of ḥādith. Make sure you do not confuse it with mutajaddid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يقوم بذاته حادث ولا بد أولاً من تحرير محل النزاع ليكون التوارد بالنفي والإثبات من الجانبين على شيء واحد فنقول الحادث هو الموجود بعد العدم، وأما ما لا وجود له وتجدد ويقال له متجدد ولا يقال له حادث وهو ثلاثة أقسام الأول : الأحوال ولم يجوز تجددها في ذاته تعالى إلا أبو الحسين من المعتزلة فإنه قال تتجدد العالمية فيه بتجدد المعلومات، هكذا ذكره الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار وقال الإمام الرازي في نهاية العقول اختلفت المعتزلة في تجويز تجدد الأحوال مثل المدركية والسامعية
The sixth maqṣid: that it is impossible that a temporal originated thing (ḥādith) should subsist in His essence. It is necessary first to define the subject of dispute so that the affirmation and negation from both sides are directed to one thing. So we say: al-ḥādith is that which exists after non-existence. As for that which has no existence and becomes renewed, it is called 'mutajaddid' and is not called 'ḥādith'. And it has three divisions: the first: the states (aḥwāl); and no one allowed their renewal in His essence except Abu al-Husayn [al-Baṣrī] from the Mu'tazila, for he said that the state of being all-knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) is renewed in Him with the renewal of objects of knowledge. Thus al-Āmidī mentioned it in Abkār al-Afkār, and Imām al-Rāzī said in Nihāyat al-ʿUqūl that the Mu'tazila differed regarding the allowance of renewal of states such as being perceiving (al-mudrikīyya) and being hearing (al-sāmiʿiyya)...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth maqṣid, which concerns the impossibility of temporal accidents (ḥawādith) subsisting in God's essence. The author first clarifies the terminology by defining 'al-ḥādith' as something that comes into existence after not existing. He distinguishes it from 'mutajaddid' (renewed), which does not involve existence from non-existence but rather a change in attribute. He then lists three divisions of such things. The first division is 'aḥwāl' (states), and he notes that only Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the Mu'tazila allowed the renewal of states in God, such as the state of being knowing changing with new objects of knowledge. He further cites al-Āmidī and al-Rāzī to show that the Mu'tazila were not unanimous on this. The passage is incomplete but establishes the framework for the argument.

Key Terms
الحادث
المتجدد
الأحوال
العالمية
المدركية
السامعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any change in God's attributes (like knowing new things) counts as a ḥādith.
Correction: The author clarifies that only things that come into existence after non-existence are ḥādith; renewed attributes (mutajaddid) without existence from nothing are different and not subject to the argument.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'al-ḥādith'?
A) That which exists after non-existence
B) A renewed state
C) A mental concept

Key Takeaway
The sixth maqṣid hinges on the precise definition of ḥādith as 'existence after non-existence,' which excludes mere renewed states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's attributes are unchanging, then His mercy, wrath, and love are eternally what they are. Does this comfort you or trouble you? Take a moment: do you secretly desire a God whose reactions change based on your behavior, more like a human? What does that reveal about your understanding of worship?

Definition of Hadith and Its Three Divisions
Al-Iji defines hadith and classifies the renewed into three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'hadith' (originated thing) and the three categories of 'mutajaddad' (renewed) as they apply to God, and why this distinction is foundational for the debate about divine attributes.

Core Idea
What is renewed in God is not the same as what is originated.

Al-Iji begins the sixth aim by clarifying the critical distinction between 'al-hadith' (originated) and 'al-mutajaddad' (renewed). Without this clarification, discussions about divine attributes would be confused. He then enumerates three types of renewed qualities: states, relations, and negations, each with its own ruling regarding God. This passage establishes the precise point of contention for the subsequent debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between hadith and mutajaddad
Read the definition of hadith as 'that which exists after non-existence' and note that mutajaddad has no existence but is a renewal.
This distinction is crucial because the debate focuses on mutajaddad, not hadith, in God.
2
Identify the three categories of mutajaddad
The passage lists states (ahwal), relations (idafat), and negations (salub). Note how each is defined and which scholars permit their renewal in God.
For states, only Abu al-Husayn allows renewal of 'being knowing'.
3
Examine the examples for each category
For states, note the example of 'being knowing' (al-alamiyya) and the dispute among Mu'tazila. For relations, note unanimity. For negations, note the distinction between negations of impossible attributes vs. possible ones.
The example of 'withness' shows how a negation can be renewed.
4
Relate this to the broader debate
Understand that this classification sets the stage for the controversy whether God can be a locus for originated accidents (hawadith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يقوم بذاته حادث ولا بد أولاً من تحرير محل النزاع ليكون التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على شيء واحد فنقول الحادث هو الموجود بعد العدم، وأما ما لا وجود له وتجدد ويقال له متجدد ولا يقال له حادث وهو ثلاثة أقسام الأول : الأحوال ولم يجوز تجددها في ذاته تعالى إلا أبو الحسين من المعتزلة فإنه قال تتجدد العالمية فيه بتجدد المعلومات، هكذا ذكره الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار وقال الإمام الرازي في نهاية العقول اختلفت المعتزلة في تجويز تجدد الأحوال مثل المدركية والسامعية والمبصرية والمزيدية والكارهية وأما أبو الحسين فإنه أثبت تجدد العالميات في ذاته تعالى الثاني : الإضافات ويجوز تجددها اتفاقا الثالث : السلوب فما نسب إلى ما يستحيل اتصاف الباري تعالى به امتنع تجدده كما في قولنا إنه ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض فإن هذه سلوب يمتنع تجددها وإلا جاز فإنه تعالى موجود مع كل حادث ويزول عنه هذا المعية إذا عدم الحادث، فقد تجدد له صفة سلب بعد أن لم تكن.
The Sixth Aim: that the Exalted is impossible for an originated thing to subsist in His essence. It is necessary first to delineate the point of contention so that the discourse of negation and affirmation bears upon one thing. So we say: the originated thing (al-ḥādith) is that which exists after non-existence. As for that which has no existence but becomes renewed (yatajaddad) — it is called 'renewed' (mutajaddad) and is not called 'originated'. It has three divisions: First: states (al-aḥwāl). Their renewal in His essence is not deemed permissible except by Abū al-Ḥusayn of the Muʿtazila, for he said that the state of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) is renewed in Him with the renewal of known things. Such is mentioned by al-Āmidī in Abkār al-Afkār. And al-Imām al-Rāzī said in Nihāyat al-ʿUqūl: the Muʿtazila differed about permitting the renewal of states such as being perceiving (al-mudrikkiyya), being hearing (al-sāmiʿiyya), being seeing (al-mubṣiriyya), being willing (al-murīdiyya), and being disliking (al-kārihiyya). But Abū al-Ḥusayn affirmed the renewal of the state of being knowing in His essence. Second: relations (al-iḍāfāt), and their renewal is permitted unanimously. Third: negations (al-salūb). What is attributed to what is impossible for the Exalted to be described with — its renewal is impossible, as in our saying that He is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident; for these are negations whose renewal is impossible. Otherwise (i.e., if the negation is not of that kind), its renewal is possible: for the Exalted exists with every originated thing, and this 'with-ness' departs from Him when the originated thing ceases, so there has been renewed for Him a negative attribute after it was not.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of the sixth maqsad. It first states the need to precisely define the point of contention (taḥrīr maḥall al-nizāʿ). The definition of 'al-ḥādith' (originated thing) is given as 'what exists after non-existence.' This is contrasted with 'al-mutajaddad' (renewed), which has no existence but is a renewal of a state, relation, or negation. The author then divides the renewed into three categories: (1) states (aḥwāl), (2) relations (iḍāfāt), and (3) negations (salūb). For states, the only one who permits their renewal in God's essence is Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī of the Muʿtazila, specifically the state of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya). The passage cites al-Āmidī and al-Rāzī, mentioning other possible states like perceiving, hearing, seeing, willing, and disliking. For relations, all agree they can be renewed (e.g., being the creator of a new thing). For negations, if the negation refers to something absolutely impossible for God (like being a body), it cannot be renewed; if it is a negation of something that can change (like the non-existence of a particular creature), it can be renewed (e.g., God's 'withness' to a creature is a relation that begins and ends). This detailed classification sets the stage for the debate about whether God can be a locus for accidents (ḥawādith).

Key Terms

متجدد
أحوال
إضافات
سلوب
العالمية
المدركية
السامعية
المبصرية

الكارهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mutajaddad' is synonymous with 'ḥādith' and both refer to temporally originated things.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes: 'ḥādith' is a thing that exists after non‑existence, while 'mutajaddad' is a renewal of a state, relation, or negation without independent existence.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three divisions of 'mutajaddad' (renewed) as mentioned in the passage?
A) al-aḥwāl (states)
B) al-iḍāfāt (relations)
C) al-ajsām (bodies)
D) al-salūb (negations)

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'hadith' and 'mutajaddad' and the threefold classification of the renewed are essential for understanding the debate on divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as 'All-Knowing,' do you ever slip into thinking His knowledge grows as events occur? This lesson forces you to confront that assumption: is your heart truly settled on His eternal knowledge, or do you subtly imagine His knowledge being 'renewed' like human learning? What would it take for you to feel the weight of His omniscience in your daily life?
?
Have you ever considered that your own existence brings about a change in God's relation to you — He is your Creator only after He creates you? How does it feel to know that Allah enters into a new relation with you at every moment, without changing? Are you living in awareness of that ever-renewed relationship?
?
The distinction between 'hadith' and 'mutajaddad' protects God from anthropomorphism. But have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah's anger or mercy as emotional fluctuations? This lesson demands you check your heart: do you truly believe His attributes are unchanging, or do you treat them as if they are like human emotions? What is one assumption about God's attributes you need to repent of today?

Majority and Magian Views on Divine Locus
Al-Iji presents the majority and Magian positions in contrast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the majority of theologians and the Magians regarding whether God can be a locus for originated things.

Core Idea
The majority forbid God being a locus for accidents; the Magians allow perfection-accidents.

This lesson examines two opposing views on whether originated events or accidents can subsist in God. The majority of religious communities, including Muslims, Jews, and Christians, assert that God cannot be a subject for accidents. In contrast, the Magians (Zoroastrians) claim that every originated thing that is a perfection attribute can subsist in God. Understanding these positions clarifies the boundaries of divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the first two sentences of the source paragraph. Notice the contrast between the majority view and the Magian view.
The Arabic phrase 'محل الحوادث' is key.
2
Understand the majority position
The majority (al-jumhūr) from people of religions and others absolutely forbid God being a locus for accidents. Reflect on why this is the mainstream Islamic view.
This aligns with divine simplicity and transcendence.
3
Examine the Magian view
The Magians allow every originated thing that is a perfection attribute (صفات الكمال) to subsist in God. Contrast this with the majority's absolute denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد اختلف في كونه تعالى محل الحوادث فمنعه الجمهور من أرباب الملل وغيرهم وقال المجوس كل حادث هو من صفات الكمال قائم به وقال الكرامية يجوز يقوم به الحادث لا مطلقاً بل كل حادث يحتاج الباري تعالى إليه في الإيجاد ثم اختلفوا في ذلك الحادث فقيل هي الإرادة، وقيل هو قوله كن فخلق هذا القول أو الإرادة في ذاته تعالى مستد إلى القدرة القديمة، وأما خلق باقي المخلوقات فمستند إلى الإرادة أو القول على اختلاف المذهبين واتفقوا على أنه يسمى حادثا وما لايقوم بذاته من الحوادث يسمى محدثا لا حادثاً فرقاً بينهما.
So there is disagreement concerning His being a locus for originated things (maḥall al-ḥawādith). The majority (al-jumhūr) from the people of the religions and others forbid it. The Magians (al-Majūs) said: Every originated thing which is a perfection attribute subsists in Him. The Karramiyya said: It is permissible for the originated thing to subsist in Him, not absolutely, but every originated thing that the Exalted needs for bringing into existence. Then they disagreed about that originated thing: it was said it is the will (al-irāda), and it was said it is His word 'Be' (kun). So this word or will is created in His essence, being dependent on the eternal power. As for the creation of the rest of the creatures, it is dependent on the will or the word according to the difference of the two schools. And they agreed that it is called 'originated' (ḥādith), while what does not subsist in itself from among originated things is called 'made to originate' (muḥdath), not 'originated', in order to distinguish between them.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage summarizes the doctrinal positions on whether God can be the locus of originated things (al-ḥawādith). Three main views are presented: (1) The majority (al-jumhūr) of the people of the religions and others absolutely forbid it; God cannot be a subject for accidents. (2) The Magians (Majūs) hold that every originated thing that is a perfection attribute can subsist in God; they do not restrict it. (3) The Karramiyya permit subsistency of originated things in God, but only those which God needs for creation—specifically the will or the creative command 'kun'. They disagree among themselves whether the originated thing subsisting in God is the will or the word. In both cases, this originated thing (will or word) is created in God's essence and depends on the eternal power for its existence, and through it other creatures are brought into being. An important terminological distinction is made: that which subsists in itself (i.e., the will or word in God) is called 'ḥādith' (originated), while other accidents that do not subsist in themselves are called 'muḥdath' (made to originate). This distinction is critical for the Karramiyya view to avoid saying that God is the locus for all accidents.

Key Terms
محل الحوادث
الجمهور
المجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is not a locus for accidents, He cannot have attributes.
Correction: The majority affirm attributes without them being accidents in a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who forbids God from being a locus for originated things?
A) The Magians
B) The majority of people of religions and others
C) The Karramiyya
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The majority of religious communities affirm God's transcendence by denying that He is a locus for accidents, while the Magians allow perfection-attributes as accidents in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your understanding of God might be subtly shaped by cultural or inherited beliefs, rather than the pure transcendence affirmed by the majority? How does this passage challenge you to examine the sources of your own beliefs about God?
?
The Magians allowed perfection-attributes as accidents in God. Do you find yourself sometimes thinking of God's attributes as separate 'things' that He 'acquires'? What would it mean for you to truly affirm that God is not a locus for any originated thing?
?
If God cannot be a locus for accidents, then His attributes are not accidental but essential. Does your daily worship reflect a God who is absolutely perfect and unchanging, or do you sometimes imagine Him as subject to change like created beings? Commit to one practical step to align your heart more with the majority's conviction.

The Karramiyya View on Accidents in God and Terminological Distinction
Al-Iji explains the Karramiyya view and their terminological nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya position on which originated things can subsist in God and the distinction between ḥādith and muḥdath.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya allow only the will or command as originated in God, and they distinguish 'originated' from 'made to originate'.

This lesson dives into the nuanced position of the Karramiyya school. They permit certain originated things to subsist in God, specifically those needed for creation: the will or the creative command 'Be'. They also introduce a critical terminological distinction between an originated thing that subsists in itself (ḥādith) and one that does not (muḥdath), allowing them to argue that God is not a locus for all accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karramiyya position
Read the portion of the Arabic passage dealing with the Karramiyya. Note that they restrict accidents in God to those necessary for creation.
The phrase 'كل حادث يحتاج الباري تعالى إليه في الإيجاد' is key.
2
Understand the internal disagreement
The Karramiyya disagree whether the specific accident is the will (al-irāda) or the word 'Be' (kun). Both are created in God's essence and depend on His eternal power.
This shows they are not monolithic.
3
Learn the terminological distinction
The passage concludes with an agreed-upon distinction: that which subsists in itself (the will or word) is called ḥādith (originated), while other accidents that do not subsist in themselves are called muḥdath (made to originate). This nuanced terminology avoids claiming God is the locus for all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد اختلف في كونه تعالى محل الحوادث فمنعه الجمهور من أرباب الملل وغيرهم وقال المجوس كل حادث هو من صفات الكمال قائم به وقال الكرامية يجوز يقوم به الحادث لا مطلقاً بل كل حادث يحتاج الباري تعالى إليه في الإيجاد ثم اختلفوا في ذلك الحادث فقيل هي الإرادة، وقيل هو قوله كن فخلق هذا القول أو الإرادة في ذاته تعالى مستد إلى القدرة القديمة، وأما خلق باقي المخلوقات فمستند إلى الإرادة أو القول على اختلاف المذهبين واتفقوا على أنه يسمى حادثا وما لايقوم بذاته من الحوادث يسمى محدثا لا حادثاً فرقاً بينهما.
So there is disagreement concerning His being a locus for originated things (maḥall al-ḥawādith). The majority (al-jumhūr) from the people of the religions and others forbid it. The Magians (al-Majūs) said: Every originated thing which is a perfection attribute subsists in Him. Commentary
This passage summarizes the doctrinal positions on whether God can be the locus of originated things (al-ḥawādith). Three main views are presented: (1) The majority (al-jumhūr) of the people of the religions and others absolutely forbid it; God cannot be a subject for accidents. (2) The Magians (Majūs) hold that every originated thing that is a perfection attribute can subsist in God; they do not restrict it. First Proof: God Cannot Be a Locus for Accidents
Al-Iji gives the first proof: if a hadith could subsist in God, it would be possible eternally, which is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam proof that God cannot be a locus for originated things (al-ḥawādith), and the distinction between a regular originated thing and the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddad).

Core Idea
If God could contain originated things, they would be eternal—a contradiction.

Al-Iji now presents the first of three logical arguments against the possibility of God being a locus for accidents. The argument is simple but profound: if God could have an accident inhere in Him, then that accident could have inhered in Him from eternity—but that would make it eternal, which is impossible. A clever objection about the 'renewed' is answered by clarifying the nature of the renewed itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If ... then ...' and how each part is defended.
Focus on the implication and the falsity of the consequent.
2
Understand the implication
The capacity (qābiliyya) for subsistence is eternal and does not depend on the actual occurrence of the accident. If subsistence is possible at any time, it must be possible eternally. Why?
This is the key step linking temporal possibility to eternal possibility.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objection says the proof also applies to the renewed (mutajaddad) like will or speech. The answer denies that the consequent would be false for the renewed, because the renewed does not admit eternity by its nature.
Distinguish between a regular originated thing (ḥādith) and a renewed thing (mutajaddad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا في إثبات هذا المدعي وجوه ثلاثة. الأول : لو جاز قيام الحادث بذاته لجاز أزلاً واللازم باطل أما الملازمة فلأن القابلية لا تتوقف على حدوث الحادث بل هي حاصلة أزلاً فإذا جاز قيامه جاز في الأزل، وأما بطلان اللازم فلأنه يلزم قدم الحادث وهو محال. فإن قلت: هذا الدليل ينتقض بالمتجدد فإنه لو جاز قيامه بذاته تعالى لجاز أزلاً إلخ مع أنهم جوزوا ذلك. قلت: بطلان اللازم هاهنا ممنوع إذا المحال هو جواز قيام الحادث الأزلي بذاته، ولا يلزم من جواز قيام المتجدد بذاته جواز قيامه أزلاً إذ المتجدد لا يقبل الأزلية لذاته.
We have three proofs for establishing this claim. The first: If the subsistency of an originated thing (al-ḥādith) in His essence were permissible, then it would be permissible eternally, and the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is because the capacity (al-qābiliyya) is not dependent on the occurrence of the originated thing but is obtained eternally, so if its subsistency is permissible, it is permissible in eternity. As for the falsity of the consequent, it is because it necessitates the pre-eternity of the originated thing, which is impossible. If you say: This proof is refuted by the renewed (al-mutajaddad), for if its subsistency in the Exalted's essence were permissible, it would be permissible eternally, etc., even though they (the Karramiyya) permitted that. I say: The falsity of the consequent here is denied, for the impossible is the permissibility of the subsistency of an eternal originated thing in His essence, and it does not follow from the permissibility of the subsistency of the renewed in His essence the permissibility of its subsistency eternally, since the renewed, by its very nature, does not admit eternity.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs against the claim that God can be a locus for originated things (ḥawādith). The proof is structured as a conditional argument. The proponent (al-Muṣannif) states: If it were permissible for a ḥādith (originated thing) to subsist in God's essence, then it would be permissible for it to subsist in Him from eternity. The implication holds because the capacity (qābiliyya) for that subsistency is present eternally, not dependent on the actual occurrence of the ḥādith. The consequent is false because it would entail the pre-eternity (qidam) of the ḥādith, which is impossible (since a ḥādith is by definition temporally originated). An objection is raised by an implied interlocutor: This proof would also apply to the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddad), such as the will or word according to the Karramiyya, because if it were permissible for a renewed to subsist in God, then it would also be permissible eternally, yet they permit that. The answer (found in the gloss) is that the consequent (the permissibility of eternal subsistency) is not necessarily false in that case, because the impossibility is specifically of a pre-eternal originated thing. The renewed (mutajaddad) by its nature does not admit eternity, so its subsistency is not possible eternally, and the proof does not apply to it. Thus the proof is valid against the ḥādith proper, not against the mutajaddad.

Key Terms
قابلية
حادث
أزل
قدم الحادث
متجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof applies equally to the renewed (al-mutajaddad), making it invalid because the Karramiyya allow it.
Correction: The renewed, by its nature, does not admit eternity, so the proof does not apply to it. The impossibility is only for an originated thing that could be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the first proof that links the permissibility of a ḥādith subsisting in God to its being possible in eternity?
A) The capacity for subsistency is eternal
B) The ḥādith is by definition eternal
C) The Karramiyya permit it
D) The ḥādith is a perfection attribute

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that God cannot be a locus for any originated thing because that would entail its pre-eternity, which is contradictory; the objection regarding the 'renewed' fails because the renewed by nature does not admit eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described Allah as 'becoming' angry or merciful, as if His attributes change over time? How does this proof—that no originated thing can subsist in Him—force you to retrain your tongue and heart to speak of His eternal, unchanging attributes?
?
If Allah's capacity for accidents is eternal, yet He never actualizes them, what does that tell you about His absolute perfection and self-sufficiency? How does this truth reshape the way you rely on Him, knowing He is not subject to any need or change?
?
When you face trials, do you subconsciously believe that Allah's will or mercy 'renews' itself in reaction to your situation? This lesson shows that even the 'renewed' cannot truly subsist in Him. Will you commit to teaching your soul that Allah's eternal attributes are the same yesterday, today, and forever—and find peace in that?

10 min
The Impossibility of Accidental Receptivity in God's Essence
Proof that qabiliyya cannot be accidental to God's essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why receptivity (qabiliyya) in God's essence cannot be accidental, as it would lead to a change in the essence's possibility and an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Receptivity cannot be accidental; otherwise God's essence would change from impossible to possible.

This lesson presents the first proof from the text, showing that if God's essence were receptive to accidents, that receptivity cannot be a mere accident. The argument uses the concepts of essential impossibility and possibility to show the absurdity of such a scenario.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the terms 'lazim' and 'arid'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two consequences of accidental receptivity
The author lists two problems if receptivity were accidental: (1) the essence would change from impossible to possible, and (2) an infinite regress of receptivities would occur. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the regress argument
The regress argument is that if receptivity is accidental, it itself would need a receptivity, leading to an infinite chain. This is why the author concludes that receptivity must be necessary.
This is a key argument in kalam against accidents in God.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يقوم بذاته من لوازم الذات وإلا لزم الانقلاب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي )، فإن القابلية إذ لم تكن لازمة بل عارضة كان الذات في حد نفسها قبل عروض القابلية لها ممتنعة القبول للحادث المقبول، وبعد عروضها ممكنة القبول له فيلزم ذلك الانقلاب ولو فرض زوال القابلية بعد ثبوتها لزم الانقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي ولما لم يكن لنا خاجة إلى هذا لم نتعرض له ( وأيضاً فتكون القابلية) على تقدير عدم لزومها وثبوتها للذات أزلاً ) طارئة على الذات فتكون صفة زائدة عليها عارضة لها، وحينئذ فلا بد للذات قابلية لهذه القابلية فإن كانت قابلية القابلية لازمة للذات فذاك وإلا فهناك قابلية ثالثة ويلزم التسلسل في القابليات المحصورة بين أزلية الحادث الموجود لا جواز أزلية غير الموجود ألا يرى أن اعدام الحوادث لها لاستحال أزلية المتجدد فيتم النقض فليتأمل .

The Second Observatory - The Sixth Intention: That the Exalted is impossible for accidents to subsist in His essence. From the necessities of the Essence, otherwise the change from essential impossibility to essential possibility would be necessary. For if the receptivity (al-qabiliyya) were not necessary but accidental, the essence in itself before the occurrence of receptivity would be impossible of acceptance of the occurring accident, and after its occurrence possible of acceptance of it, so that change would be necessary. And if the removal of receptivity after its establishment were assumed, the change from essential possibility to essential impossibility would be necessary. And since we have no need for this, we did not address it. And also, the receptivity, on the assumption of its non-necessity and its eternal establishment for the essence, would be accidental to the essence, thus becoming an additional attribute ('arida) upon it. Then the essence must have a receptivity for this receptivity. If the receptivity of the receptivity is necessary for the essence, then that is it; otherwise there is a third receptivity and there results an infinite regress (tasalsul) in the receptivities limited between the eternity of the occurring existent and the non-permissibility of the eternity of the non-existent. Do you not see that the nonexistences of accidents have it because the eternity of the renewing is impossible, thus the rebuttal is completed. So reflect. He said: (Otherwise the change would be necessary). If you say, what is meant by necessity here is the impossibility of separation (imtina' al-infikak) as he will explicitly state, and from its negation the actual change does not follow as the context of his speech requires, because it is possible that the receptivity could be permissibly removable but eternally established for the essence. I say: Their principle is based on that the attribution of the essence with receptivity must have a cause that necessitates it completely. And if it is not the essence itself, otherwise separation would not be permissible, so it is the essence by His will, since it is not permissible for the cause of the essence's receptivity to be other than it, given that the other is originated, so the origination of the receptivity necessarily follows. And by this is shown the justification of his statement: 'And also the receptivity would be' etc. So reflect. He said: (Thus it would be an additional attribute accidental to it). He said 'accidental' in the place of the descriptive adjective for his word 'additional' to indicate that what is meant by addition in the speech of the author is occurrence (al-'urud). And the usage of 'addition' in this sense is common. From it is the saying of the jurists: 'The addition in the defective sale prevents return.' And then the aspect of the inference of his statement 'Thus it would be an additional attribute' on the assumption of its non-necessity appears, and the suspicion of its weakness is repelled based on the addition being established on the assumption of necessity as well. He said: (And the regress in the receptivities would be necessary). An objection was raised that the regress in them is necessary on the assumption of their necessity for the essence also, so this aspect does not indicate necessity. And it may be answered that what is meant by receptivity is the disposition (al-isti'dad) which does not coexist with the act, and the attribute if it is necessary for the described, its disposition in the mentioned sense is inconceivable. And you are aware that this answer is refuted by the author's statement in explaining the invalidity of the necessary: 'And the soundness of the receptivity eternally entails the soundness of both sides eternally.' For there is no doubt that if receptivity is taken in the sense of the mentioned disposition, its eternity would be incompatible with the eternity of acceptance due to the impossibility of their coexistence. So how does it entail its soundness eternally, even though this inference is from the side of the theologians (mutakallimun)? And their statement about the mentioned disposition is not apparent. So the correct answer is to say that receptivity here is in the sense of the permissibility of attribution (jawaz al-ittisaf) as he explicitly stated. And if the attribute is necessary for the described, the attribution of the described with it is obligatory not possible, so he does not have another receptivity in this sense, contrary to when. The terminator and it is impossible. (And if the receptivity is from the necessities of the essence, its separation from it is impossible, so the receptivity continues with its continuation. The essence is eternal, so is the receptivity. And it (i.e., the eternity of receptivity) entails the permissibility of the attribution of the essence with it, i.e., with the originated, eternally. For there is no meaning of receptivity except the permissibility of attribution to it, i.e., to the accepted. And as for the invalidity of the necessary, it is because the receptivity is a relation requiring a receiver and an accepted. And its soundness eternally entails the soundness of both sides eternally, so the soundness of the existence of the originated eternally follows. This is the opposite. He said: (And as for the invalidity of the necessary, it is because the receptivity is a relation. What is meant by this receptivity is the actual acceptance which is the locus of the occurrence of the accident in Him, the Exalted, not the receptivity which He established as eternal. Rather, the eternal receptivity is the soundness of this receptivity which is in the sense of actual acceptance, as indicated by reflection on the context of the speech. And the clearer in phraseology is to say in explaining the invalidity of the necessary: The permissibility of standing (jawaz al-qiyam) is an expression of the soundness of attribution, and attribution is a relation requiring a described and an attribute. Its soundness eternally entails the soundness of both sides eternally, so the soundness of the existence of the originated eternally follows.)
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a complex philosophical argument from Sharh al-Mawaqif, presenting the first of two proofs that accidents (hawadith) cannot subsist in God's essence. The argument revolves around the concept of 'qabiliyya' (receptivity/potentiality). The author argues that if God's essence were receptive to accidents, this receptivity must either be a necessary (lazim) attribute of the essence or an accidental (arid) one. If it is accidental, then the essence would change from being essentially impossible of receiving accidents to being possible, which is a contradiction. Moreover, if receptivity is accidental, it would require another receptivity to receive it, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The text then addresses objections: one concerning the possibility of a receptivity that is always present but removable, and another arguing that the regress might still occur even if receptivity is necessary. The author refutes these by clarifying that 'qabiliyya' here means the permissibility of attribution, not a disposition, and that if an attribute is necessary, there is no need for a separate receptivity for it. The proof concludes that to avoid regress, the receptivity must be necessary, but then it would entail the possibility of the accident being eternal (qadim), which is impossible. Thus, the original assumption that accidents subsist in God is false.

Key Terms
القابلية
اللزوم
الانقلاب
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'qabiliyya' refers to a physical disposition or potential in the essence.
Correction: In this context, 'qabiliyya' means the logical permissibility of attribution, not a physical capacity.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence if receptivity (qabiliyya) were accidental to God's essence?
A) The essence would be impossible of any attributes.
B) The essence would change from essential impossibility to essential possibility regarding accidents.
C) The essence would become originated.
D) No consequence would occur.

Key Takeaway
If receptivity were accidental, it would change the essence's possibility and lead to an infinite regress, so it must be necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's essence cannot undergo any change, how would that affect your certainty in His forgiveness after repeated sins? Do you sometimes act as if God's mercy 'changes' based on your performance?
?
The argument shows that if any attribute were accidental, it would lead to an infinite chain of dependencies. In your spiritual life, do you rely on anything other than God that creates an endless chain of needs? How does understanding God's absolute independence challenge you to break those chains?
?
When you face a situation that seems to contradict God's promise, do you ever doubt His unchanging nature? What would it mean to live as if His attributes are eternally fixed and never subject to accident?

12 min
Objections and Refutations: The Necessity of Receptivity
Objections and final conclusion of the argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the argument and how the author refutes them, concluding that necessary receptivity entails the impossibility of accidents.

Core Idea
Even necessary receptivity leads to the eternity of accidents, which is impossible.

This lesson continues the argument by addressing objections about the meaning of necessity and the possibility of removable receptivity, and then shows the final consequence that necessary receptivity leads to a contradiction. The author clarifies that 'qabiliyya' means permissibility of attribution, not a disposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and responses
The passage contains several objections. First, an objection about the meaning of 'luzum' (necessity). Then an objection that the regress still occurs even if receptivity is necessary. Read each and see how the author responds.
The author distinguishes between different meanings of 'qabiliyya'.
2
Understand the final conclusion
The argument concludes that if receptivity is necessary, then it is eternal, and this entails the possibility of the accident being eternal, which is impossible since accidents are originated.
This is the proof by contradiction.
3
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the entire argument shows that accidents cannot subsist in God because any receptivity either leads to regress or to the eternity of accidents.

For there is no doubt that if receptivity is taken in the sense of the mentioned disposition, its eternity would be incompatible with the eternity of acceptance due to the impossibility of their coexistence. So how does it entail its soundness eternally, even though this inference is from the side of the theologians (mutakallimun)? And their statement about the mentioned disposition is not apparent. So the correct answer is to say that receptivity here is in the sense of the permissibility of attribution (jawaz al-ittisaf) as he explicitly stated. And if the attribute is necessary for the described, the attribution of the described with it is obligatory not possible, so he does not have another receptivity in this sense, contrary to when. The terminator and it is impossible. (And if the receptivity is from the necessities of the essence, its separation from it is impossible, so the receptivity continues with its continuation. The essence is eternal, so is the receptivity. And it (i.e., the eternity of receptivity) entails the permissibility of the attribution of the essence with it, i.e., with the originated, eternally. For there is no meaning of receptivity except the permissibility of attribution to it, i.e., to the accepted. And as for the invalidity of the necessary, it is because the receptivity is a relation requiring a receiver and an accepted. And its soundness eternally entails the soundness of both sides eternally, so the soundness of the existence of the originated eternally follows. This is the opposite. He said: (And as for the invalidity of the necessary, it is because the receptivity is a relation. What is meant by this receptivity is the actual acceptance which is the locus of the occurrence of the accident in Him, the Exalted, not the receptivity which He established as eternal. Rather, the eternal receptivity is the soundness of this receptivity which is in the sense of actual acceptance, as indicated by reflection on the context of the speech. And the clearer in phraseology is to say in explaining the invalidity of the necessary: The permissibility of standing (jawaz al-qiyam) is an expression of the soundness of attribution, and attribution is a relation requiring a described and an attribute. Its soundness eternally entails the soundness of both sides eternally, so the soundness of the existence of the originated eternally follows.)
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a complex philosophical argument from Sharh al-Mawaqif, presenting the first of two proofs that accidents (hawadith) cannot subsist in God's essence. The argument revolves around the concept of 'qabiliyya' (receptivity/potentiality). The author argues that if God's essence were receptive to accidents, this receptivity must either be a necessary (lazim) attribute of the essence or an accidental (arid) one. If it is accidental, then the essence would change from being essentially impossible of receiving accidents to being possible, which is a contradiction. Moreover, if receptivity is accidental, it would require another receptivity to receive it, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The text then addresses objections: one concerning the possibility of a receptivity that is always present but removable, and another arguing that the regress might still occur even if receptivity is necessary. The author refutes these by clarifying that 'qabiliyya' here means the permissibility of attribution, not a disposition, and that if an attribute is necessary, there is no need for a separate receptivity for it. The proof concludes that to avoid regress, the receptivity must be necessary, but then it would entail the possibility of the accident being eternal (qadim), which is impossible. Thus, the original assumption that accidents subsist in God is false.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الانفكاك
الاستعداد
جواز الاتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if receptivity is necessary, it can still be a disposition (isti'dad) that requires another receptivity.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'qabiliyya' here means permissibility of attribution, not a disposition. A necessary attribute does not require a prior receptivity.

Checkpoint
What is the final conclusion of the argument?
A) Receptivity must be accidental.
B) Receptivity must be necessary, but that leads to the eternity of accidents, which is impossible.
C) Receptivity is not needed.
D) The argument is inconclusive.

Key Takeaway
Necessary receptivity would imply the possibility of an eternal accident, which is impossible; thus accidents cannot subsist in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Having understood that assigning receptive potentiality to God leads to impossible consequences, do you catch yourself still conceiving of God in terms of human-like capacities? In what areas of your life do you unknowingly reduce God to a being that ' receives' from creation, rather than the utterly independent Creator? Commit today to one way you will actively purge this notion from your heart.
?
This lesson shows that even a necessary receptivity would entail the possibility of eternal accidents. But what 'accidents' in your own life—temporal changes—have you mistakenly assumed could condition your relationship with God? Are you relying on a 'receptivity' in God that would make Him dependent on your actions? How will you reorient your worship to reflect absolute divine independence?
?
The argument reveals that God cannot have a disposition (isti'dad) for anything because His attributes are necessary. How does this challenge your understanding of God's foreknowledge or decree? Does the fact that God's knowledge is not a 'receptivity' to future events change how you trust His plan? Write down one specific anxiety you bring to God, and reflect on how it might stem from a misunderstanding of His nature—then surrender it.

The Second Argument: Attributes as Perfection
Second argument with objection and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God's attributes are perfections implies they cannot be originated, and how the objection regarding occasional perfections is refuted.

Core Idea
Even perfections that seem occasional are real perfections; their absence would be a deficiency.

This lesson unpacks the second argument against originated attributes in God. The argument holds that since all of God's attributes are perfections, being devoid of any would be a deficiency, which is impossible. But a subtle objection suggests that some attributes might be perfections only at certain times. The passage explores this objection and its refutation, ultimately reaffirming the impossibility of originated attributes in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: argument, objection, refutation.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the statement that all divine attributes are perfections. Why does that make originated attributes impossible?
Key: lack of a perfection is a deficiency.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims some attributes are perfections only at certain times. How would that affect the argument?
The objector suggests absence at other times is not a deficiency.
4
Follow the refutation
The author uses renewing attributes (e.g., being provider of a specific person) to show that even these are perfections, yet their prior absence is not a deficiency. Then he addresses the sub-objection about negative relations and ends with the invalidity of infinite succession.
This is the key move: the objection only works if infinite succession is allowed, but it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الثاني من تلك الوجوه إلخ قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض الصفات بحيث لا نقصاً يكون كمالاً إلا في بعض الأوقات فحينئذ لا يكون الخلو عنه في بعض آخر منها على أن هذا الوجه منقوض بالمتجدد، فإنه صفة كمال فالخلو عنه نقص فإن قلت الذي يجوز تجدده في ذاته تعالى هو الإضافات العدمية التي ليست صفات كمال قلت إن كان قولك التي ليست صفات كمال إِن جعل صفة مساوية للموصوف ورد أن بعض العدميات قد يكون صفة كمال كعلية العالم مثلاً وإن جعل صفة مقيدة يتجه أن عدم مثله في الحوادث غير ظاهر اللهم إلا أن يقال اتصافه تعالى بالمتجدد ككونه رازقاً لعمر ولما امتنع قبله أعني قبل وجود عمر ولم يكن الخلو عنه نقصاً ومثل هذا الاحتمال في الصفة الحادثة إنما يكون على ما ذكر من تلاحق الصفات لا إلى نهاية وهو باطل عند المتكلمين.
He said: (The second of those aspects, etc.) It was said: Why is it not permissible that some attributes be such that they are not a deficiency but are perfection only at certain times? Then being devoid of them at other times would not be a deficiency? On top of that, this aspect is refuted by the renewing (al-mutajaddid), for it is an attribute of perfection, so being devoid of it is a deficiency. If you say: That which is permissible to be renewed in His essence, the Exalted, is the negative relations (al-idafat al-adamiyya) which are not attributes of perfection. I say: If your statement 'which are not attributes of perfection' is made an attribute equivalent to the described, it is refuted by the fact that some negatives may be an attribute of perfection, like the causality of the world for example. And if it is made a restricted attribute, it is plausible that the nonexistence of its like in accidents is not apparent. Unless it is said: His attribution, the Exalted, with the renewing, such as His being the provider of 'Amr, and He was not that before, i.e., before the existence of 'Amr, and the being devoid of it was not a deficiency. And such a possibility in the case of the originated attribute only occurs on what was mentioned of the succession of attributes without end (ila la nihaya), and that is invalid (batil) according to the theologians (mutakallimun).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the possibility of originated attributes in God, along with an objection and its answer. The argument is that all of God's attributes are perfections; being devoid of a perfection is a deficiency, which is impossible for God. Therefore, no attribute of His can be originated (hadith), because before its existence He would have been devoid of it, which would be a deficiency. The objection raises the idea that some attributes might be perfections only at certain times, so their absence at other times would not be a deficiency. The author refutes this by pointing to 'renewing' attributes (al-mutajaddid), like being the provider of a particular person at a specific time—these are perfections, yet their absence before that time does not imply deficiency. The objection then tries to limit such renewing attributes to negative relations (idafat adamiyya) that are not perfections. The author counters that some negative relations can be perfections (e.g., being the cause of the world). The final response is that the possibility of succession of originated attributes without end is invalid (batil) according to the mutakallimun, thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الصفات الكمال
الخلو
النقص
المتجدد
الإضافات العدمية
تلاحق الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that some perfections are only occasional, so their absence is not a deficiency.
Correction: The argument shows that even occasional perfections (like providing) are real perfections, and their prior absence would be a deficiency if they could be present. The only way to avoid this is to posit infinite succession, which is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's response to the objection that some attributes might be perfections only at certain times?
A) He concedes the objection is valid.
B) He points to renewing attributes and then argues that the objection leads to an infinite succession which is invalid.
C) He says all attributes are eternal.
D) He says negative relations are not perfections.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against originated attributes relies on the fact that all divine attributes are perfections; the objection of occasional perfection is refuted by noting that even renewing attributes are perfections, and the only escape (infinite succession) is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every perfection must belong to God, would you still feel the need to attribute any deficiency to Him in your moments of doubt? Where do you find yourself struggling to affirm His absolute perfection?
?
You have just learned that even occasional perfections cannot be absent from God without deficiency. Does your own life reflect a consistent pursuit of perfection in your worship, or do you settle for occasional acts of devotion, assuming God accepts them despite your neglect?
?
The objection in this passage tries to allow some imperfection in God by restricting perfections to certain times. Are there areas in your belief or practice where you restrict God’s perfection or mercy to certain conditions, rather than affirming His absolute and timeless perfection?

Eternal Receptivity and the Impossibility of Temporal Events in God
Core argument from eternity of receptivity to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternity of receptivity leads to a contradiction if temporal events were to subsist in God.

Core Idea
Eternal receptivity would require temporal events to be eternal, which is impossible.

The author argues that if God could receive temporal events, that receptivity would have to be eternal like His essence. But this leads to an absurd conclusion: the temporal event would have to exist from eternity, which is contradictory. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Receptivity is a necessary concomitant of the essence, so it must be eternal like the essence.
2
Follow the consequence
Eternal receptivity implies that the essence could be qualified by a temporal event from eternity.
3
See the contradiction
Since receptivity is a relation, its eternal validity would make the temporal event eternal—a logical impossibility.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت القابلية من لوازم الذات امتنع انفكاكها عنها فتدوم القابلية بدوامها، والذات أزلية فكذا القابلية وهي أي أزلية القابلية تقتضي جواز اتصاف الذات به، أي بالحادث أزلاً إذ لا معنى للقابلية إلا جواز الاتصاف به أي بالمقبول، وأما بطلان اللازم فلأن القابلية نسبة تقتضي قابلاً ومقبولاً وصحتها أزلاً تستلزم صحة الطرفين أزلاً فيلزم صحة وجود الحادث أزلاً هذا خلف. حاصرين كانت عارضة لكن بقي أن يقال عدم لزوم مثل هذا التسلسل على تقدير وجوب الاتصاف محل بحث، إذ القابلية بمعنى الإمكان الخاص وإن لم يتحقق حينئذ، لكن بمعنى الإمكان العام المقيد بجانب الوجود محقق قطعا وإلا لم يتحقق الاتصاف واستحالته ممنوعة وأما قوله المحصورة بين فقد يعترض عليه بأن الذات وإن كان معروضاً للسلسلة لكنه ليس بطرف لها، وتحقيق ذلك أن الانتهاء طولاً لا ينافي التسلسل عرضاً. فالقابليات التي هي شروط لا علل لو تسلسلت لم يلزم إلا عدم الانتهاء عرضاً، والذات إنما وقع في جانب الطول فليس طرفاً لغير المتناهي في جهة عدم تناهيه فليتدبر اللهم إلا أن يقال القابلية اعتبارية نفس أمرية فيستحيل التسلسل فيها ببرهان التطبيق على أن مجموع القابليات الغير المتناهية بحيث لا يشذ عنها شيء قائم بالذات، فلها قابلية أخرى بالنسبة إلى هذا المجموع فيلزم أن تكون تلك القابلية الأخرى داخلة في المجموع وخارجة عنه وهو باطل وقد سبق البحث في مثله مرار.
And if receptivity (al-qābiliyya) is among the necessary concomitants of the essence, its separation from it is impossible, so the receptivity endures with its duration. The essence is eternal, so likewise the receptivity is eternal. And it, i.e., the eternity of receptivity, requires the permissibility of the essence being qualified by it, i.e., by the temporal event (al-ḥādith) eternally, since there is no meaning for receptivity except the permissibility of being qualified by it, i.e., by the received object (al-maqbūl). As for the falsity of the necessary consequence, it is because receptivity is a relation requiring a receptor and a received object, and its validity eternally entails the validity of both parties eternally, so the validity of the existence of the temporal event eternally is entailed—this is a contradiction. They were occasional, but it remains to be said that the non-necessity of such an infinite regress on the assumption of the obligation of qualification is a subject of research. For receptivity in the sense of specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) is not realized then, but in the sense of general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) restricted to the side of existence is realized definitely; otherwise qualification would not be realized, and its impossibility is denied. As for his statement 'the confined between', it may be objected that the essence, although it is a substrate for the series, is not an endpoint for it. The verification of that is that termination in length (ṭūlan) does not conflict with infinite regress in breadth (ʿarḍan). For the receptivities, which are conditions (shurūṭ) not causes (ʿilal), if they were to regress infinitely, nothing would be entailed except the lack of termination in breadth. The essence only occurs on the side of length, so it is not an endpoint for the infinite in the direction of its infinity—so let it be considered. Unless it is said that receptivity is a consideration (iʿtibāriyya) and a thing of the very nature (amrīyya), then infinite regress in it is impossible by the proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq), because the totality of infinite receptivities, such that nothing escapes it, subsists in the essence. So it has another receptivity with respect to this totality, and then that other receptivity must be included in the totality and excluded from it—this is false. And the discussion of its like has preceded repeatedly.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) against the possibility of temporal events subsisting in God. The author begins by stating that if receptivity is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the divine essence, it cannot be separated from it, so it must be eternal like the essence. The eternity of receptivity implies that it is permissible for the essence to be qualified by the temporal event from eternity, because the very meaning of receptivity is the permission of qualification. The author then demonstrates the impossibility of this necessary consequence: since receptivity is a relation (nisba), its eternal validity requires the eternal validity of both its terms (the receptor and the received), leading to the conclusion that the existence of the temporal event would be valid from eternity. This is absurd (khulf) because a temporal event cannot be eternal. The passage then addresses an objection: one might say that the receptivity in question is not an eternal attribute but an occasional one (ʿāriḍa). The author responds by distinguishing between specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) and general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) and argues that the possibility of qualification is always present. A further objection about infinite regress (tasalsul) of receptivities is rebutted: the receptivities are conditions (shurūṭ) not causes (ʿilal), so an infinite regress in breadth (ʿarḍan) is not impossible; only an infinite regress in length (ṭūlan) would be problematic, but the essence is only in the length dimension, not a party to the breadth regress. The author concludes with a strong argument: the infinite set of receptivities would itself require a further receptivity, leading to a contradiction of inclusion and exclusion, which demonstrates the impossibility by the proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq).

Key Terms
القابلية (al-qābiliyya)
الحادث (al-ḥādith)
أزلية (al-azaliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the eternity of receptivity?
A) Receptivity is not eternal.
B) The temporal event would have to be eternal, which is impossible.
C) The essence is not eternal.
D) Receptivity is not a necessary concomitant.

Key Takeaway
Eternal receptivity would make a temporal event eternal, which is logically impossible, so God's essence cannot be subject to temporal events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you ever imagine that your actions could 'affect' God or cause change in His essence? How does this argument challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your understanding of God's perfection?
?
If God's essence is not receptive to temporal events, how can you be sure that your prayers are heard and that God responds to them? How does the Ash'ari explanation (that God's knowledge and will are eternal and encompass your prayer without change) give you confidence?
?
This proof shows that temporal events cannot subsist in God. Does this make God seem distant or close to you? How does the reality of God's transcendence and immutability actually deepen your awe and trust in Him, rather than weaken your connection?

Defending Against Objections: Occasional Receptivity and Infinite Regress
Rebuttals to objections about occasional receptivity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes objections that receptivity is occasional and that infinite regress of receptivities is possible.

Core Idea
Receptivity is not occasional, and infinite regress of conditions is impossible.

After presenting the main argument, the author anticipates two objections. First, that receptivity might be an occasional attribute rather than eternal. Second, that an infinite regress of receptivities could avoid the contradiction. The author responds by distinguishing between types of possibility and analyzing the nature of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Someone might claim receptivity is not eternal but occurs only when the temporal event is present.
2
See the response
The author distinguishes between specific possibility (الخاص) and general possibility (العام), arguing that general possibility is always present.
3
Examine the regress objection
Another objection involves an infinite chain of receptivities, but the author shows it is impossible because conditions are not causes.
4
Grasp the final argument
Using the proof of correspondence (برهان التطبيق), the author shows that an infinite set of receptivities would require a further receptivity, leading to paradox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The passage then addresses an objection: one might say that the receptivity in question is not an eternal attribute but an occasional one (ʿāriḍa). The author responds by distinguishing between specific possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) and general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) and argues that the possibility of qualification is always present. A further objection about infinite regress (tasalsul) of receptivities is rebutted: the receptivities are conditions (shurūṭ) not causes (ʿilal), so an infinite regress in breadth (ʿarḍan) is not impossible; only an infinite regress in length (ṭūlan) would be problematic, but the essence is only in the length dimension, not a party to the breadth regress. The author concludes with a strong argument: the infinite set of receptivities would itself require a further receptivity, leading to a contradiction of inclusion and exclusion, which demonstrates the impossibility by the proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq).

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ)
الإمكان العام (al-imkān al-ʿāmm)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
شروط (shurūṭ)
علل (ʿilal)
برهان التطبيق (burhān al-taṭbīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think infinite regress of conditions is harmless and can be dismissed easily.
Correction: Even if infinite regress in breadth is not inherently impossible, the author shows that the specific nature of receptivity as a consideration leads to contradiction via Burhan al-Tatbiq.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the infinite regress of receptivities?
A) Occasionalism.
B) The proof of correspondence (burhān al-taṭbīq).
C) Distinction between essence and attributes.
D) The principle of non-contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Receptivity cannot be occasional, and infinite regress of conditions is impossible because the totality of such conditions would lead to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah’s attributes, do you realize that His knowledge and power are eternally ready, not waiting for new conditions? How does this truth challenge any hidden doubt you might have about Allah’s complete awareness of your private thoughts and future actions? Will you commit to speaking to Him in du‘ā’ with the full confidence that He is already perfectly receptive to your call?
?
The proof of infinite regress shows that an endless chain of conditions is impossible. In your own spiritual life, do you ever fall into a pattern of thinking that you need more and more conditions before you can feel close to Allah—like more knowledge, better circumstances, or fewer sins? How can you break that regress today by turning to Allah directly, trusting that His mercy is unconditionally ready for you?
?
This lesson distinguishes between specific possibility (readiness for a particular event) and general possibility (mere absence of impossibility). Which concept better describes your belief in Allah’s power over your life? Do you ever limit Allah to only being able to respond in a specific way, forgetting that His general possibility is always unrestricted? Ask yourself: Are you truly comfortable with the idea that Allah can do anything, even if it defies your expectations?

Second Proof: Attributes of Perfection
Sanusi presents the second proof that no divine attribute can be temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attributes cannot be temporal based on the argument that they are perfections whose absence would be a deficiency.

Core Idea
If God's attributes are perfections, their absence implies impossibility of deficiency.

This lesson presents the second proof against temporal attributes in God. The argument is straightforward and relies on two premises: God's attributes are perfections, and deficiency is impossible for Him. From these, it follows that no attribute can be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: attributes are perfections → absence is deficiency → deficiency impossible → attributes not temporal.
The argument is compact; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the premises
Spot the two key premises: (1) God's attributes are perfections, (2) deficiency is impossible for God by consensus.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the conclusion follows: if an attribute were temporal, God would have lacked it before, implying deficiency—which is impossible.
This is a deductive syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من تلك الوجوه صفاته تعالى صفات كمال فخلوه عنها نقص، والنقص عليه محال إجماعاً فلا يكون شيء من صفاته حادثاً وإلا كان خالياً عنه قبل حدوثه. قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض الصفات بحيث لا نقصاً يكون كمالاً إلا في بعض الأوقات فحينئذ لا يكون الخلو عنه في بعض آخر منها على أن هذا الوجه منقوض بالمتجدد، فإنه صفة كمال فالخلو عنه نقص فإن قلت الذي يجوز تجدده في ذاته تعالى هو الإضافات العدمية التي ليست صفات كمال قلت إن كان قولك التي ليست صفات كمال إن جعل صفة مساوية للموصوف ورد أن بعض العدميات قد يكون صفة كمال كعلية العالم مثلاً وإن جعل صفة مقيدة يتجه أن عدم مثله في الحوادث غير ظاهر اللهم إلا أن يقال اتصافه تعالى بالمتجدد ككونه رازقاً لعمر ولما امتنع قبله أعني قبل وجود عمر ولم يكن الخلو عنه نقصاً ومثل هذا الاحتمال في الصفة الحادثة إنما يكون على ما ذكر من تلاحق الصفات لا إلى نهاية وهو باطل عند المتكلمين.
The second of those aspects: His attributes, exalted be He, are attributes of perfection; so their absence from Him is a deficiency, and deficiency upon Him is impossible by consensus. Therefore, none of His attributes can be temporal, for otherwise He would have been devoid of it before its occurrence. It was said: Why is it not permissible that some attributes are such that they are not a deficiency and become perfection only at some times? Then the absence of it at other times would not be a deficiency. Moreover, this aspect is invalidated by the renewed (al-mutajaddid), for it is an attribute of perfection, so its absence is a deficiency. If you say that what may be renewed in His essence, exalted, are the privative relations (al-iḍāfāt al-ʿadamiyya) which are not attributes of perfection, I say: If you make 'which are not attributes of perfection' a description equal to the described, then it is rejected because some privatives may be attributes of perfection, like the causality of the world, for example. And if you make it a restrictive description, then it is not apparent that the absence of the like of it in temporal events is a deficiency. However, it could be said that His being described, exalted, by the renewed, such as His being the provider for ʿUmar, was impossible before, i.e., before the existence of ʿUmar, and His being devoid of it was not a deficiency. And such a possibility in the temporal attribute only occurs on the basis of a succession of attributes ad infinitum, which is false according to the theologians.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against temporal events in God. The argument is straightforward: God's attributes are perfections (ṣifāt kamāl); their absence would be a deficiency (naqṣ), and deficiency is impossible for God by consensus (ijmāʿ). Therefore, no attribute can be temporal, because if it were temporal, God would have lacked it before its occurrence, implying deficiency. A potential objection is raised: perhaps some attributes become perfections only at certain times, so their absence at other times is not a deficiency. The author responds by noting that this argument is invalidated by the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddid), which is an attribute of perfection—its absence would be a deficiency. The objector might try to limit the renewed to privative relations (iḍāfāt ʿadamiyya) that are not perfections. The author attacks this: if the description 'not attributes of perfection' is coextensive with the described (i.e., all privative relations are not perfections), then it is false because some privatives like the causality of the world (ʿilliyyat al-ʿālam) are perfections. If it is a restrictive description (i.e., only some privatives are not perfections), then it is not clear that the absence of such a privative in temporal events is a deficiency. Finally, a counter-example is given: God's being the provider for ʿUmar was impossible before ʿUmar existed, yet His lack of that attribute was not a deficiency. The author notes that this only works if there is an infinite succession of attributes (talāḥuq al-ṣifāt lā ilā nihāya), which is false according to the theologians (al-mutakallimūn).

Key Terms
صفات كمال
النقص
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument means God cannot have any attributes at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny attributes; it only denies that attributes can be temporal, because temporality would imply prior deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would be the problem if an attribute of God were temporal?
A) It would mean God is changeable.
B) He would have lacked it before its occurrence, which is a deficiency.
C) It would conflict with His unity.
D) It would require an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Because God's attributes are perfections, their absence would be a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for God; therefore, no attribute can be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's perfection means He never changes and never needs anything from you? If so, why do you sometimes act as if your obedience adds to His glory or your sin diminishes it? What would change in your worship if you lived with the absolute certainty that God is completely independent and eternally perfect?
?
When you face a difficult moment, do you ever doubt God's power or wisdom, questioning whether He is enough for you? This lesson declares that deficiency is impossible for Him. What does that demand from you right now, in your current struggle, about your trust in Him?

Objection and Response: Timing of Perfection
Sanusi addresses an objection and provides a multi-layered refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that some attributes may be perfections only at specific times and the author's refutation using renewed attributes and the impossibility of infinite succession.

Core Idea
The objection fails because even renewed attributes are perfections; avoiding deficiency requires infinite succession, which is false.

After presenting the main proof, a subtle objection is considered: what if some attributes become perfections only at certain times? The author responds by examining the concept of 'renewed' attributes (al-mutajaddid) and showing that the objection would require an infinite succession of attributes, which the theologians reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the full passage from 'It was said' to the end. Notice the back-and-forth structure: objection, counter, further objection, final refutation.
This is a dialectical argument.
2
Understand the objection
The objector suggests that some attributes might not be deficiencies at all times, becoming perfections only later. In that case, temporary absence wouldn't be deficiency.
3
Follow the author's first rebuttal
The author says this is invalidated by the 'renewed' (al-mutajaddid), which is clearly a perfection; its absence would be a deficiency.
4
Analyze the privative relations counter
The objector tries to limit renewed to privative relations (iḍāfāt ʿadamiyya). The author refutes this: if all privatives are not perfections, that's false (e.g., causality of the world is a privative perfection); if only some, the absence is not clearly non-deficiency.
5
See the final refutation
The example of God being provider for ʿUmar shows that if the absence at one time is not deficiency, it must be because later the attribute arises—but that leads to infinite succession of attributes, which is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
