Answering the First Proof: Distinguishing Possibility and Eternity
The author refutes the first proof by distinguishing the eternity of possibility from the possibility of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that eternal receptivity entails the possibility of an eternal temporal event by distinguishing the eternity of possibility from the possibility of eternity.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not mean possibility of being eternal.

In this passage, the author directly responds to the first proof that attempted to show that God's eternal receptivity would allow for temporal events to be eternal. The key move is a careful distinction between the eternity of the possibility itself and the possibility of the thing being eternal. This distinction is crucial for preserving the contingency of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation at your own pace. Notice the technical terms and the flow of argument.
Pay attention to the repeated use of 'eternity' and 'possibility'.
2
Identify the core distinction
Find where the author separates 'eternity of validity' from 'validity of eternity'. Understand that the latter is impossible, not the former.
This is the main refutation.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Notice how the author turns the opponent's logic against them by applying the same reasoning to God's agency, leading to an absurd conclusion.
The reductio shows that if the opponent's claim were true, it would imply the world is eternal.
4
Examine the objection and response
The opponent objects that receptivity is an essential attribute while agency is not. The author counters by specifying that the discussion is about the eternal receptivity of action and effect.
This blocks the opponent's escape.
5
Summarize the lesson
Conclude that the first proof is refuted because the eternity of possibility does not entail the possibility of eternity.
You should now see why the distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن الجواب عن الوجه الأول بأن اللازم مما ذكرتموه من لزوم القابلية للذات هو أزلية الصحة أي أزلية صحة وجود الحادث، وهذا اللازم ليس بمحال فإن صحة وجود الحادث أزلية بلا شبهة والمحال هو صحة الأزلية أي صحة أزلية وجود الحادث وهذا ليس بلازم لأن أزلية الإمكان تغاير إمكان الأزلية ولا تستلزمه كما في الحوادث اليومية على ما مر تحقيقة. فأين أحدهما من الآخر، وأيضاً ما ذكرتموه منقوض إذ لو لزم وصح لزم مثله في وجود العالم وإيجاده، فإنه تعالى موصوف في الأزل بصحة إيجاد العالم فيصح في الأزل وجوده فيصح أن يكون العالم أزليا وهو محال فلو لزم من القابلية الأزلية إمكان أزلية الحادث للزم من الفاعلية الأزلية إمكان أزلية العالم لا يقال القابلية صفة ذاتية لازمة للذات فيلزم إمكان أزلية المقبول دون الفاعلية فإنها صفة غير لازمة فلا يلزم إمكان أزلية المفعول، لأنا نقول الكلام في قابلية الفعل والتأثير فإنها أزلية كما أشرنا إليه فيلزم إمكان أزلية المفعول لا في الفاعلية الحاصلة بالفعل. قوله : ( كما أشرنا إليه أي بقول موصوف في الأزل بصحة إيجاد العالم.
And it is possible to answer the first aspect: that the necessary consequence of what you mentioned, from the necessity of receptivity for the essence, is the eternity of validity (azaliyyat al-ṣiḥḥa), i.e., the eternity of the validity of the existence of the temporal event. This necessary consequence is not impossible, for the validity of the existence of the temporal event is eternal without doubt. The impossible is the validity of eternity (ṣiḥḥat al-azaliyya), i.e., the validity of the eternal existence of the temporal event. And this is not entailed, because the eternity of possibility (azaliyyat al-imkān) is different from the possibility of eternity (imkān al-azaliyya) and does not entail it, as in the case of daily events, as has been verified before. So where is one from the other? Moreover, what you mentioned is refuted: if it were entailed and valid, the same would be entailed in the existence of the world and its creation. For He, exalted, is described in eternity with the validity of creating the world, so the validity of its existence in eternity is valid, so it would be valid that the world be eternal, which is impossible. So if from the eternal receptivity, the possibility of the eternity of the temporal event is entailed, then from the eternal agency (al-fāʿiliyya al-azaliyya), the possibility of the eternity of the world would be entailed. It might be said that receptivity is an essential attribute (ṣifa dhātiyya) necessary for the essence, so the possibility of the eternity of the received is entailed, unlike agency, for it is not a necessary attribute, so the possibility of the eternity of the effect is not entailed. We say: the discussion is about the receptivity of action and effect (qābiliyyat al-fiʿl wa al-taʾthīr), for it is eternal, as we have indicated, so the possibility of the eternity of the effect is entailed, not about the agency that is actual (al-fāʿiliyya al-ḥāṣila bi al-fiʿl). His saying: (as we indicated) i.e., by saying 'described in eternity with the validity of creating the world'.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first proof (al-wajh al-awwal). The author argues that the necessary consequence of the first proof is actually the eternity of the validity (azaliyyat al-ṣiḥḥa) of the existence of the temporal event, not the validity of its eternity (ṣiḥḥat al-azaliyya). These are distinct: the eternity of possibility (azaliyyat al-imkān) is different from the possibility of eternity (imkān al-azaliyya). The former does not entail the latter. An analogy is given: daily events have eternal possibility before they occur, but that does not make them eternal. Then, the author uses a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent: if the eternity of receptivity entailed the possibility of the eternity of the temporal event, then by parallel reasoning, God's eternal agency (al-fāʿiliyya al-azaliyya) would entail the possibility of the eternity of the world, which is false. The opponent might try to distinguish receptivity as an essential attribute (ṣifa dhātiyya) from agency as a non-essential one. The author counters that the discussion concerns the receptivity of action and effect (qābiliyyat al-fiʿl wa al-taʾthīr), which is eternal, so the entailment holds. The gloss clarifies that 'as we indicated' refers to the statement that God is described in eternity with the validity of creating the world.

Key Terms
أزلية الصحة
صحة الأزلية
أزلية الإمكان
إمكان الأزلية
الفاعلية الأزلية
قابلية الفعل والتأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies possibility can be eternal.
Correction: The author affirms the eternity of possibility (azaliyyat al-imkān), but denies that this entails the possibility of eternity (imkān al-azaliyya), i.e., that the thing itself could be eternal.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that eternal receptivity entails the possibility of an eternal temporal event?
A) By asserting that receptivity is not eternal.
B) By distinguishing the eternity of possibility from the possibility of eternity.
C) By denying that temporal events have any possibility.
D) By claiming that God's attributes are all eternal anyway.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the first proof by showing that eternal receptivity entails the eternity of possibility, not the possibility of eternity, which does not make the temporal event eternal.

?

?
The author distinguishes between 'eternity of possibility' and 'possibility of eternity'. In your own life, do you confuse the permanence of God's will with a sense that your own possibilities are locked in place? How might this distinction free you to trust in God's power to bring about new things?
?
This passage uses precise logical reasoning to defend the possibility of genuine new events in the world. Are you willing to engage with such detailed arguments to deepen your understanding of God, even when they are difficult? What would it cost you to ignore them?

Answer to the Second Proof: Infinite Succession of Perfections
Answer to the second proof via infinite succession analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the second proof uses the concept of an infinite succession of perfections to avoid the necessity of an eternal accumulation of attributes.

Core Idea
An infinite succession of perfections preserves the common perfection without requiring all perfections to coexist.

This lesson examines the author's response to the second proof against temporal attributes. The proof argued that a being with temporal attributes would lack perfection at some point. The author counters by proposing an infinite series of perfections, each following the previous, much like celestial motions. This model shows that at any moment, Allah possesses a perfection, and the absence of individual perfections does not detract from the common perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key elements of the response
Notice the three components: the positing of infinite successive perfections, the condition that each depends on the previous, and the analogy to celestial motions.
These form the core of the argument.
3
Understand the concept of the common perfection
The author explains that the 'common perfection' (al-kamāl al-mushtarak) is preserved as an eternal type through the infinite succession of individuals. Consider how this addresses the proof's concern about deficiency.
This distinguishes between individual perfections and the type.
4
Reflect on the implication
Realize that this is a hypothetical model to refute the proof, not a claim about God's actual state. The proof is defeated by showing a possible scenario where temporal attributes do not imply deficiency.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن الجواب عن الوجه الثاني بأن يقال لم لا يجوز أن يكون ثمة صفات كمال متلاحقة غير متناهية لا يمكن بقاؤها واجتماعها وكل لاحق منها مشروط بالسابق على قياس الحركات الفلكية عند الحكماء فلا ينتقل حينئذ عن الكمال الممكن له إلا إلى كمال آخر يعاقبه ولا يلزم الخلو عن الكمال المشترك تلك الأمور المتلاحقة، وأما الخلو عن كل واحد منها فأما لامتناع بقائه ولا على أن نوع قوله: (ولا يلزم الخلو عن الكمال المشترك إلخ) يعني الكمال قديم محفوظ وجوده بتعاقب الأفراد الغير المتناهية، وحدوث كل فرد لا يستلزم حدوث ذلك النوع وإن لم يوجد إلا في ضمن فرد كما زعمه شارح المقاصد.
And it is possible to answer the second aspect by saying: why is it not permissible that there exist attributes of perfection that are successive (mutalāḥiqa), infinite, whose persistence and coexistence are impossible, each subsequent being conditioned upon the previous, analogous to the celestial motions according to the philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ). Thus, one does not move from the perfection possible for Him except to another perfection that follows it, and the absence of the common perfection (al-kamāl al-mushtarak) of those successive matters is not entailed. As for the absence of each individual one, that is not a problem. His saying: (And the absence of the common perfection is not entailed) means: the perfection is preserved as a type (kullī qadīm) by the succession of infinite individuals; the occurrence of each individual does not entail the occurrence of that type, even if it does not exist except within an individual, as the commentator of al-Maqāṣid claimed.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the second proof (al-wajh al-thānī). The author suggests that we can conceive of an infinite series of perfections that are successive (mutalāḥiqa), not all present at once. Each perfection is conditioned upon the previous one, analogous to the celestial motions according to the philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ). In this way, at any given moment, God possesses a perfection, and the absence of any particular perfection is not a deficiency because another perfection follows. The common perfection (al-kamāl al-mushtarak)—the type—is preserved through the infinite succession. The author then comments on the text: the absence of the common perfection is not entailed because the type is eternal (qadīm) and its existence is maintained by the succession of infinite individuals (taʿāqub al-afrād al-ghayr al-mutanāhiya). The occurrence of each individual does not entail the occurrence of the type, even if the type only exists within individuals, as the commentator of al-Maqāṣid claimed. This response shows that temporal attributes do not necessarily imply deficiency if they are part of an infinite sequence.

غير متناهية (ghayr mutanāhiya: infinite)
مشروط بالسابق (mashrūṭ bi al-sābiq: conditioned upon the previous)
الحركات الفلكية (al-ḥarakāt al-falakiyya: celestial motions)

Correction: The answer only proposes a possible model to refute the proof; it does not claim that God actually has such attributes.

D) Human thought processes.

Key Takeaway
The second proof is refuted by showing that an infinite succession of perfections can preserve the common perfection without requiring all perfections to be present simultaneously.

Al-Ṭūsī's Argument Against Infinite Accidents on an Eternal Substance
Al-Ṭūsī argues that infinite temporal accidents on an eternal substance are logically contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ṭūsī's argument that an infinite series of temporal events on an eternal substance leads to a logical contradiction based on the definitions of 'pre-eternal' and 'temporal event'.

Core Idea
An eternal substance cannot host an infinite series of temporal events without logical contradiction.

Al-Ṭūsī presents a razor-sharp argument that challenges the coherence of infinite temporal succession on an eternal substrate. By analyzing the meanings of qadīm and ḥādith, he shows that such a scenario is internally contradictory. Let's unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the definitions of qadīm and ḥādith.
Notice that the argument hinges on the meaning of priority (sabq).
2
Understand the definitions and implications
Al-Ṭūsī defines 'pre-eternal' as what is not preceded by non-existence, and 'temporal event' as what is preceded by it. From this, he deduces that the pre-eternal must be prior to every temporal event.
This priority is not temporal but ontological.
3
Grasp the contradiction
If there is an infinite series of temporal events, the pre-eternal would need to be prior to an infinite number of events. This would require a single state in which its priority over all events is actualized simultaneously, which is impossible, making the statement contradictory.
The impossibility arises because priority over an infinite set cannot be realized as a single state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث ذكره الأستاذ المحقق الطوسي في الزخر وادعى فيه المتانة جداً، وهو أن القول بتوارد حوادث غير متناهية على قديم كلام متناقض لأن القديم يجب أن يكون سابقاً على كل حادث إذ المراد بالقديم ما لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم، وبالحادث ما يكون مسبوقاً به فلا بد أن يكون سابقاً على كل واحد مما يصدق عليه الحادث وهذا يوجب أن يكون له حالة يتحقق فيها سبقه على كل واحد مما يصدق عليه الحادث بضرورة.
And here there is a research (baḥth) mentioned by the master investigator al-Ṭūsī in al-Zukhur, claiming it is highly sound: namely, that the statement about an infinite succession of temporal events (ḥawādith) on a pre-eternal (qadīm) is a contradictory statement (kalām mutanāqiḍ). Because the pre-eternal must be prior to every temporal event, since the meaning of 'pre-eternal' is what is not preceded by non-existence, and 'temporal event' is what is preceded by it. So it must be prior to every individual to which the term 'temporal event' applies. This necessitates that there be a state (ḥāla) in which its priority over every individual that is described as a temporal event is actualized, necessarily.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents a research (baḥth) by Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī, which he considered highly sound (mātin). He argues that the claim of an infinite series of temporal accidents occurring on an eternal substance (qadīm) is contradictory. The definition of 'pre-eternal' (qadīm) is that it is not preceded by non-existence, and 'temporal event' (ḥādith) is that it is preceded by non-existence. Therefore, the pre-eternal must be prior (sābiq) to every temporal event. But if there is an infinite series of temporal events, the pre-eternal would need to be prior to an infinite number of events. This would require a single state (ḥāla) in which its priority over all those events is actualized at once. However, priority over an infinite set cannot be realized as a single state because one cannot complete an infinite regress. Thus, the notion is self-contradictory. This argument is a powerful refutation of the possibility of an infinite series of accidents on an eternal substance, a key point in kalām discussions of divine attributes.

Key Terms
توارد حوادث غير متناهية (tawārud ḥawādith ghayr mutanāhiya: succession of infinite events)
قديم (qadīm: pre-eternal)
حادث (ḥādith: temporal event)
سبق (sabq: priority)
حالة (ḥāla: state)
كلام متناقض (kalām mutanāqiḍ: contradictory statement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God can have attributes or that temporal events can occur in relation to God at all.
Correction: The argument only rejects an infinite series of temporal accidents on an eternal substance; it does not deny that God can have attributes or that temporal events can exist in a finite sense.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does al-Ṭūsī draw from the definition of pre-eternal and temporal event?
A) An infinite series of events can occur on an eternal substance.
B) The statement of infinite accidents on the eternal is contradictory.
C) The eternal substance changes over time.
D) Temporal events cannot exist at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ṭūsī's argument shows that positing an infinite sequence of temporal accidents on an eternal substance leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unintentionally, thought of Allah's eternity as a never-ending timeline similar to ours, but simply longer? This argument exposes the contradiction in that mental picture. To protect your tawḥīd, are you willing to train your mind to accept that Allah's eternity is qualitatively different from any temporal sequence, even an infinite one? What will you do when you catch yourself slipping into that erroneous imagination?
?
Consider the humility demanded by this argument: your intellect can grasp the logical necessity of a single state (ḥāla) in which the eternal's priority over all events is actualized, yet you cannot conceive how that is possible for an infinite series. Does this remind you of the limits of human reason in matters of the divine? How does this affect your reliance on rational proofs in your faith?
?
If someone were to argue that Allah's actions (e.g., creating the world) are an infinite series of temporal events occurring on His eternal essence, this argument shows that is impossible. Have you ever fallen into the assumption that Allah 'continuously' creates in a time-bound sense? What correction does this lesson demand from you in your daily understanding of Allah's attribute of creation (takwīn)?

Answering the Objection of Successive Perfections
Sanusi answers the second objection by positing successively acquired perfections without deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the possibility of infinite successive perfections in God without deficiency, and why this view conflicts with the proof of al-tatbiq.

Core Idea
Infinite successive perfections do not imply deficiency; they constitute true perfection.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a critical objection: if God was devoid of a perfection at one time, that would be a deficiency. His answer introduces the idea of an infinite chain of successively acquired perfections, each dependent on the prior, similar to celestial motions. This allows for a continuous acquisition without any moment of absolute lack. However, he also acknowledges that this model is problematic for the mutakallimun due to the proof of al-tatbiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully once to get the rhythm. Then read the English translation.
Notice the logical connectors like فلم لا يجوز and فإنه.
2
Identify the core argument
Sanusi proposes that perfections can be successive and infinite, each conditioned by the previous. Find the analogy and the reasoning about lack of perfection.
3
Examine the two cases of lack
The passage discusses two possibilities for the lack of each perfection: either due to impossibility of permanence or due to needing to vacate for the next. Understand each.
4
Consider the closure
Sanusi notes that this conception contradicts the proof of al-tatbiq. Think about why that might be, preparing for later discussion.
This is a teaser for future lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن الجواب عن الوجه الثاني بأن يقال لم لا يجوز أن يكون ثمة صفات كمال متلاحقة غير متناهية لا يمكن بقاؤها واجتماعها وكل لاحق منها مشروط بالسابق على قياس الحركات الفلكية عند الحكماء فلا ينتقل حينئذ عن الكمال الممكن له إلا إلى كمال آخر يعاقبه ولا يلزم الخلو عن الكمال المشترك تلك الأمور المتلاحقة ، وأما الخلو عن كل واحد منها فأما لامتناع بقائه ولا كمال نسلم امتناع الخلو عن مثله مما يمتنع بقاؤه إنما الممتنع هو الخلو عن يمكن بقاؤه ، وأما لأنه لو لم يخل عنه لم يمكن حصول غيره فيلزم حينئذ فقد وجد أنه كمالات غير متناهية فكان فقده أي فقد كل واحد منها لتحصيل كمالات غير متناهية هو الكمال بالحقيقة، لا مع فقدان تلك الكمالات إلا أن هذا التصوير ينافيه برهان التطبيق على رأي المتكلم كما سيشير إليه المصنف .
And it is possible to answer the second face by saying: why is it not permissible that there exist successively acquired perfections, infinite, whose permanence and simultaneous existence are impossible, each subsequent one being conditioned by the previous, analogously to celestial motions according to the philosophers. Then he does not move from the perfection possible for him except to another perfection that succeeds it, and the lack of the common perfection of those successive matters does not follow. As for the lack of each one of them, then either because of the impossibility of its permanence, and we do not concede the impossibility of the lack of its like among things whose permanence is impossible; the only impossible is the lack of that which can persist. And as for because if he were not devoid of it, the other could not occur, then it would follow that there exist infinite perfections, so their loss—i.e., the loss of each one of them—for the attainment of infinite perfections is true perfection, not with the loss of those perfections. Except that this conception is contradicted by the proof of al-tatbiq according to the mutakallim, as the author will indicate.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides an answer to the second objection, which likely claimed that if accidents occur in God, He would be subject to deficiency. The answer posits that there could be an infinite series of successively acquired perfections, each conditioned by the prior, like celestial motions. This avoids the need for any single perfection to persist, and the lack of any individual perfection is not a deficiency because the series as a whole constitutes true perfection. However, the author notes that this conception conflicts with the proof of al-tatbiq (the argument from correlation) as used by the mutakallimun, which will be discussed later.

غير متناهية
برهان التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Successive perfections imply that God is deficient at some point.
Correction: The passage argues that the lack of a particular perfection in a series is not a deficiency because the series as a whole is true perfection, and the lack is either due to the impermanence of that perfection or necessary for acquiring the next.

Checkpoint

A) The growth of plants
B) Celestial motions
C) Human learning
D) The succession of prophets

Key Takeaway
Sanusi argues that an infinite series of successively acquired perfections does not entail deficiency; each perfection's lack is either inevitable or instrumental for continuous perfection.

Critique of a Four-Premise Proof
Critique of a four-premise proof about accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a classical kalam proof based on four premises about accidents is critiqued, particularly the problematic third premise and the conditional validity of the fourth.

Core Idea
A proof is only as strong as its premises; one weak link breaks the chain.

This passage examines a proof that attempts to argue that God's essence cannot be the locus of contingent attributes. The proof rests on four premises, but the commentator shows that the third premise lacks evidence and can be challenged, while the fourth premise only leads to the desired conclusion under certain conditions. This critique highlights the importance of scrutinizing each premise in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the commentator first lists the premises, then critiques them.
2
Identify the four premises
Locate the four premises in the Arabic text. They are introduced with 'الأولى', 'الثانية', 'الثالثة', 'الرابعة'.
The premises are: (1) every contingent attribute has a contrary, (2) the contrary of a contingent is contingent, (3) a substance is never free of a thing and its contrary, (4) whatever is never free of a contingent is contingent.
3
Understand the objection to the third premise
The commentator says the third premise is problematic because no evidence supports it. The gloss gives two possible interpretations of 'contrary' and challenges each.
The opponent can deny that every contingent has a contrary if 'contrary' means a positive opposite, or deny the consequence if it is more general.
4
Analyze the conditional status of the fourth premise
The commentator notes that the fourth premise is not complete: if the accidents are infinite, the substratum must be eternal (contradicting the desired conclusion); if finite, temporality follows. So the proof is not decisive.
This shows that the proof only works under a specific condition, which is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاستدلال يبتني على أربع مقدمات. الأولى أن لكل صفة حادثة ضدا الثانية ضد الحادث حادث الثالثة الذات لا تخلو عن الشيء وضده الرابعة ما لا يخلو عن الحادث فهو حادث والثالث الأول من هذه المقدمات مشكلة إذ لا دليل على صحتها فلا يصح الاستدلال بها والرابعة إذا تمت تم الدليل الثاني). قوله: (إذ لا دليل على صحتها إذ القائل أن يقول أن أردتم بالضد معنى وجوديا يستحيل اجتماعه مع الصفة الحادثة في محل واحد فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة حادثة ضد أو إن أردتم به ما هو أعم من ذلك فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث كيف ولو صح ذلك لزم أن يكون عدم العالم حادثا ولو كان عدمه حادثا لكان وجوده سابقا عليه وهو محال وأيضا لا نسلم أن ما قبل حادثا لا يخلو عنه وعن ضده إذ الجسم يقبل الألوان ويخلو عن الجميع كما في اشفاء . قوله : ( والرابعة إذا تمت تم الدليل الثاني لكنها ليست بتامة لأن الحوادث المذكورة لو كانت غير متناهية يجب قدم ما لا يخلو عنها لئلا يلزم بقاء الصفة بلا موصوف نعم إذا كانت متناهية يلزم حدوث محلها الغير الحالي عن واحد منها .
This proof is based on four premises: (1) every contingent attribute has a contrary, (2) the contrary of a contingent is contingent, (3) the substance is never free of a thing and its contrary, (4) whatever is never free of a contingent is contingent. The third of these premises is problematic because there is no evidence for its soundness, so the proof cannot be valid. If the fourth is established, the second proof is complete. [Gloss:] He says: (There is no evidence for its soundness) because one may say: if by 'contrary' you mean a positive existence that cannot coexist with the contingent attribute in one subject, then we do not concede that every contingent attribute has a contrary; or if you mean something more general, then we do not concede that the contrary of a contingent is contingent. Moreover, if that were correct, it would entail that the non-existence of the world is contingent, and if its non-existence were contingent, its existence would be prior to it, which is impossible. Also, we do not concede that what accepts a contingent is never free of it and its contrary, since a body can accept colors and be free of all of them, as in transparency. He says: (And the fourth, if established, the second proof is complete), but it is not complete because if the mentioned accidents are infinite, then that which is never free of them must be eternal, to avoid the persistence of an attribute without a subject; but if they are finite, then the occurrence of its substratum, which is not free of one of them, entails its temporality.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a proof that attempts to show that God's essence cannot be the locus of contingent attributes. The proof relies on four premises. The commentator first lists the premises: (1) every contingent attribute has a contrary, (2) the contrary of a contingent is itself contingent, (3) a substance is never free of a thing and its contrary, and (4) whatever is never free of a contingent is itself contingent. The commentator then points out that the third premise is problematic because there is no evidence for its soundness, so the proof fails. In the gloss, he elaborates on the objection to the third premise, arguing that the opponent can challenge both the notion of 'contrary' and the claim that a substance cannot be free of both a thing and its contrary. As for the fourth premise, the commentator notes that it is not complete: if the accidents are infinite, the substance that never lacks them would be eternal, which contradicts the desired conclusion; but if they are finite, then the substance's temporality can be established. Thus the proof is not sound.

Key Terms
حادث
ضد
مقدمات
خلو
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proof with many premises is automatically strong.
Correction: Even one unsupported premise can invalidate the entire argument, as shown by the critique of the third premise.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the third premise of the proof?
A) Because it contradicts the fourth premise.
B) Because there is no evidence for its soundness and it can be challenged.
C) Because it is not about contingent attributes.
D) Because it implies infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The success of a theological proof hinges on the soundness of every premise; a single weak premise invalidates the entire argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have accepted that Allah is free from accidents as an unquestioned truth. But this lesson shows that even logical proofs can have hidden assumptions. Examine your own heart: Are you certain about Allah's transcendence because of sound evidence, or because you never dared to question? What would it take for you to verify your conviction?
?
The third premise was rejected because 'there is no evidence for its soundness'. In your own beliefs, are there premises you hold about Allah without evidence? Will you seek out the evidence, or continue on blind trust?
?
The commentator points out that if a proof relies on ambiguous terms like 'contrary', the argument collapses. When you discuss matters of faith with others, do you ensure that you and they mean the same words? Or do you assume agreement where there is only misunderstanding? How can you strive for clarity in your own speech about Allah?

First Objection: God's Attributes and Contingent Objects
The opponent argues that God's attributes entail contingent objects, and the commentator distinguishes the attributes from their relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari tradition defends the eternity of God's attributes against the objection that they require contingent objects.

Core Idea
The attribute is eternal; only its relation to creation changes.

This passage presents a key objection against the eternity of God's attributes and the Ash'ari response. The opponent argues that attributes like speech and hearing imply contingent objects, making the attributes contingent. The commentator distinguishes the eternal attribute from its temporal relation, preserving divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponent presents his first argument. Note the agreed-upon attributes (speaking, hearing, seeing) and the reasoning that they require contingent objects.
The opponent uses a reductio: if the objects are contingent, the attributes must be contingent too.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
The opponent assumes that if an attribute depends on a contingent object, then the attribute itself must be contingent. Ask yourself: is this always true?
The commentator will challenge this assumption.
3
Read the response
Now read the commentator's reply. He distinguishes between the attribute itself and its relation (ta'alluq) to objects. Which one does he say is contingent?
The key phrase: 'What is contingent is their relation' (الحادث تعلقه).
4
Understand the distinction
The commentator explains that divine speech is an eternal mental meaning (ma'na nafsi) that does not depend on an addressee. Only its relation depends on the addressee. This is true for all attributes.
The relation (idafa) can change without changing the attribute itself.
5
Apply to other attributes
The same logic applies to hearing, sight, will, and dislike. Reflect: How does this defense preserve God's immutability?
This argument is central to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بوجوه ثلاثة الأول : الاتفاق على أنه متكلم سميع بصير ولا تتصور هذه الأمور إلا بوجود المخاطب والمسموع والمبصر وهي حادثة فوجب حدوث هذه الصفات القائمة بذاته تعالى . قلنا : الحادث تعلقه ) أي تعلق ما ذكر من الصفات ( وإنه ) أي ذلك التعلق (إضافة) من الإضافات فيجوز تجددها وتغيرها، إذ الكلام عندنا معنى نفسي قديم قائم بذاته لا يتوقف على وجود المخاطب بل يتوقف عليه ،تعلقه وكذا السمع والبصر والإرادة والكراهة .
The opponent argues with three aspects: First, there is agreement that He is speaking, hearing, seeing, and these matters are not conceivable except with the existence of the addressee, the heard, and the seen, which are contingent. Therefore, it is necessary that these attributes subsisting in His essence be contingent. We say: What is contingent is their relation (ta'alluq) – i.e., the relation of the mentioned attributes – and it (i.e., that relation) is an addition (idafa) among additions, so it is permissible for it to be renewed and changed. For according to us, speech is an ancient (qadim) mental meaning (ma'na nafsi) subsisting in His essence, not dependent on the existence of the addressee; rather, its relation depends on it. Similarly for hearing, sight, will, and dislike.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first argument and the commentator's response. The opponent argues that since God is described as speaking, hearing, and seeing, and these acts or attributes require the existence of an interlocutor, a heard object, and a seen object—which are all contingent—the attributes themselves must be contingent. The commentator responds by distinguishing between the attributes themselves and their relations (ta'alluq). He asserts that what is contingent is not the attribute but its relation to objects. For example, divine speech, according to the Ash'ari school, is an eternal mental meaning (ma'na nafsi) subsisting in God's essence. It does not depend on the existence of an addressee; only its relation (talluq) does. Similarly for hearing, sight, will, and dislike. Therefore, the contingency of the relation does not imply the contingency of the attribute itself.

Key Terms

سميع
بصير

إضافة
كلام نفسي
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute's object changes, the attribute itself must change.
Correction: The attribute itself remains unchanged; only its relation to objects is temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is contingent when God speaks to a contingent being?
A) The attribute of speech itself.
B) The mental meaning of speech.
C) The relation (ta'alluq) of speech to the addressee.
D) The existence of the addressee.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of God's attributes is preserved by distinguishing the eternal attribute from its temporal relation to created objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you truly believe that God's attribute of hearing is eternal and unchanged, and that your prayer does not 'activate' His hearing but rather is the object of His eternal hearing? How does this change the way you approach Him?
?
If God's speech is an eternal mental meaning independent of creation, then the Quran as His speech is also eternal. Does your reverence for the Quran reflect the belief that it is not a created book but the very word of the Eternal? What would change if you held this conviction more deeply?
?
You are a contingent being whose existence depends entirely on God. But God's attributes do not depend on you. Are you living as if God needs your worship, or as if your worship is simply the occasion for His eternal attribute of seeing and hearing to relate to you? Ponder: which attitude brings true humility?

Second Objection: The Rectifier for Accidents Subsisting in God
The opponent argues that being an attribute suffices for subsisting in God, and the commentator begins a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the rectifier for something subsisting in God must be simply 'being an attribute', and why he rejects pre-eternity as a condition.

Core Idea
The opponent argues that pre-eternity cannot be part of the rectifier because it is a negation.

This lesson presents a key objection in the debate about whether contingent attributes can subsist in God. The opponent argues that the rectifier (musahhih) for something subsisting in God must be simply 'being an attribute', not 'being an attribute combined with pre-eternity'. He claims pre-eternity is a negation and thus cannot serve as a cause of validity. Read the passage carefully to follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the opponent presents two possible rectifiers and eliminates one.
The response 'We say: the rectifier...' is incomplete; we focus on the objection.
2
Identify the two options
The opponent states that the rectifier is either (a) simply being an attribute, or (b) being an attribute with the description of pre-eternity and not being preceded by non-existence.
3
Understand why option (b) is rejected
The opponent argues that pre-eternity is a negation (salb) and therefore cannot be part of the cause of validity. This leads him to conclude that option (a) is correct.
The rejection is based on the nature of pre-eternity as a negative attribute.
4
Reflect on the implication
If option (a) is accepted, then contingent attributes can subsist in God because being an attribute is sufficient as a rectifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : المصحح للقيام به إما كون صفة فيعم هذا المصحح الحادث ( أو ) كونه صفة ( مع وصف القدم وكونه غير مسبوق بالعدم وإنه سلب لا يصلح جزاً للمؤثر ) في الصحة فتعين الأول فيصح قيام الصفة الحادثة به، (قلنا المصحح)
Second: The rectifier (musahhih) for subsisting in Him is either being an attribute, so this rectifier includes the contingent, or being an attribute with the description of pre-eternity (qidam) and being not preceded by non-existence, but it is a negation (salb) not fit to be part of the cause of validity. So the first is determined, and thus the contingent attribute subsisting in Him is valid. (We say: the rectifier... [incomplete])
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's second argument and the beginning of the commentator's response. The opponent argues that the rectifier (musahhih) for something subsisting in God's essence is either (a) simply being an attribute, in which case the rectifier would apply to any attribute, including contingent ones, or (b) being an attribute combined with pre-eternity (qidam) and not being preceded by non-existence. However, the opponent claims that pre-eternity is a negation (salb) and thus cannot be part of the cause of the validity of subsisting. Therefore, the first option must be correct, and consequently contingent attributes can subsist in God. The commentator begins his response with 'We say: the rectifier...' but the text cuts off, so the full response is not present in this window. Nevertheless, the argument itself is clearly presented.

Key Terms

صفة
قدم
سلب
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pre-eternity can serve as a condition for the rectifier because it is a positive attribute of God.
Correction: The opponent argues that pre-eternity is a negation (salb) – it means 'not being preceded by non-existence' – and thus cannot be part of the cause of validity. In kalam, negative attributes are not causally effective.

Checkpoint
Why does the opponent reject the second option for the rectifier?
A) Because it would make contingent attributes impossible.
B) Because pre-eternity is a negation and cannot be part of the cause.
C) Because the rectifier must be a positive entity.
D) Because the first option is more general.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the rectifier for an attribute subsisting in God cannot involve pre-eternity because pre-eternity is a negation, so being an attribute alone suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the opponent uses pure logic to decide what can be attributed to God. When you think about God's attributes, do you trust your own reasoning to determine what is possible for Him, or do you rely solely on revelation? Are you comfortable with human logic defining what God can or cannot be?
?
The opponent argues that pre-eternity cannot be part of the rectifier because it is a negation. Reflect on your own understanding of God's eternity: do you treat it as a mere absence of beginning, or as a positive, active attribute that shapes your daily awareness of Him? How does this affect your worship?
?
This lesson presents a logical argument that all attributes can subsist in God simply by being attributes. Yet we know God is unlike anything. Are you tempted to rely on such human arguments to understand God, or do you recognize the limits of reason and submit to the mystery of His essence? What would change in your faith if you truly accepted that God is beyond what the mind can capture?

Refuting the Consensus Argument: Attribute vs. Relation
Presents the opponent's first argument from consensus and refutes it by distinguishing the attribute from its relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the consensus argument preserves the eternity of divine attributes by distinguishing the attribute from its relation.

Core Idea
The relation of an attribute changes; the attribute itself remains eternal.

In this brief but significant passage, al-Qushanji presents the objection that since God's speech, hearing, and sight require temporal objects, the attributes themselves must be temporal. The Ash'ari response, grounded in the concept of divine speech as an eternal internal meaning, draws a crucial distinction between the eternal attribute and its temporal relation. This move protects the transcendence of God while accounting for His engagement with creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Try to follow the flow of the argument before analysing it.
Notice the structure: objection (الأول) then response (قلنا).
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues from consensus that God is speaking, hearing, seeing, and these require temporal objects. Therefore the attributes must be temporal. Find this part in the Arabic.
The key phrase: 'وهي حادثة فوجب حدوث هذه الصفات'.
3
Find the key distinction in the response
Look for the phrase 'الحادث تعلقه' – the temporal thing is the relation (تعلق). The response distinguishes between the attribute itself and its relation to temporal objects.
The relation is an 'إضافة' (accident/relation) that can change while the attribute remains eternal.
4
Relate to the doctrine of internal speech
The passage mentions that speech is an eternal internal meaning (الكلام النفسي). This anchors the eternity of the attribute. The relation to an addressee is temporal, but the speech itself is not.
This applies also to hearing, sight, will, and aversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الاتفاق على أنه متكلم سميع بصير ولا تتصور هذه الأمور إلا بوجود المخاطب والمسموع والمبصر وهي (حادثة فوجب حدوث هذه الصفات القائمة بذاته تعالى . قلنا : الحادث تعلقه ) أي تعلق ما ذكر من الصفات ( وإنه ) أي ذلك التعلق (إضافة) من الإضافات فيجوز تجددها وتغيرها، إذ الكلام عندنا معنى نفسي قديم قائم بذاته لا يتوقف على وجود المخاطب بل يتوقف عليه ،تعلقه وكذا السمع والبصر والإرادة والكراهة .
First: The consensus that He is speaking, hearing, seeing, and these [attributes] are not conceivable except with the existence of the addressee, the heard, and the seen, which are temporal, so it is necessary that these attributes subsisting in His essence be temporal. We said: what is temporal is the relation, i.e. the relation of the mentioned attributes, and that relation is an accident among accidents, so it is permissible for it to be renewed and to change. For according to us, speech is an internal meaning, eternal, subsisting in His essence, not dependent on the existence of an addressee, rather dependent on it is its relation. Similarly for hearing, sight, will, and aversion.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first argument and its refutation. The opponent argues from consensus that God is described as speaking, hearing, seeing, and these descriptions require an object (addressee, heard, seen) which are temporal. Therefore, the attributes of speech, hearing, sight must be temporal. The refutation distinguishes the essential attribute from its relation (تعلق). The attribute itself is eternal, but its relation to temporal objects is temporal. Speech, for example, is an internal meaning (معنى نفسي) that is eternal in God's essence; it does not depend on the existence of an addressee, but the relation of that speech to an addressee depends on the addressee's existence. So the temporality lies in the relation, not in the attribute. This preserves the eternity of the essential attributes while accounting for the temporal instances of their relations.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الإضافة
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God speaks to a temporal creation, then His attribute of speech becomes temporal.
Correction: The attribute of speech itself is eternal; only the relation of that speech to a temporal addressee is temporal. Speech in God's essence does not change.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what is temporal when God addresses a temporal creation?
A) The attribute of speech itself
B) The relation (تعلق) of speech
C) Both the attribute and its relation
D) Neither the attribute nor its relation

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are eternal; only their relations to temporal objects are temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God hears your every prayer. But if His attribute of hearing is eternal and unchanging, what does it mean that He 'hears' your specific, temporal words? Does this distinction between the eternal attribute and its temporal relation bring you closer to God or make Him feel more remote? How should this shape the urgency and sincerity of your du'a today?
?
The opponent's argument feels logical: if God speaks, He must have someone to speak to. How often do you limit God by assuming He must operate within the cause-and-effect framework of His creation? In what area of your life are you subtly expecting God to be bound by the very laws He established? Will you repent of that mental limitation right now?
?
God's attributes are eternal and unchanging, yet your relationship with Him is dynamic and ever-changing. How do you reconcile the stillness of His nature with the motion of your faith? What does this teach you about the unmoving Anchor you have in the midst of life's turbulence? Are you building your life on that eternal stability or on shifting sands?

The Second Objection: Ground for Subsistence of Attributes
Presents and refutes the argument about the ground for subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari position on divine attributes and the refutation's key argument that not every temporal attribute has a necessary opposite.

Core Idea
The ground for subsistence cannot be simply being a quality, or else any temporal attribute could be predicated of God.

In this passage, the opponent presents a dilemma: either the ground for an attribute's subsistence in God is simply being a quality, or it includes additional conditions. Since the additional conditions are negations, the ground must be simply being a quality, which would allow temporal attributes. The refutation challenges this by questioning the necessity of an opposite for every temporal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the opponent frames the dilemma as a forced choice between two options.
2
Identify the opponent's dilemma
The opponent argues that the ground for subsistence is either just being a quality (which would include temporal attributes) or being a quality plus additional eternal properties. Since the additional properties are negations, they cannot be part of the cause, so the first option is chosen, making temporal attributes valid.
3
Understand the refutation's counterexample
The refutation first responds by questioning the claim that every temporal attribute has an opposite that is also temporal. They provide the example of a body and colors: a body can be free from both color and its opposite simultaneously.
This shows that the absence of an opposite does not prove temporality.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation undermines the opponent's assumption. If not every temporal attribute requires an opposite, then the opponent's dilemma is not exhaustive, and the ground for subsistence cannot be simply being a quality.
This connects to the broader debate about whether temporal attributes can subsist in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : المصحح للقيام به أما كونه صفة فيعم هذا المصحح الحادث ( أو ) كونه صفة ( مع وصف القدم وكونه غير مسبوق بالعدم وإنه سلب لا يصلح جزاً للمؤثر ) في الصحة فتعين الأول فيصح قيام الصفة الحادثة به، (قلنا المصحح) قوله : (إذ لا دليل على صحتها إذ القائل أن يقول أن أردتم بالضد معنى وجودياً يستحيل اجتماعه مع الصفة الحادثة في محل واحد فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة حادثة ضد أو إن أردتم به ما هو أعم من ذلك فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث كيف ولو صح ذلك لزم أن يكون عدم العالم حادثا ولو كان عدمه حادثا لكان وجوده سابقاً عليه وهو محال وأيضاً لا نسلم أن ما قبل حادثاً لا يخلو عنه وعن ضده إذ الجسم يقبل الألوان ويخلو عن الجميع كما في اشفاء . قوله : ( والرابعة إذا تمت تم الدليل الثاني لكنها ليست بتامة لأن الحوادث المذكورة لو كانت غير متناهية يجب قدم ما لا يخلو عنها لئلا يلزم بقاء الصفة بلا موصوف نعم إذا كانت متناهية يلزم حدوث محلها الغير الحالي عن واحد منها . قوله : ( احتج) الخصم بوجوه ثلاثة الأول إلخ) قد سبق أن الخصم هو الكرامية والمجوس والدليل لا يصلح للكرامية والمجوس والدليل لا يصلح للكرامية على ما نقل المصنف منهم من أنهم لا يجوزون إلا حدوث إرادته تعالى أو قوله تعالى : كُن ﴾ [الأنعام: ٧٣]، فإما أن يجعل لهم دليلا الزاميا أو تحمل الأدلة على التوزيع فتأمل. قوله : ( وإنه إضافة) فإن قلت أي احتياج إلى بيان إضافة التعلق مع أن محله نفس الصفة لا ذات الباري تعالی ،سبحانه فعلى تقدير وجوده لا يلزم كونه تعالى محل الحوادث قلت الدليل الأول يدل على أن القديم مطلقاً لا يكون محلاً للحادث فوجه الاحتياج على هذا ظاهر. قوله : ( الثاني المصحح للقيام به تعالى إما كونه صفة إلخ) سياق كلامهم بدل على أنهم يجعلون المصحح للقيام نفس كونه صفة فيرد عليهم أنه يقتضي صحة اتصافه تعالى بكل صفة حادثة، وقد سبق أنهم لا يجوزون قيام الصفة الحادثة التي لا يحتاج إليها في الإيجاد . قوله : ( وإنه سلب لا يصلح جزاً للمؤثر فيه بحث أما أولاً فلأنا لا نسلم أن ما ذكره حقيقة القدم، بل هو عارض لها ولهذا عده البعض من الصفات الحقيقية، وأما ثانياً فلأن صحة القيام أمر عدمي، فيجوز أن تكون علتها عدمية كما سيأتي مثله في بحث الرؤية وأما القول على تسليم وجودية المعلول بكون القدم شرطاً للتأثير لا جزاً من المؤثر، أو كون الحدوث مانعاً، فلم يلتفت للقيام به هو حقيقة الصفة القديمة وهي مخالفة لحقيقة الصفة الحادثة بذاتها)، ) فلا يلزم اشتراك الصحة
Second: The ground (musahhih) for subsistence in Him: either it is being a quality, and this ground would include the temporal, or being a quality with the description of eternity and not being preceded by non-existence, and it is a negation that does not serve as a part of the cause for effect on correctness, so the first is determined, thus the subsistence of the temporal attribute in Him is valid. We said: the ground. His statement: 'for there is no proof of its validity, for the objector may say, if you mean by opposite an existent meaning impossible to coexist with the temporal attribute in one substrate, then we do not concede that every temporal attribute has an opposite, or if you mean by it more general than that, then we do not concede that the opposite of a temporal is temporal. How so? If that were correct, then the non-existence of the world would be temporal, and if its non-existence were temporal, its existence would be prior to it, which is impossible. Also, we do not concede that what precedes a temporal is not free from it and its opposite, for the body accepts colors and is free from all of them, as in al-Shifa.' His statement: 'The fourth: if it is complete, the second proof is complete, but it is not complete, because if the mentioned temporal events are infinite, then what is not free from them must be eternal, lest the attribute remain without a characterization. Yes, if they are finite, then the occurrence of their substrate, which is not devoid of one of them, is entailed.' His statement: 'The opponent argued with three faces, the first etc.' It has preceded that the opponent are the Karramites and the Magians, and the proof does not apply to the Karramites and the Magians, and the proof does not apply to the Karramites according to what the author transmitted from them that they only allow the occurrence of His will or His saying 'Be' (Quran 6:73), so either a binding proof is made for them or the proofs are taken as distributed, so contemplate. His statement: 'And it is an accident (idafa).' For if you say, what need is there for explaining the accidentality of the relation while its locus is the attribute itself, not the essence of the Exalted God? So on the assumption of its existence, it does not entail that God is a locus of temporal events. I say: the first proof indicates that the eternal absolutely is not a locus for the temporal, so the need for this explanation is apparent. His statement: 'The second: the ground for subsistence in the Exalted is either it being a quality etc.' The flow of their speech indicates that they make the ground for subsistence itself being a quality, so it is objected to them that it would imply the validity of the Exalted being described by every temporal attribute, and it has preceded that they do not allow the subsistence of a temporal attribute that is not needed for creation. His statement: 'And it is a negation that does not serve as a part of the cause for effect.' There is discussion: first, because we do not concede that what he mentioned is the reality of eternity; rather it is an accident of it, and for this some counted it among the real attributes. Second, because the validity of subsistence is a non-existential matter, so it is permissible for its cause to be non-existential, as will come similarly in the discussion of vision. As for the statement that, assuming the existence of the effect, eternity is a condition for effect, not a part of the cause, or that temporality is a preventer, he did not pay attention. For subsistence in Him is the reality of the eternal attribute, and it is different from the reality of the temporal attribute in its essence, so no commonality of validity is entailed.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and a detailed refutation. The opponent argues that the ground (musahhih) for an attribute subsisting in God is either simply being a quality, which would include any quality including temporal ones, or being a quality with the additional description of eternity (being unoriginated). Since the latter description is a negation (salb) and cannot be a part of the cause, the first option must hold, thus temporal attributes can subsist in God. The response is given in the commentary: first, the opponent's assumption that there is no proof for the validity of temporal attributes subsisting in God is challenged. The objector's argument about necessary contrast (didd) is refuted by stating that not every temporal attribute has a necessary opposite, and the example of colors in a body shows that a substrate can be free from a quality and its opposite. Second, the 'fourth' (perhaps a lemma) is discussed: if temporal events are infinite, the substrate that does not lack them must be eternal to avoid the attribute remaining without a subject; but if finite, the substrate is temporal. Third, the identity of the opponent (Karamites and Magians) is noted, and the proof is shown not to apply to them. Fourth, the need to explain that the relation is an accident is justified because without it one might think the attribute itself changes. Fifth, the main refutation: the opponent assumes the ground for subsistence is simply being a quality, which would lead to the absurdity that any temporal attribute could subsist in God, but the opponent himself does not allow certain temporal attributes. Moreover, the reality of the eternal attribute is different from that of a temporal attribute by essence, so no common ground for subsistence exists. The discussion also delves into the nature of eternity, noting that some consider it a real attribute, and that validity of subsistence is a non-existential matter that can have a non-existential cause.

Key Terms
المصحح
الضد
القدم
السلب
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an attribute is a quality, it can subsist in God simply because it is a quality.
Correction: The passage shows that the ground for subsistence is not simply being a quality; otherwise, any temporal attribute could be attributed to God, which is rejected.

Checkpoint
What is the first point of the refutation against the opponent's second objection?
A) The opponent's assumption that the ground for subsistence is simply being a quality
B) The opponent's claim that every temporal attribute has an opposite
C) The opponent's use of scriptural evidence
D) The opponent's reliance on the authority of the Karramites

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the opponent assumes a ground that is too broad, and the refutation shows that not every temporal attribute has a necessary opposite, thus undermining the dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What unexamined assumption about God's attributes do you carry that might be based on comparing Him to created things? How does this passage call you to scrutinize that assumption today?
?
When you reason about God, do you tend to assert conditions that feel necessary without proving them? Will you commit to testing one such condition this week against what revelation and sound reason actually say?
?
The refutation in this lesson centers on the ambiguity of the term ‘opposite’. What term in your own understanding of God do you realize might be ambiguous and needs clarification, even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Third Objection and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation on change in attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection that God's attributes change is refuted by distinguishing between essential attributes and their relational aspects.

Core Idea
God's essence never changes; only relations to creation do.

The third objection claims that God must change when He creates the world because He becomes Creator after not being Creator, and His knowledge changes. This lesson presents the objection and the key refutation: change occurs only in the relations (al-idafat) of the attributes, not in the attributes themselves. This foundational principle safeguards divine immutability while acknowledging the temporal order of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection (lines 1-4) and the refutation starting with 'قلنا'. Note how the opponent describes change in creatorhood and knowledge.
The refutation begins immediately with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the two kinds of attributes
The passage distinguishes between real attributes (like knowledge) and relational attributes (like creatorhood). See how each is explained.
Knowledge is real but its relation changes; creatorhood is relational.
3
Understand 'taghayyur fi al-idafat'
Focus on the phrase 'التغير في الإضافات'. This is the central doctrine: change is in the relations, not in the essence or the attribute itself.
Contemplate why this preserves divine immutability.

تعلق بالمعلوم يتغير ذلك التعلق بحسب تغيره والخالقية من الصفات الإضافية أو من الحقيقية والمتغير تعلقها بالمخلوق لأنفسها ( وقالت الكرامة أكثر العقلاء يوافقوننا فيه، أي في قيام الصفة الحادثة بذاته تعالى ( وإن أنكروه باللسان فإن الجبائية قالوا بإرادة وكراهة حادثتين لا في محل لكن المريدية والكارهية حادثتان في ذاته، و كذا السامعية والمبصرية تحدث بحدوث المسموع والمبصر ، وأبو الحسين يثبت علوماً متجددة. هو أن تعلق العلم والمريدية والكارهية يتجدد أو نقول هؤلاء مر إليه المصنف لا لأنه ينفي القيام بالفعل لا إمكانه الذي هو مدعى الخصم كما توهم إذ المفروض هو الشرطية أو المانعية للتأثير في الصحة لا في نفس القيام بل لأن أجزاء العلة التامة للموجود كلها وجودية على زعم المصنف كما سبق في بحث العلة والمعلول. قوله : (أو من الحقيقة) بناء على القول بالتكوين. قوله : ( لكن يدية والكارهية إلخ فإن قلت هذا ينافي ما ذكره المصنف سابقاً أخذاً من أبكار الأفكار، وإن وافق ما نقله الشارح من نهاية العقول قلت هذا شبهة الخصم، والمذكور فيما سبق هو القول الحق فلا محذور. قوله : ( فيكونان حادثين هذا مدفوع بأن الكلام في الوجود بعد العدم لا في العكس على أن الانتهاء صفة الحكم لا الواجب تعالى فالأولى أن يقول الشارح بعد قول المصنف وهما عدم بعد الوجود وما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه فيلزم حدوث الحكم القائم بذاته تعالى وهذا التقرير هو Third: That He, exalted, became different from the world after not having been, and became knowing that it [the world] exists after having been knowing that it will exist, so the attribute of creatorhood and the attribute of knowledge occurred in Him. We said: change is in the relations. For knowledge is a real attribute that has a relation to the known; that relation changes according to its change. And creatorhood is among the relational attributes or [real], and what changes is its relation to the creature, not itself. And the Karramites said: most rational people agree with us, i.e., in the subsistence of the temporal attribute in the essence of the Exalted, even if they deny it by the tongue. For the Jubba'is said: will and aversion are two temporal [events] not in a locus, but the [attributes of] willing and averseness are two temporal [events] in His essence. Similarly, hearing and sight occur with the occurrence of the heard and seen. Abu Hashim affirms renewed knowledges. The Ash'aris affirm naskh (abrogation), which is either the lifting of the ruling subsisting in His essence or its termination, and both are non-existence after existence, thus they are temporal. And the philosophers affirmed the accidents (idafat), i.e., they said they exist externally, with the occurrence of 'withmess' and 'priority' renewed in His essence, as if they also went to the subsistence of temporal events in Him. And the answer: change is in the relations, and it is permissible, as has preceded in the analysis of the point of dispute. So the intention of the Ash'aris is that the relation of the ruling ends or is lifted, and similarly the intention of Abu Hashim and the Jubba'is is that the relation of knowledge, willing, and averseness is renewed, or we say: these [views] are referred to by the author not because he denies actual subsistence but its possibility, which is the opponent's claim, as was thought, for the supposition is conditionality or preventiveness for effect on validity, not on the subsistence itself, but because the parts of the complete cause for existence are all existential according to the author's claim, as has passed in the discussion of cause and effect. His statement: 'or from the real' based on the saying of takwin (bringing into existence). His statement: 'but willing and averseness etc.' For if you say, this contradicts what the author mentioned earlier, taken from Abkar al-Afkar, even if it agrees with what the commentator transmitted from Nihayat al-'Uqul, I say: this is a doubt of the opponent, and what was mentioned earlier is the correct statement, so no problem. His statement: 'so they are two temporal things' is rebutted because the discussion is about existence after non-existence, not the reverse, and because termination is an attribute of the ruling, not of the Necessary Existent. So it is better that the commentator says after the author's statement: they are non-existence after existence, and what is established as eternal is impossible to cease, so the temporality of the ruling subsisting in His essence is entailed, and this exposition is more suitable for the answer. His statement: 'the two renewed in His essence' concerning the renewal of priority there is discussion, unless its mention is taken as predominant, and the answer: the discussion is based on the existence of priority, and what is established as eternal is impossible to cease, and since its non-existence is established by the realization of 'withmess', it is realized that it is not eternal, as has passed in the discussions of relative categories, so contemplate. His statement: 'and similarly the intention of Abu Hashim and the Jubba'is etc.' The statement about the renewal of omniscience in the sense of renewal...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its refutation, along with a discussion of other sects' views. The opponent argues that God becomes different (i.e., creator after not being creator) and His knowledge changes from knowledge that the world will exist to knowledge that it does exist, so there is change in God. The refutation is that change occurs only in relations (idafat), not in the essential attributes. Knowledge is a real attribute, but its relation to objects changes as the objects change. Creatorhood is either a relational attribute or real, but what changes is its relation to creatures. The Karramites claim that most rational people agree with them on attributing temporal attributes to God, pointing to the views of the Jubba'is (who posit temporal will and aversion but not in a locus, though the attributes of willing and averseness are in His essence), Abu Hashim (who holds renewed knowledges), the Ash'aris (who assert abrogation which involves lifting or termination of the ruling, which are temporal), and the philosophers (who affirm accidents like 'withmess' and 'priority' renewed in God). The answer is that all these sects are actually talking about changes in relations, not in the essence or essential attributes. The commentary then clarifies that the author's reference to these views is not to deny actual subsistence but to deny its possibility, and it discusses issues related to eternity and priority. It also rebuts the idea that abrogation implies a temporal change in God, explaining that it is the relation of the ruling that changes, not the ruling itself. Finally, it notes that the discussion of priority is based on its existence, and if it is not eternal, then it is not impossible to change.

الإضافات
التغير في الإضافات
الصفة الحقيقية
الصفة الإضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's attributes themselves change or are temporally acquired when He creates.
Correction: The attributes are eternal; only their relations to creation change.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what changes in God when He creates the world?
A) The attribute of knowledge itself
B) The attribute of creatorhood itself
C) The relations (إضافات) of these attributes
D) Both the attributes and their relations

Key Takeaway
The third objection is refuted by showing that change occurs only in the relational aspects of God's attributes, not in the eternal attributes themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes never change, then His mercy, justice, and truthfulness are eternally the same. When you fail, do you believe God's attitude toward you has changed? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what would it take for you to live in the security of His unchanging attributes?
?
The refutation shows that change is only in relations, not in God Himself. Consider your prayers: do you think they 'change' God's mind or make Him more pleased with you? How can you worship God knowing that His perfect attributes are unaffected by your actions, yet He still commands you to seek Him?
?
God's knowledge of your future sins is eternal and unchanging. Does this make you feel trapped or liberated? How does the truth that God's attribute of knowledge is unchanging affect your trust in His forgiveness and His plan for your life?

Reconciling Sectarian Views: All Change Is Relational
Discussion of sectarian views and their reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how various Islamic sects (Jubba'is, Abu Hashim, Ash'aris, philosophers) inadvertently support the Ash'ari position by referring to relational changes rather than changes in God's essence.

Core Idea
All apparent changes in God reduce to changes in relations.

After presenting the core refutation, the text shows that even opponents who seem to attribute temporal events (hawadith) to God actually speak about relational changes. The Karramites claim most rational people agree with them, citing the Jubba'is on will and aversion, Abu Hashim on renewed knowledges, Ash'aris on abrogation, and philosophers on accidents like 'withmess'. This lesson dissects how all these views are reinterpreted to refer to changes in relations, not in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
List the sects and their claims
Read the passage from 'وقالت الكرامة' onward. Make a mental list: Karramites, Jubba'is, Abu Hashim, Ash'aris, philosophers. Note what each says about God.
Each sect appears to posit a temporal feature in God.
2
Understand the reframing
The answer states that all these are actually changes in relations (idafat). See how each claim is reinterpreted: e.g., Ash'ari abrogation is termination of relation, not change in ruling.
The commentary explains each point in detail.
3
Grasp the broader argument
Appreciate that the Ash'ari position does not deny that there are temporal aspects related to God; it only denies that these affect the eternal essence or attributes.
This reconciles scriptural language about God's actions with philosophical immutability.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That abrogation (naskh) implies a change in God's will or ruling itself, meaning God changes His mind.
Correction: Abrogation is the termination of the relation of the ruling to a particular time, not a change in the eternal ruling or God's will.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, how should the Ash'ari understanding of abrogation (naskh) be interpreted?
A) As a change in God's eternal ruling
B) As the termination of the relation of the ruling
C) As a temporal event in God's essence
D) As a metaphor with no real meaning

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's act of creation or His responses to your prayers, do you unconsciously imagine a God who changes like a created being? How does realizing that only relations change—not His essence—reshape your confidence in His eternal steadiness? Will you pause next time you ask something of Him and remember that He remains the same while His relation to your request changes?
?
The passage reconciles even seemingly problematic positions by attributing change to relations. Is there an area of your theological understanding where you struggle to reconcile apparent contradiction? Does this principle of relational change give you a tool to resolve it? Commit to studying one such difficulty this week, using this lens.
?
You confess that God is unchanging, yet your heart may still be troubled by tragedies says God 'willed' them. Does your belief in changelessness actually comfort you in hardship, or do you secretly fear that God has changed toward you? Challenge yourself: When pain strikes, will you actively remind yourself that God's relation to you is not one of abandonment but of a new, wise relation? Write down a prayer declaring your trust in His unchanging love.

Divine Attributes

Change in Relations, Not Essence: Divine Immutability
Al-Iji defends divine immutability by distinguishing change in relations from change in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot have temporal accidents and how change in relations preserves divine immutability.

Core Idea
Change in God's attributes is only in relations, not in essence.

This lesson tackles a subtle theological objection: if God's attributes change, doesn't that imply change in God? Al-Iji responds by distinguishing between attributes themselves and their relations. He shows that real attributes remain eternal, while only their relations to creation can change. This preserves divine transcendence while explaining how God can have diverse relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English. Notice the structure: first a general principle, then an objection, then the response focused on change in relations.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if God became different (e.g., Creator after not being Creator), then change occurred in His essence. Pinpoint where this is stated.
Look for 'صار مخالفاً للعالم بعد ما لم يكن' and 'فقد حدث فيه صفة الخالقية وصفة العلم'.
3
Understand the response: change in relations
Al-Iji's key phrase is 'التغير في الإضافات'. This means the change is in the relations (iḍāfāt), not in the essence. How does this apply to knowledge and Creator?
Knowledge is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) with a taʿalluq (relation) that changes. Creator is relational or real; only the relation changes.
4
Apply the distinction to real vs. relational attributes
Note that knowledge is a real attribute but has a relation that changes. Creator is described as either relational (iḍāfiyya) or real. In both, only the relation changes. Why does this solve the problem of divine immutability?

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يقوم بذاته للقيام به هو حقيقة الصفة القديمة وهي مخالفة لحقيقة الصفة الحادثة بذاتها)، ) فلا يلزم اشتراك الصحة ( الثالث : أنه تعالى صار مخالفاً للعالم بعد ما لم يكن و) صار ) عالماً بأنه وجد بعد أن كان عالماً بأنه سيوجد ( فقد حدث فيه صفة الخالقية وصفة العلم ( قلنا : التغير في الإضافات)، فإن العلم صفة حقيقة لها تعلق بالمعلوم يتغير ذلك التعلق بحسب تغيره والخالقية من الصفات الإضافية أو من الحقيقية والمتغير تعلقها بالمخلوق لأنفسها
The Second Observatory - Sixth Section: That the Exalted is impossible that subsist in His essence... for that by which it subsists is the reality of the eternal attribute, and it is different from the reality of the temporal attribute in itself, so there is no necessary sharing of soundness. Third: That the Exalted became different from the universe after not having been, and became knowing that it existed after having been knowing that it would exist, so there occurred in Him the attribute of Creator and the attribute of knowledge. We say: Change is in relations. For knowledge is a real attribute that has a relation to the known; that relation changes according to the change of the known. And Creator is among the relational attributes or the real, and what changes is its relation to the creature, not itself.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It addresses the objection that if temporal attributes were to subsist in God's essence, it would imply similarity between eternal and temporal attributes, which is invalid. The author counters by stating that change occurs only in relations, not in the essence of the attributes. The example of knowledge and Creator illustrates that the relation (ta'alluq) changes while the attribute itself remains eternal. The key point is that the attribute's essence is immutable; only its relational aspect can change.

Correction: This passage clarifies that relational changes (ta'alluq) do not affect the essence of the attribute; only the relation to the transient object changes.
